The Red Page
Thou Shalt Not
Only one Moses in the competition for
a new Australian Decalogue hit upon the
flaw that makes the average Australian
annoyed with the ancient Decalogue. We
are not accustomed to be told thus curtly
and uncompromisingly that we shall or shall
not do any particular thing. In fact, to be
told not to do a thing is, for the Australian,
as for the unspoiled kid anywhere, the great-
est temptation to butt right in and do it —
just to see what happens. It usually does
too. The Decalogue suits the Huns: the
spend their whole lives shrinking from larg
“Verboten ” notices. Curious that they ar
the race that need the Moses Decalogue most.
But even “Keep off the Grass” arouses al
that is most Australian in us. Hence th
monotonous recurrence of “Thou shalts” anc
“Thou shalt nots” rather spoils the exeellen
made-in-Australia commandments submitted
The competitor who hit upon the formula
“You might as well” went, by merely tha
phrase, a long way toward the prize.
The number of mute, inglorious Mosese
in Australia is enormous. This is certainly
the most popular competition yet devised by
this page. Of course, Moses was severely
hampered by the necessity of inscribing each
letter upon stone. Naturally he had to
prune all redundant literary graces from his
phrases—a unique example of the influence
of matter over literary style. Anybody who
has seen an Australian tombstone employee
engraving, at union rates and union speed
the name of the dear departed will under
stand Moses’s Inclination to be brief and to
the point. But there is another reason why
those competitors who preferred length to
brevity had to be discouraged. Decalogues
must be brief. Remember, you had to
learn the old Ten Commandments when you
were young; and you may have children of
your own. Be merciful to them, as this page
means to be.
Several new commandments were men-
tioned by a majority of the competitors. On
counting the total votes received these com-
mandments would head the list, in something
like this order:—
1. Thou shalt not borrow.
2. Thou shalt not commit adulteration.
S. Thou shalt not toowse.
k. Thou shalt honor Australia.
5. Thou shalt not import.
Herewith some inspired commandments
that occurred in various otherwise prosy
decalogues:—
Cultivate a taste for self-sacrifice: you’l
need it.
Don’t have a god if you have no use for one
Thou shalt try a new god. Wealth has
had a long innings.
Honor thyself, for it was for their own
sakes only that thy father and thy mother
brought thee into the world.
Thou shalt be certain what adultery is;
then, don’t commit it.
—All these by A.E.P.
Thou shalt call no other country “Home”
but Australia.
—E. L. Lundstrom.
Thou shalt curse the pubs, the P.L.L. and
the Potsdam Pirate; and with a loud voice
shalt thou curse the Censor and the neces-
sity for the hospital at Milson’s Island; but
thou shalt remember that curses alone avail
not.
—Rupert Brown.
Thou shalt do the fair thing.
Thou shalt love all women and be faithful
to one.
—Q.E.D.
Thou shalt work.
Honor thy contracts and thy Courts of
Arbitration, that thy strikes may be unknown
in the land. '
—“Moses Wattleblum.”
Marry a good woman whilst thou art still
young, and bring not into the world more
children than thou canst well support
—Susan O.
Remember the heroes of Anzac and keep
their memory green.
—“D. Tease.”
Six days shalt thou “labor” and do all
manner of shirk, but the seventh thou shalt
keep wholly for pleasure.
Thou shalt not “scab.”
—Both by “Delver.”
Thou shalt not worship any fool image or
hero, but shalt reverence thy reason, respect
thy neighbor, esteem thy horse, and show
kindness to thy cattle pup.
Inwall thy stealings be mindful of the law.
Thou shalt do unto others as they would
do unto thee. And shalt do it first, whether
it be good or whether .it be evil, thereby
creating a hearty rbspeet for that which is
good.
—These three by H. G. James.
Thou shalt love thy town more than thy-
self and thy State more than thy town, and
Australia more than thy State. And thou
shalt also, but with more brain, love the
Empire. And to those who dislike this com-
mandment say: “Australia and I are two,
but the Empire is a crowd.”
—“Ah Gee.”
Never change the last pound note.
—J.R.D.
Thou shalt have no other goods from over-
sea.
Thou shalt not bow down thyself to
Cohen, nor serve him ; for lOU, his god, is
a jealous god, visiting the indebtedness of
the fathers upon the children even unto the
third generation of them that worship him,
and charging interest by the thousand to
them that curse him, yet keep his command-
ments.
Remember the eight-hours’ day and keep
it wholly. Eight hours shalt thou labor and
do all thy work, for the six-hours’ day is a
fond thing vainly imagined, whether by thee,
or thy son, or thy daughter, etc. For in not
less than eight hours mayst thou accomplish
thy duty to thyself and thy fellows; where-
fore has Australia blessed the eight-houra-
day and hallowed it
Thou shalt not covet thy neighbor’s House,
for thou hast seven Houses of thine 0wn....
—This loquacious lot from Guy Innes.
You shall not sacrifice the kid, either by
preventing his arrival, leaving him to grow
anyhow, or borrowing moneys that he will
have to repay.
Cleave to your mate, your husband or
your wife with all your heart. Any love
remaining over shall be prudently and
cleanly used.
—O. N. G.
Thou shalt not use the word “Blanky.”
Give not overmuch cheek to thy parents.
They have piled up mountains of debts for
thee to pay, wherefore go slow, lest they
lump on more.
Thou shalt not allow thyself to be made
a co-re., unless thou canst afford it. Like-
wise, remember that the baby-bonus abol-
isheth not thine obligation to marry the girl.
Thou shalt not covet thy neighbor’s wife.
The most desirable-looking wives are gener-
aly take-downs, yet the red-headed woman
with freckles often makes the best wife.
And, anyhow, there are stacks of women.
—This instalment from F.C.
Thou shalt have a wife.
Thou shalt not let any water run to
waste, for it is a dry country.
Thou shalt not kill for sport.
—Henry Smith.
Thou shalt not commit adultery, nor form
illicit unions; with peace shall come a
revision of the law.
Thou shalt not covet thy neighbor’s coun-
try until to thy neighbor thou canst effectu-
ally say, “Keep off the grass”; thou shalt
not covet thy neighbor’s wife, nor wilt thou,
after thou hast become thoroughly acquainted
with her.
: —L. Woolacott.
I am thy Parliament, which hath made
of thee a nation, which hath brought thee
out of the houses of bondage, even the 12
Houses of the six States. Thou shalt have
no other Parliament before me.
—H. Stewart.
Thou shalt take the name of the Capital-
ist in vain, for the Union shall hold him
guilty who speaketh well of the Boss.
—R. Nought X.
We are blanky Australians, and don’t
forget it.
Thou shalt, in all cases of doubt, danger
or embarrassment, grin and carry on.
Thou shalt not speak disparagingly of the
good, kind censor. He knows what is best
for us.
—Pontius Potter.
Thou shalt not take Australia’s name in
the stinking-fish vein; for Good Australians
w tJ., not hold him Shitless that doeth this.
Thou shalt not bear false witness... .ex-
cept to save a woman from dishonor.
—L. Sparrow.
Don’t skite.
Take full interest and pride in things Aus-
tralian—also in the big things outside Aus-
tralia.
—May Siede.
Thou shalt get work.
Thou shalt not pawn the kid.
Thou shalt honor thy word, and build me
a railway to—not from—my Northern Terri-
tory in the shortest possible time, and by
the shortest possible route.
—“Ben Sun.”
Thou shalt choose to be born in great
numbers.
—“Moses Junior.”
Thou shalt, in the end, die game.
—O.T.G.
Whatever thy religion or irreligion, respect
it.
—Teresa Teece.
Thou shalt read The Bulletin.
Thou shalt not express thy patriotism in
verse, but in cash, lest the people curse
thee.
—Freda Oldman.
Thou shalt not starve.
—“My Oath.”
Quite a number of private or sectional
decalogues were submitted. H. B. Yencken
supplies The Trades Hall Decalogue, of
which the best commandments are:—
The Trades Hall spake these words and said:
Thou shalt have none other Gods but Me.
Thou shalt not take for thyself any kind of non-
union labor, or too much of it, that is in Heaven
above, the earth beneath, nor the wateni under the
earth; for I, thy Union, am a jealous Union, and
weit the Bins of the fathers upon the children of
them that hate me, and take the wages of thousands
of them that loathe me but keep my commandments.
Kemember thou keep sober the Sabbath day. Six
days shalt thou drink all thou art able to, but the
seventh is the Bartenders’ Holiday. In It thou shalt
buy no manner of intoxicants, thou, thy son, thy
daughter, thy manservabt, thy maidservant, thy
cattle, and the stranger that is within thy bar.
And the Union Secretary also spake these
words, submitted by Erie Cox, saying:—
Thou shalt not be a blackleg.
Thou shalt not possess thine own soul, neither shalt
thou make up thine own mind on any matter under
Heaven or earth save only as I direct. For I thy
Boss, am a jealous Joss, and will deal it out to thee
and thine even to starvation shouldst tliou forsake
me, and find fat billets for such of ye as are truly
humble and vote according to my word.
Thou shalt boost Australian manufactures such
only as are not “black.”
Remember the Sabbath Day, and get thy beer on
Saturday night. Forty-eight hours a week shalt thou
labor, and in that time do as little as possible, and
for the rest draw time and a half. But on the Sab-
bath Day draw double time,, and if thy Boss will
not cough up according to the word of Higgins, get
thee to Bondi, thou and thy missus and thy kids
and thy cobber which is next door. Even the Lord
would not labor on the seventh day.
Thou shalt know more than thy father and thy
mother put together that thy days may be long on
the land which thine old pot worked for.
Thou shalt vote against conscription, for thy Union
must come before thy country.
Love ye one another, but do it discreetly.
Thou shalt not steal, but this clause applies only
to capitalists and employers. Politicians and Unions
are specially exempt, and for them the Public purse
is ever open, for the Public is a Monumental Ass.
Thou shalt not pause to utter any slander against
the other side, for be sure they will do likewise
unto thine.
Thou need not covet anything on earth, be thou
wowser or bung or unionist or any other darned
thing, for if thou canst not get thy desire honestly,
grab it by Act of Parliament and call it lawful.
While “Janet” whispereth these councils
to the Flapper, tittering:—
Be happy. The doctrine of Eternal Punishment is
out of date, anyhow.
If you can’t be pretty, be chic.
Make the most of yourself. Disdain not aids to
beauty. Many a proposal has withered in the shine
of a sun-kissed nose, and many men like the taste of
lip-salve.
Respect your elders—youth is evanescent.
Cultivate a charming inanity. There are few men
brainy enough to stand cleverness in a wife.
Mind your own business. The other cat says just
the same things about you.
Be innocent. Men prefer it—in their wives.
Covet not the other girl’s man. A fickle fiancfi
makes a constant co-respondent.
Marry an orphan. Relations-ii>law are the limit.
Be a bromide. Most of us are, and it is far less
trouble.

Qit work—thet iz, if y’r carn’t git ennything else.
Don’t b’ a scab, because there is no ointment t’ be
got f’r shaded legs.
Stoush y’r neighbor if ’e narks y’r, also th’ John
if ’e ’orns inter th’ scrimmage.
Shicker in spite ov blanky referendums, ’n’ fergit

Remember th’ boss giveth ’n’ th’ boss taketh away
—y’r daily toil.
Don’t pinch ennything ’n’ git found out —’tis
cheaper t’ buy or borrow it.
Consider Sundee ’n’ loaf well, because Mundee
means git set again f’r th’ next round.
Save a life when y’r git a charnce. Huns do the
other thing.
Don’t yap erbout Old Hughie too much, but, if y’r
carn’t ’elp it, gor’ blime iz right enuff now ’n’ again.
Look arfter th’ old chap ’n’ muther, f’r they wuz
dinkum t’ y’r before y’r block ’n’ muscle got strong.
While N. Freeberg boosteth Australia,
blankying:—
Boost Orstralyer.
Boost the country like ’ell.
Strike me fat, we can prodooce the goods,
then w’y not well boost some more!
Ain’t our lump o’ dirt the freest and best in the
world? Well, roust yer selves and boost
like a sweatin’ of a football barracker.
D’yer need t’ be told that there ain’t no
country in the world ’s good ’s yer own?
Well, crack the welkin fer Orstralyer.
Keep clear o’ these narks oo run the
place down.
Any bloke in Parlyment oo well keeps shady

ladder, go dead crook on the ’s if ’e’d
stolen yer fav’rit bit o’ skirt.
Ladle it out to any foreign oo thinks ’is late
land is better than ’is new ’ome. Give ’im
’ell, the low cow.
Don’t ferget to boost the place. Boost it fer
all yer worth; spend yer last tray and breave
yer last* breff fer good old —- Orstralyer.
A Buchan catcheth the eye of the
Speaker, saying:—
Thou shalt have no other gods before the Caucus.
Thou shalt not make unto thee any graven image,
etc., excepting Brooklyn Bridge and the Twopenny
Tube.
Thou shalt not take the name of the Lord thy God
in vain without first referring to the Trades Hall.
Remember the Sabbath day to keep it holy, even
though the hotels do close at 6 o’clock on Saturday
night.
Honor thy father and thy mother, and get as much
out of them while living, remembering probate dues
to come.
Thou shalt not kill, nor issue too much paper
money, as it amounts to the same thing.
Thou shalt not commit adultery, until a Bill of
sanction is passed by both Houses.
Thou shalt not steal—just keep on borrowing.
Thou shalt not bear false witness, etc., for con-
scientious reasons even though it may get through a
Royal Commission.
Thou shalt not covet thy neighbor’s house, etc.
Just monopolise them in the name of the State.

after sifting the hundreds submitted, several
considerations ruled. First, length. A
number were thrown out awing to extreme
loquacity. The shortest was submitted by
“Peggy”:—
Thou shalt (1) love , (2) know, (8)
oclieve in, v (If) advance the interests of,
(5) speak the praises of, (6) inscribe the
beauties of, (7) read the books of, (8) work
for, (9) live for, (10) die for Australia.
B. J. H. Owen’s lot could have been In-
scribed on one tijißlet. It, and the other
decalogues here Honorably mentioned, will
appear subsequently on this page. Another
consideration is that of casting the new
commandments in the mold of the old, thus
making the Australian Decalogue more easy
to remember. “Ma’feesh” and “Moses
Junior” come very close to the literary form
invented by Moses, sen.; but their con-
tents are not particularly striking. Excel-
lent decalogues, with some weak command-
ments, are inscribed by “Australian Cadet”
and J.D.C., while R.S. cleverly rimes the
old ten with modern improvements.

necessary quality: they are Australian a !
it was Australian commandments that

The New Australian Decalogue.
1. Worship no god who claims to he
The one and only deity.
2. Since each god swears that he’s the one
You d better worship, say, the Sun
3. Keep up the Sabbath—on the beach-
The mermaids there know how to preach.
If. Incite the countless jaws of Towser
To strafe the cad, the slcite, and wowser
5. Live now; love life and love and.
laughter;
And take your chance for Death-and-
after.
6. Take life as more or less a joke
But be a clean and honest bloke.
7. Treat each girl as a decent tart ';
She’ll do her best to live the part.
8. Try to be peaceful and polite,
But for your own ideals fight.
9. If War be dealt you by the Fates,
Remember Ansae, and your mates.
10. Ten is the golden rule; a sort
Of keystone: Always be a sport.
—Bartlett Adamson.
An Australese Decalogue.
1. Yer might as well stick fer Orstralia.
2. Yer might as well stop borrowin’.
3. Yer might as well keep from usin’ too
many blanky swear-words.
If. Yer might as well send yer kids ter Sun-
day School—it won’t ’urt ’em.
5. Yer might as well ’ave a bit of teamin’.
6. Yer might as well give yer boss a fair
deal —yer might be boss yerself some
day.
7. Yer might as well stick to yer own old
woman ( they’re orl alike).
8. Yer might as well make yer own tools
and duds.
9. Yer might as well pay yer debts.
10. Yer’ll blanky well ’ave ter be a better
stousher than the next bloke.
—Ethel Pawley.

The Ansae Book (Cassell, 3s. 6d. out
here), written and illustrated in Gallipoli
by the men of Anzac, for the benefits of
Anzac funds, is a souvenir of The Great
Adventure that will be treasured by every
patriotic Australasian. The book was pro-
duced in the lines at Anzac toward the end
of 1915. Practically all the stuff in it was
composed in the trench or in the dug-ont
in the danger zone. The book was to have
been a New Tear Magazine, published at
Anzac; but the news of the unexpected
evacuation delayed publication, and ulti-
mately it appeared in London. The Ansae
Book is the first and the last book produced
in Gallipoli. The artistic contents reach an
astonishingly high level of excellence, despite
the fact that the artists had to work with
such materials as iodine brushes, red and
blue pencils, and paper that was not always
white. The artist who has made an Anza-
cian reputation is David Barker. His color
work is remarkably good.
The letterpress has the cheerful, don’t-
care-a-damn Anzacian humor; and there
must have been almost as many poets as
there were fighters at Gallipoli.
Incidentally General Birdwood settles the
question of the origin of the word “Anzac.”
It will come as news to most readers to
hear that it was invented in Egypt. “When
I took over the command of the Australian
and New Zealand Army Corps in Egypt a
year ago,” writes General Birdwood, “I was
asked to select a telegraphic code address
for my Army Corps, and then adopted the
word ‘Anzac.’ Later on, when we had
effected our landing here, I was asked by
General Headquarters to suggest a name for
the beach where we had made good our first
precarious footing, and then asked that this
might be recorded as ‘Anzac Cove’ —a name
which the bravery of our men has now
made historical, while it will remain a geo-
graphical landmark for all time.” That
seems to dispose of the other usually
accepted explanation that “Anzac” was inj
vented at Gallipoli by a certain signallers
corps.
f
Arthur H. Adams writes: I was sur-
prised to find in The Ansae Hook, on page
104, that some verse entitled “The Trojan
War, 1915,” and published over my name
in The Bulletin, was printed, word
for word and with the same title, as
having been written by J. Wareham, Ist
Aust. Field Amb. Well, if J. Wareham
wants the copyright of my verses be is wel-
come to them. Anybody who fought at
Anzac has more right to them than, the
author, who didn’t. In fact, it is a highly
appreciated compliment that J. Wareham
selected the verses and that the editors
thought them good enough to appear in that
brilliant record of Australianism. Tbats
good enough for me.

VARIOVS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Respite.
A sun-bright day, when bent the silv’ry
trees
Before a dawdling wind, that sway’d their
cool
Leaves laughingly, until they twinkled green
And glistening as the ripples o’er the bay !
Upon the topmost bough the pine tree
reared
A blackbird sang. And in the shimmering
Of gum-tree boughs, black cassock’d magpies
shook
Their dusky feathers, chimed their mellow
notes.
No. traffic’s turmoil rose upon the air.
This day of rest was as a cool, strong palm
Laid on the throbbing brow of fevered Life.
A golden evening followed, trailing slow
Her garments, opal-hued, along the sky
Where, altar-like, poised high the crescent
moon.
The shaven grass sprang cool beneath our
tread.
While garden scents made dear and inti-
mate
Our quiet thoughts. No need was there of
words;
Lor understanding silence makes no call
On the interpreter. The world around,
Iveyed to that vibrant chord from muted
strings,
Was girt as we to meet the tides of life
E’er tragic Morrow, waking, stirred the
clouds
That had beguiled and hid it from our sight.
Spirit of this glad sun-warmed day, when
sleep
And darkness, in similitude of death,
Shall fold us, lead us on through aisles of
dreams
To view the cloistered goal where dreams
come true!
VICTORIA.
M. B. PATON.
A Silver Birch.
The silver birch upon the wintry hill
Stands like a white witch-maid.
Clear where the moon has found her with
its light,
Unheeding of the vagrant winds of night,
Mysterious and cold.
As spring’s blithe heralds blew their elfin
pipes
She woke to deftly robe
Her slender limbs in budlets, soft and
green,
And wove herself a pale, translucent screen
Of flakes of emerald.
Beneath her bending boughs the murmurous
bees
Plundered the summer’s store;
Each roving wind caressed her tenderly,
And bore afar some strains of melody,
Caught from her lovers’ lutes.
When autumn bent the ripened grain she
wore
A cloak of trembling gold;
Briefly, for in sweet waywardness of mood
She cast her golden cloak away, and stood
In silver nakedness.
I know not when she is most fair to see,
This lady mutable;
Ever she sways, a slender, sylphid thing,
While soft wind-song and wildbirds’ carol-
ling !
Proclaim her meed ,of praise.
MAORILAND.
LILLA GORMHUILLE MCKAY.
The Barmy Bo’sun.
I am the Barmy Bo’sun. ’Ere I stay
Watchin’ the tangled riggin’ of th’ trees
An’ thinkin’ this asylum Is a bay
And all th’ little ’illocks rollin’ seas.
They tole me I was barmy all the way
From Bonies Aires! I fought th’ flamin’
lot
An’ showed ’em I was just as sane as they !
An’ now they’ve gooe an’ left me ’ere to
rot! ’
“You prove I’m barmy an’ I’ll damn well go
Over th’ side an’ never sail no more!”
That broke ’em up! They laughed “IIoo-
hoo! 110-ho!
You’re goin’ straight in when we gets you
ashore!” Ht’A-
I never b’lieved ’em till th’ Weedin’ night
Th’ Ole Man served out rum an’ John
Bee kaid,
Shovin’ ’is Tot aside an’ full o’ fight,
“Blarst all yer rum! Warrer for me
instead !”'
/
I tremblin’ like a bleedin’ child.
I ses, “John Bee! Are you teetotal now?”
You never see a sailor man so wild!
’E knocked me down and yells, “You
blanky cow!”
I think ’e must ha’ give me sev’ral kicks;
I ’eard, far orf an’ dim, a stinkin’ row,
An’ ole John damnin’ all th’ loonatics
An’ roarin’ curses on “a lyin’ cow.”
I krwo then I was mad as mad could be!
I never fought no more—and then they
come
To joke an’ say, “Th’ poor ole beggar! ’e
Thought ole John Bee was done with
drinkin’ rum !”
An’ when th’ ship paid orf they brought me
’ere.
An’ ole John shook me fist. “Farewell,”
says ’e,
“Drift on, shipmate, an’ don’t y’ try ter steer,
An’ I’ll forgive th’ thing y’ said o’ me.”
So ’ere I sit with ’ands upon me knees,
Th’ wind among the riggin’ singin’ soft,
A-sailin’ this asylum through green trees—
Th’ maddest man that eVer went aloft.
N.s.w.

THURSDAY, JUNE 29, 191 G.
ENGLAND.
Wiien Hengist landed at Thanet island,
with a hoarse laugh of derision for the
credulity of the British prince who had
called him in to undertake a piece of con-
tract fighting against his own countrymen,
the world was looking the other way, and
the civilised fragments of it had no idea
that Providence had thrown into its retort
a couple of elements which in the experi-
ments of the ages were to produce some
astounding chemical reactions. Behind
Hengist came shipload after shipload of
Jutes, Saxons, Angles and Danes; and as
the evil-smelling shields of the piratical
hordes pressed inland, England began to
take shape and form. Crude Saxon his-
tory seems to suggest that the invaders
entirely slaughtered the original British
population; but there is decent proof that
they did nothing so hurtful. What really
happened was a race-blending in which the
savage Teuton from overseas, conquering
the semi-civilised Briton, brought him down
to his own level, married his sister and
passed on a blend of thought, feeling and
instinct to future generations. The so-
called Anglo-Saxon was really an Anglo-Celt,
as is shown by the shape of his .head, the
contour of his mind and, to some extent, by
the variegation of his whiskers. This was
the sub-structure of the nation ; but as time
went on other elements were added. The
Norman Conquest brought in a fine crowd of
brigands of almost every race to be found
at the handy end of Europe. The habit
which oppressed foreigners have had of look-
ing for an asylum in a country that was less
prone to homicidal insanity than their own
added to the blend from age to age. Jews,
Flemings, French, Huguenots and others
came in tens of thousands and mingled with
the island race; and later on a tremendous
peaceful invasion of the modern Piets and
Scots set in from the North and West. The
result is the English people—the paradox
among nations.
The man who claims that England has
done the finest things accomplished by any
nation is not a liar. The _ian who declares
that England has first and last been the
most unimaginative country in the world
may be equally truthful. The person who,
with his eye glued on certain events in the
European record, asserts that England
possesses matchless courage, faith and forti-
tude is speaking no idle thing. The cynic
who with a scoff assures us that the English
are the most ridiculous of peoples is neither
fool nor knave. The race-blend that Hen-
gist originated is all these things and a
great many more. There is scarcely an end
to the English paradox. The English people
are high-minded, enthusiastic, dull, keen,
sensitive, unemotional, efficient, slipshod,
steadfast. uncertain. courageous, . easily
stricken by panic, just, bitterly prejudiced,
patriotic, unsentimental, proud, meek, silent,
talkative, phlegmatic, vivacious, headstrong,
calculating, incorruptible, mercenary and 50
other things all at once. There never was
a nation so easily moved to a sort of
hysteria nor a nation that so completely
kept its head in time of crisis. There never
was a nation that expressed such contempt
for its leaders and yet backed those leaders
so solidly in all that they might attempt
or do. There never was a nation more
prone to eat soup with a fork, nor one that
had a better appreciation of the value of a
spoon. In a word, all history has no record
of muddle at all comparable with the Eng-
lish muddle, and no record of the achieve-
ment of the impossible that will stand beside
that of the English race. From England
have sprung the finest impulses that have
moved mankind, yet those impulses sel-
dom moved England. The Englishman has
gone forth to the ends of the earth and
exhibited himself as hero, clod, saint and
charlatan all rolled into one. He is loved,
feared and hated. The world looks to him
for wisdom and jests at his -folly. His
self-sacrifice is stupendous, his greed invin-
cible ; his vanity over-reaches all bounds,
and modesty is one of the keynotes of his
life. It all goes back to Hengist; for the
Englishman is the stolid Teuton, in an insu-
lar environment, with the Celt in his blood
forever trying to break through the outer
husk, but restrained, modified and kept in
its place by the Saxon weight and bulk that
surrounds it.
* * * *
In the storms of the past English . gales
blew strangely. The Puritan revolution of
the seventeenth century—a revolution in
which the great bulk of the people were only
remotely interested —made way for all other
revolutions, but died quietly in the sordid
return of Charles 11. to the throne. Eng-
lishmen in the coldest of cold blood, with
judicial thoroughness and a sense of im-
mense responsibility, cut off a king’s head;
then they brought back their kings, nursed
them, tamed them, revered them and clung
to the notion of monarchy when others to
whom they had first set the example of
republicanism had done with royalty for-
ever. Always there was the same impati-
ence of control in the hearts of Englishmen
as that which moved the French and Irish ;
but the Englishman was the easiest of all
men to govern, and in a kind of explosive
patience he waited for the slow reform
which gave him half what he asked for and
left him in a fury of restrained discontent.
He could pull down the railings of Hyde
Park to be allowed free speech on a public
question; but he would use no violence to-
wards the men in Parliament who delayed
the serious discussion of that question. His
outlook on the world was strangely insular or
even parochial. He hated Napoleon and
Nicholas I. as tyrants, but endured the
more intimate tyranny of the squire and
parson without serious protest. In time of
war sparks of fierce patriotism blazed up in
him and, through all confusion of purpose,
ignorance and blundering, he backed his
leaders until they pushed things to a suc-
cessful finish. He hated war and shouted
approval to Quaker whiskers wagging in
the cause of peace; yet, when that peace
was ruptured, he would put his cash and
labor into the ensuing discord as if war
were the supreme joy of his existence. First
and last, he was the surprise-packet of
Europe; but he thought himself the straight-
est goer of all and wondered when French-
men bitterly railed at “Perfidious Albion.”
The bulldog idea and the notion that Eng-
land’s strength lay in her holding-on power
which gradually grew up in the universal
mind was only partly true. It was the
quick, sudden flash of unexpected Celtic
imagination and Celtic valor, followed by
the stubborn, weighty Saxon determination,
that gave England so remarkable a place
in history; and it was the failure of this
erratic compound to explode at the right
time which once or twice dragged her down
in shame and humiliation.
* * * *
To speak of England’s national ideals is to
waste breath. Idealism exists in England to
a greater extent than in most countries; but
national ideals there are none. The sub-
conscious thought that makes Germans speak
of the fatherland and Englishmen of the
mother country is correct. The sex of Eng-
land, as a nation, is female. She makes up
for the absence of national ideals by strong
national instincts. She does not reason; she
feels. When, as in the case of the venerated
doctrine of Cobden, she attempts to arrive
at conclusions by logical argument, she throws
all the misguided enthusiasm into the busi-
ness of a washer] ady with convictions on the
question of eternal punishment. If the washer-
lady would give her instinct a chance it
would guide her into happier places; hut she
has been reasoning things out, and the dis-
aster of her thought becomes her fetich in
perpetuity. Because in periods of crisis there
is little time to apply feminine reason to the
state of affairs, England is generally on the
right side in a big turmoil; and once she has
taken her side, nothing known on this planet
has force enough to shift her or to make her
doubt that hers is the cause of God and
humanity. Somewhere, too, in the woman-
heart of the nation is a big tenderness for
those who are sic-k or hungry, for those out-
casts among peoples who lack something
which she deems might make them happier.
This is why she runs so much to missionary
societies, and why her ample shawl is flung
over the distant and sweltering nigger to the
partial neglect of some of her frozen children
at home. She who nursed Freedom on her
knee and spanked it into disciplined obedi-
ence to precedent and custom still feels some-
thing of a motherly thrill when the name of
the infant is mentioned.
* * * *
In the tremendous world-war that now
begins to look healthy through its agonies,
England at last seems like taking her true
place. The millions on the French front, the
steel wall of the blockade, the busy munition
factories, and the enormous war-credits which
leave something over to hand to needy Allies
are lively signs that the nation has once
more broken the husk of muddle, and stands
as a leader among the world-powers which
move on to victory. That victory seems now
to be fairly well assured, though it will still
take time to bring the German to a state of
complete repentance. But when the war is
over, England steps out into a new place, and
must face q. fntute different from any of
which the nation could have dreamed. No
lohger is England in any real sense an island
Rower. War invention has straitened the
narrow seas until they are of little more con-
sequence than the castle moat or the ditch
about a Roman castra. Henceforth England
is a continental nation, moving to the im-
pulses of other lands and touched by the
spirit of alien peoples. The test of peace
will be a bigger one than the test of war. A
re-adjustment of all old things will be neces-
sary ; a revolution within her own borders is
certain That revolution will probably be a
peaceful one; but it will be something larger
in its sweep and deeper in its purpose than
any movement that has yet shaken the Anglo-
Celtic world. Australia, with the perspective
gained bv looking over half the world, takes
stock of these things with some emotion. We
have our own work to do, our own place to
fill in the scheme of things; but we should be
dull indeed if so great an event as the re-
making of England left us cold. Under all
the folly, wisdom, cant, sincerity, boastful-
ness and heroism of that parent nation there
lies something of strength and truth and
reality which her sons must admire. The old
order changes; and the day when the
cannons cool may be bigger for Eng-
land than that on which Hengist drove his
viking prow to Thanet. After all, of the old
world’s peoples England still holds the largest
possibilities.
HOW ABOUT “THE VOICE OF THE
PEOPLE” GETTING A FEW WORDS
IN?
Of course, the referendum already has a
place in the machinery of government in
Australia. Only by its use can change be
made in the Commonwealth Constitution.
The numerous Parliaments may bring the
referendum into operation as part of the law-
incubating process. One of them did it the
other day in connection with the N. S. Wales
Pub Closing Bill, when a bewildered Minis-
try was looking round for a method of saving
its face. But this clumsy limitation on the
referendum is of little use to the people. Its
legitimate place in the scheme of things is not
that of an instrument to be employed only by
the grace of politicians who happen to tumble
into a hole or to be terrified by doubts about
the way the cat is going to jump. Used in
that uncertain way by flustered balancers on
the rail of expediency, it may easily become
a source of abject political demoralisation,
making Party Government an even more
unclean thing than it is. To a democracy
the referendum must be something the elec-
tors can get hold of. Without it they are
almost powerless so far as popular govern-
ment goes —mere clay in the hands of adven-
turous potters, moulding what they call the
popular will into any distortion of the origi-
nal that the circumstances of a moment
may suggest. When and how and through
whom are the people of Australia going to
see the referendum made a genuinely work-
ing part of the machine? Labor has a plank
in its platform bearing this label. Labor has
been prodigal of promises on the subject.
But Labor in Parliament with a majority is
something very different from Labor battling
to get there. -Labor politicians have system-
atically side-tracked this pledge. Why ?
The answer is easy. Because the politician,
of every brand in every country, has shrunk
from yielding any of the powers of patron-
age within his grasp. The referendum-initia-
tive -would tear many of them away, make the
politician the servant of the people in a
sense that was real, and thus end the
anomaly of a democracy suppliant at the
doors of Parliament. The greatest of all
Parliaments is the Parliament of the People.
Thf, Bulletin wants to see it called into
session, its deliberations systematised. Hur-
riedly whistling it up once in a bine moon is
not the ideal. And the march of events in
Australia, where the landscape is getting
cumbered with political confusion, where the
allegedly National Government skulks in
obscurity and various other Governments are
playing the mischief generally, makes it more
necessary than ever it was that this Parlia-
ment should take a hand in the game. For
many years a host of loud talkers have been
running round yelling that they want to do
something or other “for the people” because
they have a mandate to that effect. What
this paper, with a profound distrust of the
“mandates” flung from the hurly-burly of a
general election, wants to see is the people
given a chance to consult the menu for them-
selves, and if they object to being served
with hash to say so.
* * * #
The Bulletin is not nursing any fan-
tastic notions about the referendum being
The Great Democratic Pill —a universal cure-
all for every social and economic evil. Nor
is this paper filled with a large, full-bodied
faith that the. referendum, if brought into
regular operation, would not lead to some
queer results. It certainly would. But that
is no argument against a political system.
If it were, the outfit the democracy is work-
ing with now would have been blown out
long ago. Anyhow, the results of not having
the referendum are sufficiently queer to be
done without in the future. That it could be
ridden to death, and that its adoption is not
without difficulty are admitted. Applied at
once to the whole field of legislation by seven
Legislatures it would become a nuisance.
There are some Bills submitted to Parlia-
ment which it would be farcical to submit
to a referendum, their bulk and complexity
being such as to make it ridiculous that they
should go holus bolus to the constituencies for
an a^ e /; in ? “ Yes f ” “No.” To submit a
Tariff Bill, tor instance, to the popular V nte
would be silly; to submit it item by item
wildly insane. A complicated Pensions Bill
or a Railways Authorisation Bill cou
hardly come within the scope of a referent
to the ballot. In such measures as th
principle and detail are so bound up that the
question on which the vote would hinge could
not be determined. But these consideration*
of difficulty do not touch the point at issiJ
They are difficulties which in any case Zo
overcome by the public having* authority
through the referendum to call upon Parlin
merit to resign. On the other hand Bills
covering such subjects as Compulsory Mili-
tary Service, Preference of Employment to
Unionists, Abolition of the Leg.' Council
Amendment of the Electoral System in -mv
given direction, the Extinction of Cocked
Ilats, the Control of Hospitals, the Political
Rights of Women, and so on, could appro
priately be made subjects of reference to the
public. They affect the public in a particu
larly intimate way. Their points are clear
To deny the public the right to consultation
about them is a denial of democratic faith
With a widening of the functions of the State
and increasing political intelligence in the
people, the growth and power of extra-
parliamentary institutions and the accumu-
lating influence of the wire-pull, the exten-
sion of popular control over Parliamentar-
ians and their work grows steadily more im-
perative. Parliaments in Australia are not
under control of the people except so far as
the electors can at long intervals apply the
axe to the Ins or Outs. That looks a poor
substitute for the real thing. And the fruits
of it are rotten.
* * * *
Men who call themselves democrats and
yet dodge the democracy’s claim to self-gov-
ernment are shams, afraid of their own medi-
cine. Men who argue that the democracy
will make grave mistakes and run amok and
commit hari-kari merely say what can be said
about every form of government. Who have
made more appalling mistakes than despots?
What greater messes have been perpetrated
than those committed by Parliaments un-
accountable to the mass of the people whose
lives and liberties they juggled with? If
there is anything in the theory of Democracy,
if popular government is ever going to be
given a chance, if men and women are to
govern themselves instead of being governed
by superior persons, windbags, time-serving
flatterers or scheming reactionaries, then the
theory has to be pushed on to a plane where it
can be tested by trial. If the Australian
people have been bound in any direction it is
towards the creation of a Democracy. But
the goal is never going to be reached by sit-
ting contentedly down on the way, fumbling
at the problems of democracy with blunt tools
and nerveless hands, and only getting up to
dance to the tunes of irresponsible adven-
turers. No one contemplating the political
history of Australia can at this stage seri-
ously contend that if the referendum had
been in operation the position of the country,
socially and economically, would not have
been more creditable, the nation’s industries
more stable, and government more enlight-
ened than it is to-day. The opposite argument
cannot come even from the mouths of those
people whose mission in life it has been to
obstruct the extension of popular govern-
ment. Their complaint all along has been
that the public will was being outraged.
What force, then, would there be in an objec-
tion from them against the public will being
expressed in some more coherent way than
by the riot and uproar of general elections—
those often degrading orgies of abuse and lies
and travesties on the settlement of public
policy? Is government by yell and howl, by
handing out doles and soothing-syrups in
anticipation of the next yell, by walking
tight-ropes and clamoring at the pawnshop
preferable to government directly through the
people? This paper knows of only one
answer. It believes that the majority of the
people know of only one. And it hopes that
patriotic Australians will begin to demand
where the politicians are on this subject and
refuse to be fobbed off by a passionate under-
taking in the name of Democratic Progress
to supply something or other at a ha’penny
a pound cheaper.
TRAINING A SOLDIER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I once saw a representation of a troopship
in a melodrama. The men,'gowned to perfec-
tion, stood about in taking attitudes, bearing
rifles and swords; some leaned over the side
and drew each other’s attention to enemy
ships; others sat about polishing weapons
and pipe-claying belts. Oflicers clad in the
panoply of war passed to and fro. All
hands saluted, presented arms, brought their
swords to the recover, and in other respects
made the scene glorious. I believed it all
before I took to soldiering.
We parade after breakfast. The wear is
what our sergeant terms “blueys and white
’ats.” Smith is found to be absent. A cry
goes up for him. People howl “Smith down
companion-ways. In a dishevelled and panic-
stricken condition Smith appears —four am
a half minutes late. His superiors view him
with loathing. He is reminded of his past,
of his habit of leaning against railings; o
the fact that he appeared on parade with hm
coat-collar undone some days previously, a
of an occasion when he had to be told to ge
his hair cut. A shameful end is predicted to
him. . fW
A sensation is caused by the discovery t <
Brown has not shaved. He puts forwarc ■
desperate defence —that he is suffering no
“scurvy,” that his razor has been stolen, a
that, when used, it tears the skin off his la •
He is told that he is the one dirty' naan
the ship, that such conduct is mtolera >
that by it the fame of his troop, aye> or
entire unit, is imperilled. Saluting
anguished manner he withdraws his
butic countenance to the rear. ,
A diversion is occasioned by the Did ,•
Captain shouting menacingly for the (Jr •
Trumpeter, who has sounded tne
minutes’” call two minutes early.
Orderly-Trumpeter is an absurd lad, wk ,
all day in a remote part of the deck)
his instrument festooned about his ne •
garishly-colored cords, and practises
in a low key. Harmless, and very earnest, as


most musicians are. It is easy to see that
lie hns measured the enormity of his offence.
]lis rabbit-like eyes wear a hunted look.
Orderly-Officer: “Trumpeter, what is the
meaning of this What, I ask you,
is the meaning of it? Your orders are to
blow your bugle at five minutes to nine; you
blow it at seven minutes to nine. Two
minutes early! Don’t salute, sir! I say
stop saluting! Stand properly at attention
and don’t move. Never mind about the ship
rolling. Don’t move, and be silent! ’Ten-
shun! Stand steady there. Have you not
the ship’s clock to take your time by? Is it
not there for all to see? Stop moving your
mouth about instantly. Do you realise the
seriousness of your conduct? And why
haven’t you got your jacket on, sir? Gone
to be washed, eh!” (Bitterly) “It’s always
being washed. Always! You never wear it.
I saw you the day before yesterday sitting
without it up there on the monkey-deck,
where, in any case, you are strictly forbidden
to go. What do you mean by it? llow do
von expect to end? Orderly-Sergeant, see
that this boy gets his jacket on, and let me
have no more nonsense with him.”
The rebuke public is mostly along these
lines. Our officers are not, on the whole, an
articulate lot. They have a weakness for
the repetitive method. It has been fairly
successful up to now, but I question some-
times whether it will stand the wear and tear
of the months to come. And yet—l am not
sure. The uniform has a marvellous potency.
It is more than an emblem of power: its in-
fluence is spiritual.
Apply it to my case. I am in the ranks.
In private life I held a good position in my
profession. In the struggle for success, as
the world understands it, I am ahead of my
captain. In the interests of good order and
discipline, the thousands of private soldiers
who might be inclined to question the superi-
ority of their superiors need to be brought
into line. The methods under which one
chafes at first are those which mankind, after
thousands of years of practically ceaseless
warfare, has decided to be the best. The
scheme is very simple and very human. The
end desired is that the soldier shall regard
his officer —anv officer-i—as infallible or there-
abouts. If the spell fails to work the officer
is not fit for his job. In the first place there
is the sartorial element, which as every
priest, parson and barrister knows, is a most
effective hypnotiser. It is hard to feel
really captain of one’s soul in blue dungarees
that "fit nowhere, a rag hat that has not been
washed for days, and bare feet. Only the ex-
ceptional man can preserve his dignity when
deprived of his boots. The soldier wears the
dungarees; the officer a uniform in which
most men look their best. When, in addition,
the officer is authorised to order his
men to their amusements and their
devotions as well as to their labors; to scru-
tinise their every meal in detail and provide
advice on the subject—-when, in fact, he is
in a position to behave as though he knew
it all, and the able-bodied adults under him
were the victims of some form of arrested
mental development, then the spirit of carp-
ing dies, and the sturdiest of rebels begins to
accept the whole business as a matter of
course.
That is the first stage in the training of a
soldier. It is as far as I have got at present.
Oxford.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Shaking the Bones of Cobden in the
Teeth of Reform.
The deadly fear that after the war Britain
mav come to her senses and go in tor a soim
tariff reform which will give her own people a
job sits like a nightmare on the chests ot
some peo])le in London and Manchestei.
After the I’aris Conference, where W •
Hughes, lifelong Freetrader and tan
sinker, voiced a furious appeal for the crus 1
ing of German trade by inserting a i ig
m a role of apparently meaningless words in
certain resolutions, the London Daily JSe s
came out with the amazing declaration that
“it was Freetrade that enabled Lngland
bear the brunt of the war.” _ By what ex-
traordinary jirocess of reasoning the choco-
late daily arrived at that conclusion t e
cables give no hint; but it is evident enough
that the doctrines of Cobpen are still being
preached in spite of all they have involved,
and that there are people fatuous enough
cling to the notion that a country can lose
its means of livelihood to the foreigner an
still keep on living in fit condition to retain
him blow for blow in the event of wai.
On all the evidence, it seems quite Pl aia
that but for the ConoEN-ites Germany would
never have risen to the height of wealt
and power which could make a war on sue
a scale possible. Living on her outside in-
vestments and her carrying trade, Britan
starved her own people, increased the num-
bers of her odd-jobbers and reduced the total
of her genuine industrials, and then pointei
with pride to the fact that the things made
in Germany were cheap. And all the tim
Germany, out of the profits of that cheap-
ness, was making big guns and preparing tor
war. It was rotten policy on the German s
ptu-t The Daily News and the British
soap-boilers and the makers of English choco-
iate-creams were fighting his battle far more
effectively than he could hope to fight it in
the held. But the swank of the Prussian
forced things, and the uproar started. How
. reetrade enabled England to play her part
in the struggle hiust remain one of the great
Nothing has yet come to light
which can show that it had the most remote
connection with war preparation. On the
contrary, Freetrade practically caused the
war, was in effect set aside when the war
began, and can only be revived if the nation
decides on suicide when the war is over.
How About Australia?
It is easy enough to understand why Pro-
tectionist newspapers in London should be
turning handsprings over the resolutions
adopted at the Paris Economic Conference.
They have their propaganda to push and
these resolutions are obviously of much
assistance in carrying on the job. But
there is nothing in them to cause any Aus-
tralian to fling up his hat, nor on the face
of them any indication that all Hughes’s
strenuous elbowings to get into the proceed-
ings were of any service to the people of
this country. The exact purpose of the
Conference was never clear. It was pro-
bably _ more diplomatic than anything else.
Certainly the powers of the delegates were
extremely limited. They could bind their
respective Governments to nothing, and it
is more than likely that the real object of
the Conference was to frame something for
the special entertainment of Berlin. Any-
how, that is what it did. How much it will
impress the German remains to be seen.
Perhaps he will not be impressed at all.
What the rest of the world will probably
be most impressed by is that the war is still
in full blast. Whether it can be so con-
cluded that it will be possible to impose the
Conference resolutions upon the enemy is
a question going to. the heart of the whole
business. It is a question not answered
by making optimistic forecasts. There are
half a dozen conceivable endings to this
war, and the one which alone would make
possible a situation such as was envisaged
at Paris is not the most probable of them.
It is no good being deluded on this point.
In any case, Australian soldiers are not
fighting in France to keep German-made
frying-pans out of the Commonwealth. That
can be done by work in Australia. The only
reason it had not been accomplished before-
hand was that quite a number of people were
unpatriotic, lazy and blind. The kernel of
the long series of resolutions is that the
Allies undertake to increase their produc-
tion, make themselves independent of enemy
countries so far as raw materials and
“manufactured articles essential to the nor-
mal development of their economic activi-
ties” are concerned, and facilitate the inter-
change of products. And they are to do
this by adopting “such measures as seem to
them most suitable for carrying out this
resolution... .according to the principles
which govern their economic policy.” It
was not necessary for Australia to get into
the war or to stay in it to know that she
wanted a tariff. The loud reminder on the
subject from Paris is merely a repetition of
what has been said a thousand times within
the Commonwealth, and the essence of it all
is that the duty of Australia is to get
busy on the vital affairs of the nation. It
will he as easy as tumbling off a log for
Australia after valiantly blatherskiting
about German trade to find herself 10 years
hence in essentially the same economic and
industrial condition as she was prior to the
war, and yet to have conformed to the
spirit of the Paris resolutions. Australia
has heard the voice of Hughes lifted afar
off in strident protestation about the Empire
and the German. What Australia still has
to hear about is the distinctly Australian
side to the problem. To us that is the
important point. Rooting out German
trade in Australia while war is on is one
thing—and easy. Building up Australian
trade is another. And that we must do in
Australia and for ourselves.

Enter the Prophet’s Family with a Gun.
The green flag of Mahomet, which didn’t
wave when the Bulgar was at the gate of
Constantinople, and hasn’t been hoisted in
the present tumult, looks as if it might pass
permanently back to the land of its birth.
Arabia, the' land of mystery and the Prophet,
has leaped to arms, and the jump is not at
the throat of the Allies, but at the whiskers
of the Turk. Rebellion has been simmer-
ing a long time in the hot home of coffee and
dates, and it is scarcely to be wondered at
that the true Arab who first flashed a holy
scimitar in the name of God and the cause
of a great ideal has been looking askance at
some of the recent doings by the Bosphorus.
A Sultan, wearing the Iron Cross of the
infidel whose emissaries appeared in Arabia
only as tax gatherers and exploiters, could
not seem good in his eyes. The long pilgrim
railway creeping down from Damascus
looked a devilish contrivance to the ancient
faithful, particularly as it could bring sol-
diers to buck up the demand for taxes. Ihe
Turk, Teutonised and modern looking, made
no appeal to the imagination of Islamic
saints; and the sight of huge masses of the
Prophet’s followers prosperous under British
rule in the east, and contented under I rench
rule in the west, helped to aggravate matters.
The result is that Araby the Blest has risen
up with a gun and decided to break with the
breed of Abdul. If the Mohammedan world
rallies to the Arab cause it may be a good
thing all round. In all the greed of nations
for territorial expansion, no hand, unless it
were a Mohammedan one, as in the case of
Egypt and Turkey, has ever been stretched
out towards Nejd and Nafud and Dalma.
Arabia is the shrine of ancient things—per-
haps of things more ancient than a_y or
which the most imaginative archaiologist
dreams —and if the Kalifate returns there,
e reen flag and all, under the protection of
the Allies, it may save Europe some worry.
Tf only for the sake of poetry and romance,
it is up to some country to remain ancient
and refuse to countenance wireless telegraphy,
typewriters, ice-cream and musical comedy.
The Mexican Fire and the Danger of its
Spread.
The attempts of German agents to stir up
trouble in Mexico might easily have far-
reaching effects in causing a wide cleavage
between Latin America anu the big Republic
of the northern continent; in fact, if Ger-
man diplomacy were the thing it has been
cracked up to be, the discord in the western
hemisphere might almost equal that of
Europe. Three years or so back, when the
trouble in Mexico first became acute, the
big Latin States of the South were seri-
ously worried about it, and matters may not
have improved since. Of course, Mexico is
a sort of an outlaw, and the brigandage and
disorder prevalent there are recognised as
bad business all round; but the conditions
are not much worse than they have
been in the past in Argentine, Chile
and Brazil, not to speak of the mob of
smaller republics further north. United
States interference in the island of Hayti
doesn’t matter much, as the landscape about
there is mostly nigger, but the interference
of Washington in Panama and Nicaragua
is more serious in Latin,-American eyes.
Some time ago the United States of Colom-
bia retired hurt and bitterly cursing from
w'ordy warfare with the Yankee; and
Venezuela, not so many years since, had a
nasty feeling of resentment about being dic-
tated to by a person who had very little
understanding of her local troubles. Alto-
gether, for a country that doesn’t keep an
army, and whose navy is pretty small in
proportion to the vast things it has to guard,
the United States has a good many irons in
various fires which, while small in them-
selves, might make a tolerably large blaze
if they came together. If the German were
clever enough to inflame the racial ‘feeling
of sympathy with Mexico, and Washington
seriously pushed the war beyond the Rio
Grande, the supply of American munitions
for the Allies might suddenly stop because
the makers of the fireworks had to turn and
defend Standard Oil and the Beef Trust
against the attack of 15 angry nations in
arms.

A Weary Old Swindle.
The scandal of the indecently spun-out law-
suit persists, at the expense of pretty well
every member of the community except the
lawyers who profit by it. The most cynic-
ally impudent example is provided in the
divorce jurisdiction. Here the length of
many trials is limited only by the capacity
of the parties to find the cash to pay for
them. For months on end divorce cases are
run through at the rate of dozens per day.
Then the steady business of unhitching the
indigent is interrupted by. a ‘‘society
divorce” —meaning an affair in which one
or more of the participants has money.
Those who understand the game settle down
forthwith to the spectacle of a lengthy if
not strenuous struggle. What follows is no
indication that justice was not done in the
hundreds of brief, business-like trials which
went before. It means merely that a horde
of barristers and attorneys have observed
easy money in the offing—and are out for
it. At the “advice on evidence” stage
counsel and the instructing solicitors begin
by earmarking troops of likely witnesses.
Many of them have nothing of any value to
say. But when they get into the box it is
counsel’s own fault if they are not anchored
there. They are encouraged to maunder
on about a hundred pointless incidents; to
tell how the respondent took a ticket to
Essendon on a day when the co-re. was
seen at Moonee Ponds; to detail the exact
position in which the lady stood while she
was buying the ticket, and the sort of
costume the co-re. was wearing; after
which the gentlemen representing “the
other side” uprise, and, with splendid assi-
duity, go over the whole idiotic yarn once
more. All at so many guineas per hour.
It is the best of good business for the law-
yers—one of the few departments of the
world of money-making where, while the
charges are exorbitant, there are no bad
debts. The legal gentlemen see to that by
insisting on being paid as they go along, if
there is anv doubt about the matter. But
it wastes the time of the Judges, who are
the servants of the public; it results in
monopolising court rooms, which are the
property of the taxpayers; and it provides
the community with the corrupting example
of a legalised swindle. A strong Judge can
do a little to check the abuse; but only a
little. Nothing will be achieved in a big
way till the lower legal orders acquire a
new viewpoint of their duties towards
litigants. But the profession is so but-
tressed by cant on the subject of its “high
ideas,” “unsmirched honor” and the like,
that reform from withiXi appears to be a
long way off.
A Glimpse of Russia.
From a neutral, formerly in Sydney, after
lately spending some weeks in Russia:
It is quite on the cards that I shall return to
Russia soon, though I hope not before the war is
over —or at least before peace is in sight, because
life in Russia under war conditions is not exactly
pleasant. This is especially the case in Petrograd
for, strange as it may seem, the official capital is
much worse off, in nearly every respect, than any
other town in the great Empire. This is due to the
fact that in times of peace Petrograd depends chiefly
on oversea trade for its supplies and fuel and
materials for its industries. Now, of course, every-
thing has to come by rail, and the railways are quite
unable to deal with all the extra war transport. Con-
sequently everything is frantically dear in Petrograd,
and there have been all kinds of “famines.” Also,
for want of fuel, the factories have had to stop
sometimes, and even the private houses could not
always be warmed sufficiently—and last winter was
very cold. In Moscow at Christmas the thermometer
went down to 30deg. below zero Fahrenheit!
Hack of organisation has always been one of the
Russian’s worst shortcomings, and it makes itself
badly felt again in this war. At times it has paralysed
military actions and spoiled the otherwise splendid
spirit of the army. But the Russians are a wonderful
people. Almost any other nation would have thrown
up the sponge in despair if it had got into the hope-
less mess that Russia made for itself last summer
after the big retreat from Poland. Think of the
awful disappointment: the collapse of one stronghold
after the other, and that after the brilliant .Galicia
campaign. And then think of the flood of refugees
six millions of them! It was chaos everywhere. The
stories one hears about the retreat and flight are
pitiful. And then to think that it was. all (or
chiefly) due to the highest and most responsible man
—the Minister of War. Either from stupidity ov
worse (there are very ugly rumors) he neglected to
order the necessary ammunition and rifles in time.
He is now being prosecuted at the demand of the
Duma. I am afraid it is going to be the biggest
scandal of the war.
But the Russians are more or less used to scandal
of this kind (there was another, very much the same,
of a General who betrayed his army in Eastern Prus-
sia), and they don’t seem a bit discouraged through
it all. For toughness and doggedness they almost
beat the Britisher—which is saying much !
As for trade and other possibilities in Russia after
the war, there is no doubt that capital and suitable
men will be the main difficulties. The German had
practically a monopoly of Russian trade, and
it won’t be easy to keep them out once
peace is restored. For although there is a good deal
of talk in Russia about “doing without the Germans
in future,” the conviction that it is possible or de-
sirable is not at all strong.
The Chinese Kitchener.
“Feng Siiui” reads a joint burial service
Between Ytjas Shih-Kai, strongest man in Asia,
and Kitchener, strongest man in Europe, there were
points of resemblance. Europe is as full of political
mandarins as China. The belly of the party joss
is as enthusiastically rubbed in Peking as in London.
The silver tongue of the Occidental orator becomes
in China the magic brush of the writer of memo-
rials, full of brave words and empty of intentions.
Amidst the torrents of oratory and blizzards of
memorials that swept around them, Yuan Shih-Kai
and Kitchener remained comparatively silent.
Kitchener's tall, straight figure looked like efficiency
in uniform. Yuan Shih-Kai, in his enormous
winter clothes, with his elephantine waddle, looked
like a fat, stolid, enigmatical Buddhist deity. Both
of them stood for efficiency and organisation. If
Yuan’s results were not as great as Kitchener s
it was only because his material was more pathetic-
ally impossible.
The fiasco of the Japanese war taught him the
absolute necessity for military preparedness. Through
all the subsequent ups and downs of Chinese political
life; through the stormy period of the Empress
Dowager’s rule, when heads fell like rain; through
the Boxer trouble, the Hundred Days of Reform,
the coup d’etat , the Manchu abdication and the
setting-up of the alleged Republic—through all these
he kept his head above water, because he possessed
a highly efficient little army, trained on modern
lines, and officered by Chinese who had been tlnough
the military mill of Germany. This army was the
trump card which he always threw on the table at
the right moment. It obediently broke through its
really good discipline and looted Peking merely to
give him a good excuse for not proceeding to Nan-
king, where the provisional Government had offered
him the Presidency while really thirsting for his
blood. The contrast between these troops and the
rest of China’s military machine was ludicrous. They
were Yuan’s soldiers, as the new British army was
Kitchener’s army, and without patriotic prejudice
would loot, burn and kill their fellow-countrymen
at his word. They gained him the Presidency, and
the Presidency gained him the money-bags. Especially
in his later days Yuan showed all Kitchener’s
contempt for political tomfoolery. The Chinese
Parliament, after 12 months of wrangling, tearing
of pigtails, bombing, knifing and poisoning, had not
decided on its own internal constitution. The
fangs, or political parties, were as numerous and
unstable as the shadows of the bush. Yuan sent
300 members packing. Those who made an
early start got to the foreign settlements
with whole skins; the rest disappeared in
mysterious ways. Yuan then, ignoring Parliament,
which talked itself to death, elected an advisory
council of his personal friends. The solemn farce-
delicious spectacle to the Chinese people —of repub-
lican government by means of a military autocracy
went on to the day of his death.
Yuan saved China from utter disintegration on
three separate occasions, and his dropping of the
helm seems to be a sign for the breaking of the vast
hull of the empire—that empire which with
a thousand leaks, a sprung keel and 40ft. of water
in the hold, has yet kept afloat through so many
international typhoons that it had come to be
regarded as unsinkable.
Arranging for Fifteen Years of Muddle.
“Chug’’ his kick :
The matter of reserving nine acres of water
frontage as a park is not one of enormous import-
ance; but small tufts of fur show the way the cat
jumps. North Sydney Council asked for the penin-
sula between Gore Cove and Bald Head Bay for
public recreation purposes; and the Government
replied that it could have the ground from July 1
for 15 years on the distinct understanding that it
handed it back at the end of that period, owing to
the “absolute necessity for providing water front-
ages for commercial purposes at the port of Sydney,
which was destined in the comparatively near future
to become one of the largest maritime ports of the
world.” Nothing could indicate much more plainly
the grip which the congestionists have on N. S.
Wales or the depth and sincerity of the Govern-
ment's alleged policy of decentralisation. Already
the port of Sydney is carrying just about all the
traffic which it ought reasonably to be expected to
carry; and the opening up of the closed ports to
north and south would leave a little room for the
children to play about the harbor’s edge. But the
Government apparently cares nothing for these
things. To pack Sydney to the infinite discomfort
of its people, the heaviest possible loss to the State
and the personal advantage of certain persons of
generous waistcoat girth, is evidently the secret
policy of those who have the political sway. Decen-
tralisation is merely election talk. If those who
have again and again promised to find outlets for
trade at other deep-water ports of N. S. Wales had
spoken with any thought of sincerity and had made
the smallest effort towards carrying their schemes to
fruition, there would be no need to worry about
Sydney water frontages. As things stand, it may
be taken that the case of the Bald Head park time-
limit affords proof that the State Government has
decided that there shall be no serious effort towards
decentralisation for 15 years, and for as long there-
after as the public can be fooled into believing
that an accumulation of bricks in one spot means
progress. Nothing could be idler than the idea that
the growth of Sydney to abnormal dimensions is a
subject for pride. Every additional brick stands
for further muddle and lessened efficiency ; and it is
about time that the State said definitely that it
could no longer afford to drag goods all round the
country for the mere purpose of filling somebody’s
pocket in Sydney when there were simple and direct
roads to the sea.
An Aspiration.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
This is the shortest day of all the year.
So few the hours between the pale sunrise
And the first star of twilight flashing clear .
Give me to greet the day with shining eyes
(The little day too quickly doomed to die) ;
In love and patience make me very wise.
And if the radiant ones of earth pass by
Curve Thou my lips responsive to then-
mirth ; .
Or tune my ear to sorrow’s faintest sigh.
On leaves and stilly trees and pulseless
earth
I’ll pour a deeper measure of my love
Maybe a spring flower sooner will have
birth.
So late the sunrise in the sky above.
So soon the star of twilight flashing deal.
This is the shortest day of all the year!
N.s.w., .Tune 21.
JOHN EGAN.

BRAINS DIRECT EVERY OPERATION.
A business run by “hands” alone
soon lags in the race against another
run by hands AND DIRECTED BY
EXPERT BRAINS.
The Goodyear Tyre and Rubber pro-
ducts wouV never have obtained the
high pitch of perfection they now
possess had hands alone been respon-
sible for their production.
Every year a sum in excess of
£26,000 is spent in research work
alone at the Akron factory. The
rubber experimental laboratories are
the most complete in the world, and
discoveries made there by experts
have attained world-wide acceptance
in many industries.
Primarily their object is to ever and
always improve the quality and wear-
ing powers of Goodyear Rubber, to
eliminate waste and expense in pro-
cess of manufacture, and, above all,
to produce this year a better article
for the money than was made last
year.
That their efforts are successful is
more than proved by the ever-
increasing sale of Goodyear Tyres
and Rubber products, an increase in
1915 over the preceding year of 18;i
per cent, in Tyre products, and 98
per cent, in mechanical lines, such as
Belting, Hose and Packing.
Nothing that we have said about
Goodyear products has as much in-
fluence on Goodyear sales as the
things which Goodyear users say of
them every day.
Ask the user of Goodyear products,
and prove it.
Goodyear 1 vies, Tubes , Belting , Hose and Packings are now obtainable throughout Australia & N.Z
Verdun—and Other Places.
Verdun remains a nightmare mystery.
The story about the place being of no im-
portance has had a sick look for a long
time, and so has the confident assertion that
the situation could not he forced by the
German. The inescapable facts are that
Verdun has seemed to France important
enough to defend to the last ditch, and to
the Hun sufficiently desirable to attack in
the most desperate manner. Whether the
Hun is a lunatic and doing all tHis in
obedience to a brain storm is another mat-
ter ; and, after all, it is of little consequence
one way or the other. The crux of the
sanguinary business is—Will the French or
the Germans succeed in what they are try-
ing at the moment to do? So far the Ger-
mans are creeping closer, and at the begin-
ning of the week were almost where the
decisive struggle would be determined. They
must have lost enormously. The French
losses must have been very great, too. If
the German fails to get Verdun he will have
suffered a serious blow. If the French fail
to keep him out there will be nothing for
the Allies to rejoice over, unless it be that the
price the enemy paid for success was greater
than the advantage achieved. But on that
point there can be no certainty until it is
clear what price he did pay, what the
defence cost, and what the advantage
achieved consists of. Meanwhile along the
rest of the Western Front the position
remains one of almost-stationary activity.
The comforting signs are on the other side
of the scrum. There the Russians are still
toiling hard and successfully, and appear to
have pushed the enemy in the south right
back to the Carpathians. That is good to
hear; and as the Italian is also hitting
back hard in the Trentino, the Austrian is
a busy person. There are indications that
the German is feeling rather more anxious
about his sausage than was the case a year
or so ago. But the Allies cannot afford to
ease off in the least on that account. Bigger
and still bigger efforts are demanded —if only
to relieve a little of the awful pressure upon
the magnificent French. Incidentally, peace
talk is again rumbling in the rear; but the
kind of peace the Allies are after is not in
sight yet.
From the fact that there are still about
150,000 unmarried men of military age in
Australia who have not got into khaki, it
looks as if the Defence Department’s system
is wrong somewhere. The Bulletin there-
fore proposes:—
(1) That no further casualty lists should be pubs
fished. The knowledge that a man is liable to be
killed may stop him from recruiting.
(2) That no further military punishments or court-
martials be reported. The fact that a man is liable
to two or three years’ gaol for a military offence is
liable to turn him against the job.
(3) That no reference to rifles, cannons, bombs,
howitzers, swords, bayonets or other unpleasant
weapons be permitted. These give a shivery feeling
to many people.
(i ) That no reference to pen-ions be made. Many
men, being reminded thus of the dangers of the
soldier’s fife, may be thereby induced to stay at
home.
(5) That no reference be permitted to war profits.
The fact that fat war profits are to be made at home
is liable to restrain many men from enlisting for
the Front.
(6) That no reference should be permitted to the
heat of Egypt or the cold of Flanders, to the burn-
ing sand o! Sinai or the slop of France. These are
uncomfortable things, and many of the 150,000 who
haven’t enlisted must hate discomfort.
There are a number of other restrictions
which m : ~ht seriously be considered. Con-
scription ing vetoed by the political Labor
bodies and therefore “unthinkable,” the Gov-
ernment, which has promised “the last man
and the last shilling,” must plainly do some-
thing more than has been done hitherto in
order to get these 150,000 to the Front. The
fault seems to this paper to lie largely with
tlu Censorship. _t has permitted the people
to know that war is war —and under a volun-
tary system nothing is more likely to
keep a lot of people from recruiting than
that. If they can’t be got to the
Front in any other “voluntary” way they
must be cajoled there. Anything, in short,
including the complete muzzling of the press,
rather than offend the P.L.L. by bringing in
Conscription.
The Commissionership of Police in S. Aus.,
becoming vacant by Raymond’s superannua-
tion, has been filled in a routine and un-
surprising way. The various soldiers and
politicians suggested by press and public are
disregarded, and seniority in the service takes
Inspector Edwards into the top place. 11. A.
Ring, of the Commissioner’s office, becomes
Chief Clerk in place of G. L. Reed. This
means promotions for others for a long way
down the ladder. Indeed, as Edwards is
already 62, the whole question will be opened
again before long.

Melba duly gave 6clat to the first night
of “Quinney’s” at Melbourne Playhouse on
Saturday by appearing in portion of the
fourth act of “Otello.” The excerpt con-
tained none of the trills or thrills that the
audience possibly expected, but mostly re-
vealed in softly-sung recitative Desdemona’s
apprehensions of her approaching doom. The
scene was artistically set with a medieval
bed and other ancient furniture, and Melba
was charmingly made up for the part; but
the incident was too slight to show her
powers or fully test the ability of Fritz
Hart’s band. Of course, the songstress ren-
dered the music beautifully, and dramati-
cally created the atmosphere of tragedy.
The orchestra, too, did its bit very
well, and Miss Anne Williams officiated
pleasingly as Emilia, though her job was
so small that she seemed like an almost
superfluous detail.
* * * *
“Quinney’s,” the four-act comedy which
followed after a long interval, is quite big
enough to have an evening to itself. Written
by H. A. Vachell and showing considerable
dramatic craftsmanship, it presents the
story of a domineering curio-dealer forced
by circumstances and his feminine belong-
ings into adopting a more agreeable bearing.
The improvement in the ancient, Joseph
Quinney, is brought about through his
daughter, Posy, hankering after his foreman,
James Miggott. James is a clever cabinet-
maker and a steady and reliable youth all
round, but Joseph will not tolerate him as
a son-in-law. When Posy insists on marry-
ing the lad with the quiet but determined
approval of her ma, Joseph offers his rela-
tives the option of obeying his orders or
leaving the Quinney roof at once. Mother
and daughter accept the latter alternative
with a decision and firmness that tempo-
rarily shake Joseph’s resolution to crush
the rebellion. Hereabouts, however, the old

Arthur Styan plays Joseph Quinney and
provides an excellent study of the rough,
homely and dogmatic Yorkshireman whom
the dramatist declares has the soul of a
connoisseur. The self-effacement, meekness
and devotion of Mrs. Quinney are disclosed
with positive brilliance by Miss Eily Malyon,
especially in the scenes with her forceful
husband. Miss Joy Gwyn is the physical
embodiment of the comely Posy, but her act-
ing merely amounts to reiterations, with a
relentless succession of pretty attitudes until
the bewildered critic almost howls for mercy.
