W. H. L. Cooper looks rather “correct” and
priggish as James Miggott, but, like Helton
Daniell as Cyrus Hunsaker, was not too sure
of himself on Saturday and will doubtless
improve in his work this week. Frank Keon
insists on the importance of Sam Tomlin’s
red nose in the scheme of things, and Miss
Llandley as Mabel Dredge affords a brief
glimpse of a lovely maiden’s unreciprocated
yearning. Gregan McMahon is only a cul-
tured intrusion in the last act, but he is as
effectively decorative as the bric-a-brac and
other priceless objects that figure with great
success in “The Sanctuary” of Act I.
The crowded state of Melbourne Princess’s
on Friday and Saturday last seemed proof
positive that the Gonsalez Opera Co. has
come to cater for a want and is judged to
be supplying the material. Of three operas
produced during the week “La Traviata”
scored the least success, although two of
the new artists were pleasing revelations.
Miss Gonsalez, as the heroine of the
“Camille” story, is a brighter and more
flexible soprano than the De Revers lady
who had the floor in “Trovatore.” She
couldn’t sing “Ah fors e lui,” like a Melba
or compare with many another brilliant
songstress of the concert platform, but her
warble was sufficiently sweet and her acting
of the part in all its stages, including the
death scene, was strong and convincing.
Miss Gonsalez shared the bun of approba-
tion with baritone Filippini, who figured as
the heavy father of the demi-mondaine’s
lover and voiced his sympathy with their
respective sadness in tender strains. Ilis
saccharine song to dear Alfredo moved the
audience to tumultuous encore. Alfredo the
tenor was another important first-appenr-
nncer—a tall, slim Dagradi in a cavalier
costume of cobalt blue velvet, the dressing
of this show being atrociously absurd. But
his clothes were much more beautiful than
his vocalisation. To Australian ears he is
a tenor only by courtesy. He has a nasal
snarl which is never in open con-
flict with the music, but is never tuneful.
I here are. several sorts of Italian tenors.
Dagradi is one. of the unsympathetic
sorts, with a saving grace of face, figure
and dramatic aptitude. It is, bv the way,
joyfully remarked of the Gonsalez Co. that
its three principal tenors are all tall and
good looking, and no member of the organi-
sation is conspicuously short and plump.
A thud tenor, who finished a good second
to Capelli in popularity, bore the brief and
easy name of Lois, and made a capital
arrival in “Cavalleria.” Lois is a mere
boy. in appearance, tall and debonair
equipped with a pleasant musical voice of
conventional Italian quality, and a natural
talent for acting. He was an interesting
young libertine in his long scene with De
Revers, who improved vastly on her first
night performance in “Trovatore.” She played
Santuzza convincingly; moreover, her tremo*
lous tones, taking kindly to Mascauii’s
music, revealed a little of the essentia? oil
of melody. This was almost lacking
in the singing of Miss Russ the
alleged contralto of the eo. Miss Iluss nre
sen ted a cute and saucy little Lola, wife of
the teamster Alfio, but her singing has
strong a tremolo that one note might as
well be another. The star of this “Caval
leria” performance, all things considered
was Scamuzzi, as Alfio—quite a different
cut of a teamster from all others that Mel
bourne had seen. A good, honest baritone
and a smart young man, Scamuzzi excels
as an actor. In “Pagliacci,” which followed
Mascagni’s opera, he played the clown
Tonio, who sings the prologue and subse
quently calls Canio’s attention to his wife’s
giddiness because she had declined to be
otherwise than quite proper towards him
Whilst Scamuzzi was great, not to say
immense, as the vindictive clown, a new
lyric soprano. Miss Visoni, was a delightful
surprise as Nedda. The little lady has a
smooth, sweet voice and a breezy, unaffected
style of expressing a round of emotions
Filippini again sang well, but acted coldly
as Selvio. The harlequin was well played’
and Dagradi’s vigorous histrionics in ‘the
Punchinello character made amends for his
failure as the tenor in “Traviata.” It was
in the double bill that the Gonsalez Co’s
stock made a rapid rise. The chorus— the
weakest part of the show thus far—did bet-
ter than in the preceding operas, and the
orchestra, which contains the best local
instrumentalists available, put in some fine
work.
* * * *
The dressing, scenery and swing of “Caval-
leria” and “Pagliacci” gave promise of ,1
fetching performance of “Lucia.” This duly
took place on Saturday. “Lucia” is ridicu-
lously Old-fashioned, nevertheless it will
always be beloved of Italian opera-goers
when the tenor is as soulfully musical as
Capelli, and the trillsome prima donna is as
satisfactory as Miss Gonsalez, and the famous
sestet in the second act gets a good all-round
rendering from principals, orchestra anil
chorus. Marlow’s theatre shook with
applause after the sestet item on Saturday
night, and the fourth act. which is mostly
tenor and tombstone, was beautifully sung
by Capelli. The new baritone, who came to
light in “Lucia,” had a commanding presence
and a lamentable tremolo.
* * * *
The Gonsalez order of production is more
merciful to first-nighters than was the Quin-
lan time-table. There are only two new
bills this week —“Un Ballo” and “Rigoletto”
(on Friday and Saturday). Next week four
of the operas will be repeated, and “La
Tosca” will be the only fresh production.
Julius Knight at Sydney Royal told the
almost-moribund “Royal Divorce” to take
up its decree absolute and march last Satur-
day. And the faithful old thing, hearing
the well-remembered voice, shook the dust
out of its Napoleonic uniforms, wiped the
cobwebs off its Moscow red lire, and
shouldered its musket. Julius falls naturally
into the traditional picture-poses and raps
out the familiar lines with fresh emphasis;
nevertheless there is a certain amount ol
weariness perceptible in his sound perform-
ance. Olive Wilton josephines appealingly
and is herself: but Eileen Sparks as Mane
Louise is Irene Browne to the last sneer.

(Grimaud) both register appearances, and

"A ROYAL DIVORCE.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Napoleon was a bilious bloke
Who “chucked” his steady girl;
lie wasn’t one to see a joke;
He had a forehead curl,
His funny coat was cut away
Like Hope at Waterloo;
They made it so, the tailors say,
To let his stomach through.
Ilis hat sat sideways on his head;
He gave his troops no rest,
But frowned and fought, till Hope was dead,
With one hand in his vest.
They had no movies then, you see,
To film a fighting lad;
But that Queer Man of Destiny
Went on as if they had.
A Hat, a Frown, a Funny Coat
That let a Stomach through—•
They ruled the world in times remote
And fell at Waterloo.
I marvel at the actor’s art,
And wonder if it’s right.
Is Julius Knight like Bonaparte?
Was Bonaparte like Knight?
N.S.W.

At Sydney Adelphi on Saturday Ben
Fuller introduced Wish Wynne, the “Some-
thing Doing” revue and a number of vaude-
ville turns. Wish Wynne’s character
studies of English life are delightful. Her
schoolgirls, country lass, slavey and ’Liza
are all genuine characters, each with a good
story to tell, and Miss Wynne interprets
them in convincing style and secures her
effects in a thoroughly natural way. In the
revue, “Something Doing,” there’s too much
doing that does not matter; and in several
places the show threatens to fall to pieces.
A subsequent series of one-minute dramas
was acceptable; and the gutta-percha
contortions of Ted Stagpoole were amaz-
ing. Charles Edenbury, who made half a
dozen attempts to light a cigarette with
imported matches whilst spinning on his
head, is advised to patronise the good local
product for the future. Harold Parkes
recited C. J. Dennis’s “Singing Soldiers,”
and Bailey’s clever posing dogs, an eccentric

Bloom of the Peach, gilt of the Ginger-bread,
Once rubbed and gone, they have for ever fled ;
And though Revival come with Pipe and Dance,
No “Girl” shall dance with color, being dead.
For who, rememb’ring nights in that Sweet Age,
When all was Peace, can look upon a stage,
Yet see no Sword of Darkness hanging o’er,
Nor trace red smears upon the music-page?
Ah, some who revelled in this whirling Dance,
Poor victims are of Time and Iron Chance ;
No drum can wake, no song can cheer them now,
In the far field where broke Young Honor’s lance.

Omar Fitz Gerald might have expressed
himself that way if he had been asked to
“do” the present revival of “The Quaker
Girl” at Her Majesty’s, Sydney. This pre-
war frivolity really revives very well, and
though Tanner’s “book” doesn’t wear as well
as Monckton’s lively music, it isn’t as dog-
eared as the usual document. Leslie
Holland, who is one of the few survivors of
the original co., plays his old lightweight
part of Tony Chute with surprising dash,
and bright-eyed Minnie Love dances through
the job of Phoebe. Ethel Cadman makes a
dainty but fragile Prudence, and Alice
Bennetto, as Princess Mathilde, is just about
as thin. Phil Smith gives another version
of himself in the sad clothes and haircut of
Jeremiah, the side-slipping Quaker; and
Addie Lennard smartly doubles the angry
French actress and the Quaker superioress.
Derek Hudson, in a conical French silk hat
and a super-shopwalker manner, lords it over
the stage and all its women for one number
as Prince Carlo, the Gay Dog of Paris.
Thanks to the power of advertisement,
No. 13 Soap is still being freely sold by
Hale Hamilton and Myrtle Tannehill at
Sydney Criterion.

Sydney films this week:—
Crystal Palace: “The Ne’er-Do-Well,’’ a first-
class dramatisation of Rex Beach’s popular novel. Its
nine reels fill the programme.

Duncan McClure and the Poor Parson
are still appealing to the great heart of the
multitude at Melbourne King’s. That the
appeal has also reached the multitude’s
pocket is disclosed by the managerial inti-
mation that the first week’s takings made
all concerned feel happy.
Clement May, the convincing imperso-
nator of Dickens characters, was an inter-
esting new arrival at Melbourne Bijou on
Saturday. Bert Coleman, a whistling ex-
pert, and George De Almo, a smart banjoist,
were also heard a first time; whilst other
additions included Ibex, a shadowgraphist;
Arthur Douglas, the Scawtch comedian ; and
Sylvia, a serpentine dancer who relies for
her success on lantern effects. Belle and
Betty, just out from England, are dancers
of a different kind, their efforts being con-
fined to energetic leg-show in sundry pleas-
ing changes of costume.
“The Blindness of Devotion,” “An Enemy
to Society,” and “The Rack” were the
heavyweight thrillers at Hoyt’s theatres,
Melbourne, on Saturday. The first-named,
featuring Robert Mantell and Genevieve
Hamper, was exclusively screened at the
Olympia address. Similarly “The Rack”
was reserved as a special treat for Hoyt’s
Bourke-street supporters. But “An Enemy
Society,” wherein Lois Meredith is a
■fetching apparition, also had a turn at
Olympia after finishing its run at head-
. quarters.

Another revival of “High Jinks” is put-
tin" in 32 nights and two mats, at Melbourne
Majesty’s. The management applies the
inopprobrious term “Frisky Frivolity” to
this gaudy story of the magic perfume and
the girls who didn’t mind. By any other
term it would smell as pungently. All the
High linkers go on a jag more or less, and
the purpose of the bottled essence which
plavs the name-part is to make everybody
feef that nothing matters. Two or three
notable changes in the cast make n 9 appre-
ciable difference to the result. Connie Ediss,
in place of Florence Vie, gives a rather fuller
expression to the gaieties of the long-giassed
widow who leads the intemperance movement
in the last act. Greig, lately the irreconcil-
able juryman in “On Trial,” is now the
plump husband from abroad who bumps
against his gladsome spouse' at a refreshment
table, and blithers awhile under the delusion
that he begat Dorothy Brunton before he
went forth into the wilderness. The rest of
the conspirators are Workman, with his little
bottle of joy-restorer, Field Fisher, Blanket,
Daisy Yates, Marie Eaton, Cecil Bradley,
Frith, etc., etc. The etceteras include the
chorus, and the chorus glorifies a multitude
of sins that are not hard to conceive.

The true inwardness of Premier Holman’s
“Croix d’lionneur” has not appeared. Here
it is. In the first dark days of the German
raid on Paris, when wireless communication
everywhere in these seas was interrupted,
and rumors of predatory Bosche fleets and
cruisers roaming the Pacific were rife, the
small French community in New Caledonia
was marooned from all sources of news.
Holman (who had not long before visited
Noumea and France) was then, as Premier
of N. S. Whies, receiving exclusive official
reports from the British Government of the
progress of the war, and, knowing the
plight of the small French outpost, caused
a daily budget of news to be cabled. The
anxious New Caledonians first heard the
glad tidings of .Toffre’s decisive victory on
the Marne, and the pursuit of Ivluek to the
Aisne, from the N.S.W. Premier, and natu-
rally associated him with their relief and
rejoicings. This and a long series of
friendly services to the French officers and
soldiers passing to and from New Caledonia,
and other courtesies less conspicuous,
together with a sound knowledge of French
as she is spoken and written, secured the
unique acknowledgment from PoincarA The.
“Croix d’honneur” has not hitherto been
bestowed Australiawards, the nearest having
been Tom Hughes’s decoration of “Officier
d’Academie.”
“Ex-eontrib.”: N. S. Wales Public Service
has become tangled in the recently-passed
Superannuation Act. The scheme does not
become operative all round until after peace
is proclaimed, but a rumor is on foot that
any public officer over 60 retired by the
P.B. Board, for other than an offence, is
entitled to a pension, although he has not
paid anything towards it. Officers over 60
who ask to be retired, it is agreed, get
nothing, but the Board is afraid that if it
forces an old hand to pass in his quill, against
his expressed desire, it will have to recom-
mend him for a pension. Quite a lot of pub-
lic servants over 60 are now clamoring to
be kept on, and hoping that they will be fired
out. Meantime, the Board is approving of
the retention of has-beens while in mortal
fear that they aren’t competent to look after
their jobs.
Dropped out at Castle Hill (N.S.W.),
the other day, at 84, Alexander Kethel, once
the biggest timber man in the Ma State.
For the best part of half a century he had
a fleet of small craft dodging in and out of
the tricky bar harbors on the N.S.W. North
Coast. He knew enough about the timber
industry to see that Australia was living
on its capital, without a thought for the
future, and he did his best to establish a
State Forestry Department and a systematic
reafforestation policy. Thirty years ago
he stirred up Henry Pai-kes to move in the
matter, the State nurseries at Gosford being
the result; but Governments have mostly
been too busy catching votes to bother about
posterity’s rights. Kethel was in the
Assembly for some years, going later to the
Council. In the elected House he was mem-
ber for the ’longshore constituency of West
Sydney, which Billy Hughes now repre-
sents.

“Masetto” : Max Vogrich, who died a week
ago ia New York, came to _ -Australia as
accompanist to the great violinist vSilnelmj
when that artist came this way in Hz. set-
tling in Sydney, he became conductor ot tlie
Sydney Liedertafel, and was the first to
wield the baton over the Sydney 1 hilhar-
monic. But he did not reign long, and was
succeeded by pianist Kowalsky. Alice, Rees,
who was making a reputation as a singer,
married Vogrich in 1884, and they soon after-
wards left for the U.S. The “Australian
Nightingale” survives him.
“II.O.”: Musical critics of the Melbourne
dailies protest against encores being given
in the Gonsalez productions of “II Trova-
tore,” at the Princess’s. I cannot appreci-
ate the objection. Surely operas like ‘ Iro-
vatore” and “Itigoletto,” with their set solos,
duets, trios and quartettes, specially invite
encores for numbers such as “La donna
e mobile”? In the Wagnerian operas
music and acting are closely allied,
but Verdi —the earlier Verdi —was never
above holding up the action to spin a
tune; and to suggest that the artistic unity
of “Trovatore” is ruined by the repetition of
these deliberately interpolated numbers is to
treat that melodious bellowdrama with
uncalled-for seriousness.
With the opening of the Majestic,
Adelaide theatres have completed then-
change of character. A few years back the
Royal had a monopoly of drama, and the
Tivoli of variety. Then the little King's
started competition in variety. Then
Mclntosh took the Tivoli name and pro-
gramme along to a big new house, which
had been meant to compete with the Royal
in drama. Now the Fuller circuit has moved
into a new Majestic on the old Tivoli site,
and shows twice a day, leaving the King’s
to try melodrama. The King’s would be a
good house for concerts or amateur shows,
but a new Queen’s Hall caters for them. The
Tivoli has a tendency to melodrama, with
special variety and pantomime programmes
occasionally. The Royal fills in the spaces
between drama cos.’ visits with pictures.
The Town Hall is available for anything
that doesn’t want scenery. And the Exhibi-
tion is used for big concerts and public
meeting •.
“Jean S.”: Hugh McCrae is to face the
biograph shortly as the impersonator of
Adam Lindsay Gordon in an Australian film
version of the poet’s life. As Mac. is related
to the deceased on his mother's side, blood
is expected to tell. The work, however, will
be necessarily trying, and will involve the
young man in at least 14 days hard in the
picture studio and in the country under the
supervision of producer W. J. Lincoln. Inci-
dentally Mac. will have to strike attitudes
in the saddle, but fortunately he has become
expert on the backs of plain mokes since he
turned his Pegasus out to grass.

“C. Ockney”: Quiller Couch’s first
attempt as a playwright, an adaptation from
his “Mayor of Troy,” was produced at
London Haymarket lately; but it hasn’t
caught on, and is to be succeeded by a play
by the all-pervasive Vachell, Couch’s bro-
ther-novelist. This will make Vachell’s
seventh produced play during the last two
years. His best-known piece, “Quinney’s,”
has just been seen at Gregan McMahon’s
Melbourne Playhouse, and it might be worth
some manager’s while to stage here his other
successes, “Searchlights” (done by H. B.
Irving in England) and “Jelf’s.”
The cry of a musician in Sydney D.T.: —
For Sale, Bass Viol, French, best model, cost
£25. Bargain, £9. Viol, P. 0., Newtown.

the heading, “Poultry, Birds and Dogs.”
At latest Marie Lohr was rehearsing the
leading role in “The Show Shop” at London
Apollo. She plays a girl whose people want
to disgust her of the stage. The conspira-
tors arrange her appearance in a play that
they regard as a certain frost. But it turns
out a huge success and she becomes a glit-
tering star in a single evening.

That the multitude sniff.

FOR THE AMATEUR ON THE LAND.
“I just love cows and things — it's a pity
farming's such hard work."
“How about bee-farming? They find their
own feed and do their own milking."
“Bloore” : Don’t believe all you hear about
the wonders of Anzac Day in London. As a
matter of fact, in the hands of this muddling
English War Office everything was sacrificed
to ostentatious display—everything, including
the feelings of Anzacs themselves. At the
service in Westminster Abbey there were
scores of men who had not the slightest claim
to be called “Anzacs.” That the London
Coster might see something for his fare on
The Tube, tall and beefy fellows were
selected from battalions which had never seen
the real thing. I’ll say nothing about myself,
but will mention the case of a cobber—a
trooper of the 12th Light Horse —who with
22 others had to stand outside the Abbey and
watch late arrivals from Egypt march into
the Abbey. Although this trooper put in
several months of .strenuous work on the
Peninsula, and although liis regiment was
practically wiped out of existence, he, with
the other seasoned veterans, had to “do a
perish” on the kerbstone. This trooper is
about sft. 6in., can ride the tail off anything
that is horse, and is as game as they make
’em; but in spite of his Peninsula record he
was turned down. He wasn’t big enough for
this bit of theatricalism. I’m glad to say it
didn’t end that way, though. Birdwood came
along and happened to spot them amongst the
spectators. He inquired what they were doing
there. Being informed that there was
“nothin’ doin’ for little blokes inside the old
tabernacle,” he exploded three times and then
promptly arranged for the admission of the
turned-down 20, all of them dyed-in-the-wool
Anzacs.
* * * *
This comes from a Dutchman, formerly
resident in Sydney : —•
In Odessa I heard of a Russian who was in Sydney
when the war broke out. He used to be an officer
in the Russian Navy and served on the Baltic Squad-
ron that was smashed up in the battle of Tshsima
12 years ago. At the end of the Japanese war he got
into trouble with his Government ar.d thought it wise
to clear out to Australia. After a most adventurous
life he at last managed to get a position in a lawyer’s
office and worked himself up nicely. When the present
war broke out he at once decided to return to
Russia and offer his services to the Government.
As he expected, he was court-martialled; but instead
of being pardoned, as he hoped, he was condemned
to be exiled to Siberia. However, his friends suc-
ceeded in getting him permission to go to the Front
as a private. Within five months he had by his
desperate bravery got all four classes of the St.
George Cross (which is the Russian V.C.), and this
entitled him to be promoted again to officer’s rank.
He was then transferred back to the Navy, and when
I heaid of him he was commander of a ship lying
in port. As it happened, he was to travel by the
same train as I, so we went together to Petrograd,
where he was to meet his wife, a Sydney girl who
had just arrived via Siberia. They call him “the
Australian.”
That part of the extraordinary story which
so modest a man would tell, the Russian had
already told to Ernest Laurence, the Sydney
solicitor who had accidentally come across
him and engaged him as a confidential clerk
.in the firm's office. Prior to that he had done
many things in a little while; among his
other jobs he had been cook in a-city cake-
shop.
The parson who flings reckless statements
broadcast has been a nuisance for a long
time, and one of him has at last got a little
of what was coming to
him. While the tight
between Cold Tea and
Bung wps raging prior
to the Ma State referen-
dum, W. J. Ritchie, of
Newcastle (N.S.W.), was
holding forth from a bal-
cony in the six o’clock
interest. lie claimed that
the eight o’clock closing
which Pearce had en-
forced under the War
Precautions Act had
been a great improve-
ment, and, pointing to a
John Hop on the out-
skirts of the crowd, said :
“He can prove it.” A
local Bung interjected,
“The police are for nine
o'clock, not for six.”
“No wonder,” retorted
Ritchie. “Many a pot
they get from publicans
on the nod.” Challenged
to prove it, he added:
“It’s right. I’ve seen it.”
But when John Hop gave
him a chance to say
where he had seen it he
did the usual crayfish act
and pleaded that his
remark was a jocular
repartee to an interjection, and was not
meant seriously or used with offensive
intent. A dull beak couldn’t see the joke,
and said it was worth a pound, anyway,
with the usual costs, or 24 hours.
“Dalriada”: Old-timers grew reminiscent
over a burial in Williamstown’s quaint
cemetery the other day. The inmate of the
grave was Denis Mangan, once carpenter on
the Victoria, Australia’s first warship, which
took a rescue party to the Gulf of Carpen-
taria in search of the Burke and Wills ex-
pedition. Decadent moderns wonder whether
it was intended to reach the headwaters of
Cooper’s Creek. The Victoria did some use-
ful work in her time, and some of her crew

smelt powder. A sloop-of-war commissioned
in the early ’6o’s, her first commander was
Captain H. Norman, father of C. E. Nor-
man, chairman of Vic. Railway Commission-
ers, who was among the mourners at Sian-
gan’s funeral. The Victoria’s officers and
crew wore uniforms like those of the British
navy, except that silver lace replaced gold
on the jackets of the after-guard. The ship’s
first active duty carried her to Maoriland
during the war. Some of her crew took part
in the capture of several of Henare’s numer-
ous pahs. Having returned to Victoria the
little sloop compelled the Confederate cruiser
Shenandoah, then refitting at Williamstown,
to discharge certain hands illegally engaged.
Then peacefully she remained in the service
until the early ’Bo's, and was broken up a
few years later. But Mangan. the carpen-
ter, stayed on and was attached to the Vic.
naval forces for 34 years.

At Melbourne Stock Exchange—of all
places!—the war work of the Y.M.C.A. re-
ceived a boost last week. The brokers made
a little demonstration in honor of Lieut.
Throssell, Y.C., and that young khakied
hero from Westralia slipped in a good word
for the Y.M.C.A. service “abroad” and in
London. It is characteristic of the Victoria
Cross men in general that they seem always
to be thinking less of what they did than of
what was done for them.

Alf Farthing, ex-pedagogue, now publican
and M.L.A. for Yeast Melbourne, has lost
his £5OOO “rum, Rome and rascality” slander-
action against parson Worrall, the jury hav-
ing returned a majority verdict (nine to
three) in favor of the raucous Henry. Right-
eous George Maxwell and a pair of lesser
Bar-lights stated the case for Farthing, while
Irvine, K.C., made one of three expensive
pleaders for Worrall, so it was a glorious
victory for Costs. Now, unless Farthing has
changed his mind since making a public state-
ment as to his volunteering intentions, the
way will be clear for him to go and drown
his soi-rowful recollections of the case in the
roar of battle.

“F.N.T.”: Edward Pescott, principal of
the Burnley (Vic.) school of horticulture,
sees this year the unique result of several
seasons’ wattle-culture. He is now able to
go out into the garden on any day of the
year and pick a bunch of some kind of
wattle-blossom —a happy consummation of
his gentle labors, even though a pawnbroker
may have his own ideas as to the value
thereof. Pescott. by the way, is one of the
few officials of the Agricultural Department
whose branch of nature-study never seems
to come in for hostile criticism, from press
or Parliament. The Burnley school is a quiet
spot, even on the busiest working day. Only
the hum of bees is heard, the occasional
cackling of the poultry-competition, the thud
of an apple falling from the bough, the
scrunch of the spade as the earnest pupil
turns the sod in seai'ch of wisdom, and the
faint moan of the female student attempting
to avoid the woolly caterpillar. But Pescott
is an energiser all right, and looks it; which
may partly account for the fact that the
critics leave him alone, or even go the
length of saying a kind word as they rush on,
knife out, to cut up the Research Farm or
hand one in the neck to Dookie.
* * * *
As a lawyer sees it: —
Let me protest against the suggestion (B. 25/5,/’l6)
that “the Law has a stupid aversion to commas and
other marks of punctuation.’’ The aversion exists,
all right; but it isn’t stupid. When you realise
how little is understood about punctuation by many
quite intelligent men—printers included —you see
that it would be absurd to let an important matter
turn on the little mark that one lawyer may put
in and another leave out. Experience has proved
that one asks for trouble in placing any real reliance
on commas. They may be printed in a clause of
an Act for the sake of convenience in reading it;
but they are not a vital part of it any more than
the marginal notes, and a clear-headed draftsman
should have no difficulty in so using words that
their meaning is clear if all the punctuation is
swept away.

THE COMING OFFENSIVE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Some of the best authorities believe that we are
on the eve of great events. —Daily paper.
Scarred is the earth and red the battle flame,
The thunder of the guns rolls down the wind.
The master horror that no thought can tame
Rages among the nations stark and blind.
This is the lull of war and all men wait
The wilder hour of vengeance and of fate.
Those guns, forever loud with hymns of death,
Are but the treble music of the score.
The bass is yet to come, its organ breath
Blent in one awful and destroying roar,
When levelled trench and earthquake-shaken hill
Shall own in fear the strong avenger’s will.
We wait afar; but there in long array
The marshalled hosts chafe in the leash of pride
And count the hours towards that tremendous day
When ruin, armed by truth, stands deified
And headlong valor, eager for its place,
Joins with hot steps the mad triumphant race.
Keep still, unquiet heart. The anthem grows;
The awful sky is roofed with flying steel;
Fear its confusion heaps upon our foes.
At last, at last the Teuton stalwarts reel,
The word is spoken and the gaps of doom
With the fierce rush of long-pent manhood bloom.
Onward! The neighing warhorse, free at last,
Leaps with loose bridle to the trumpet’s bray.
The sabres gleam and war’s full human blast
Blows like a tempest through the stricken day.
Above the voices of the guns the cheer
Of faith exultant echoes long and clear.
And somewhere, girt with more than mortal dread,
A cold and fretful fury in each heart,
World-traitors sit with bowed, averted head
And helpless hands. Chill echoes round them start
And dreadful whispers run about the walls
As iron retribution nearer calls.
They staked their chance upon an iron shard—-
The loaded dice of battle long prepared—
ith subtle falsehood the fierce ga; le they marred
And, vaunting shame, the world’s long honor dared.
“Lo, war is war!” they cried; and war full-grown
Stalks through the wreck of all that was their own.
From east and west the wings of battle roll
Tumultuous floods of fire and leagues of men.
The last defence is broken and the soul
Of treason clings for rescue to a pen....
Shall these things be? The sand runs down the glass,
Time holds with truth and the swift moments pass.
X.SAV.

From behind a mudheap in Mesopotamia:
The campaign here is plain gehenna. It is not
si ipuch the enemy as the country that makes our
progress so deadly and so slow. The Turks’ simple
tactics are to fortify a short line of defence resting on
a river on one hand and a huge marsh on the other,
entrench themselves several lines deep and wait to
b? attacked. The whole country is without undula-
tions, deep in mud, not a tree to be seen, and there
is practically no drinkable water away from the
rivers. We have to do all the assaulting, and as
the Turkish trenches on this flat ground are very
difficult to see, especially when the other difficulties
are helped by a Satanic mirage, we can't get as
much help from the distant fire of our guns as we
should anywhere else, and heavy losses are the
unavoidable result.
At the end bf 190 G the number of
trade-unionists in Australia was estimated
at 175,529; Knibbs reckons that in 1915 the
number had arisen to 528,031. It is rather
surprising to learn that when every occupa-
tion seems to possess a union only slightly
more than 52 per cent, of the total number
of male employees in the Commonwealth are
affiliated. Still, it moves. The N. S. Wales
Industrial Court is pondering over an appli-
cation by the Professional Officers’ Associa-
tion, comprising barristers, solicitors, archi-
tects, engineers and surveyors employed in
Government or municipal work, for the con-
stitution of a Wages Board. Justice Ileydon,
in a puzzled manner, said that if. the Board
was granted he might be pursued with simi-
lar demands from under-secretaries, the
judiciary and members of Parliament. After
that lot would presumably come the Union
of Overworked Governors and the Amalga-
mated Society of Searchers for Lost Money.
* * * *
Ballarat, which generally sports two
bishops, has borne up nobly without any for
the best part of a year. Dr. Higgins, the
lI.C. prelate, died last September; and Dr.
Green, who wore the Anglican gaiters, gave
up bishing about the same time and settled
down near Melbourne. The It. C’s have been
the first to fill the vacancy, and have not
had to go outside Victoria to do it. Father
Daniel Foley, of Terang (Vic.), who has
been appointed, was until lately parish priest
at Creswick, in the diocese which he now
goes to boss. He is a cousin of Dr. Mannix,
Coadjutor-Archbishop of Melbourne. Mean-
while the Anglicans have found no one fit
to wear Dr. Green’s discarded apron. A
committee elected by the local Synod lias
the right to appoint; but the western Vic.
squatter-members want to hand the
.appointment over to the Archbishop of
Canterbury, on the grounds (1) that
they themselves are too wretchedly
ignorant to carry out the job of
choosing a boss shepherd of their own dio-
cese, and (2) that the special needs of Bal-
larat would naturally be better known in
England. Other members of the committee,
however, reckon that it is up to Ballarat to
drop the importing craze, and the denomina-
tional paper calls the deadlock "a scandal.”

“Chasuble” : The World’s Catholic Euch-
aristic Annual Congress would have been
held in Sydney this year but for the war.
When the guns stop the Pope will give the
order and make the Yellow Pup howl. The
next location after Sydney is Lima, Peru.
Sydney will see a cardinal from Rome, with
prelates from everywhere. The most notable
of recent congresses have been those of
London, Montreal, Cologne, Malta, Madrid
and Lourdes. An Australian cardinal is sure
to be made at the local outing.
“Brain” : The exploits of the officer who
did some shooting in Dublin and was re-
cently adjudged insane, recalls the gentle-
man with bats in his loft who stirred up
Launceston in the early days. Major Gordon,
who arrived there in 1810, became affected by
sunstroke, and it so happened that at the
very same time a person named McHugo, who
had just arrived as owner and supercargo of
a vessel, also became deranged. On land-
ing from his ship he represented him-
self to be a, military officer of
noble birth, 'and assumed the
title of General Count McHugo.
lie said he had been ordered
by the Government of India to
inquire into and, if necessary,
redress the grievances of the
soldiery of Tasmania —of which
he had probably heard some
rumors elsewhere. He deluded
not only civilians, but also the
military who composed the
garrison, and was everywhere
received with the respect due
to his exalted rank. The mad-
man played his part
so jwell that the com-
mand of the garrison
was actually surren-
dered to him for about
a w r eek. Placing Major
Gordon under re-
straint, he instituted
an inquiry into the
conduct of the prisoner
as regards the hard-
ships which the men
alleged they had ex-
perienced at his hands.
McHugo actually sen-
tenced the Major to
be hanged ; and hanged
he would have been,
sure enough, but for
the timely return of a young subaltern named
Lyttleton, who had been away on leave. The
“Count” was sent back to his ship, and
had sense enough to skip as soon as possible.
“Gustavus Gravelpit”: Dr. Robertson, the
chairman of the Melbourne Board of Health,
deserves a pat on his frock-coated hack for
some recent remarks pertaining to second-
hand clothes. As most of us, if the war
keeps on much longer, will be rushing to the
old-clo’ emporium for our wearing apparel,
his words are timely. Says he: “I think the
Board should have power to compel the
fumigation of second-hand clothes before they
pass from a dealer to a custupier. . . .All
clothing in stock should be fumigated before
being exposed for sale.” This is so eminently
sensible I wonder it has not been insisted
on before. But if the Russell-street Israel-
ites, who do most of the Melbourne trade in
this class of goods, don’t raise their prices a
hundred per cent, for their new line of “fumi-
gated suits,” I'm no judge of the race.
The Separation Allowance Branch of John
Bull’s War Office gets some quaint corres-
pondence these times. One of the men who
has to deal with it passes these samples on
to a Maoriland friend :
Please Sir,—l have received your letter, and have
to say that lam his grandfather. He was born and
brought up in this house in answer to your letter.
Sir, —I am writing these few lines for Mrs. Morgan
who cannot write herself. She is expecting to be
confined and can do with it.
Dear Sir, —I see you have changed my little boy
into a little girl. Will it make any difference?
But the commonest little note of all is an
intimation that Mrs. Atkins is “expecting,”
and what has she got to do next?

“Mujik”: Victorian Labor-leader Elmslie
remarks, apropos of the Melbourne bakery
riot: “Day was created for man to work and
night for him to enjoy himself and rest.” I
don’t know whether Elmslie is in the confi-
dence of Providence, but if he isn’t his
remark is sententious nonsense. Where is
THE LESSER EVIL.
HE: “What shall we do? Talk or dance?"
SHE: "I'm awfully tired. Let’s dance.”
the evidence to support him? Toets, phil-
osophers, bookmakers and Chidley all agree
that the day is for the enjoyment of man.
They tell us to get out in the open, to enjoy
the sunlight, go to the races and the surf
and work in the garden —to revel in the
sunlight, the breeze and the play of shadow
on the distant hill. Elmslie is merely a dull
Conservative. This chaining of people to
desks and workshops and kitchens while the
climate calls to them “Come Out!” is an
atrocity, perpetrated in the interests of
Greed. No good, lasting work was ever done
in the daytime and never will be, except by
surgeons, and they get busv as early as they
can. Will somebody less stodgy than Elms-
lie please summon the workers to arise and
awake and work through the wretched period
of blackness and turn the hours of sunshine
into hours of rejoicing?
TRUE TILL HEALTH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Sweetheart, if I were only sure
That you had been as true
These last two months or more to me
As I have been to you,
A happiness profound, divine,
Also blank wonder, would be mine.
For eight long weeks no Gladsome Eye
Has by your slave been aimed
At any girl. No maiden’s hand
Has by his hand been claimed.
No foot has known the tender touch
Of his. Can you, dear, say as much ?
Even in fancy I have been
As innocently good.
For weeks I thought of nothing much,
And then I thought of Food—
Of toast and fresh-made scones and cakes,
And pork and beans, and chops and steaks.
For quite a month the kitchen held
My chastened mind in chains.
I lay and yearned for ham and eggs,
And sausages and brains.
Would that your thoughts were so employed I
The news would leave me overjoyed.
But now we may expect a change:
My typhoid bout has fled,
My strength is coming back again,
And I am out of bed.
Later there may be more to tell —-
There mostly is when one is well.
K.s.w.

Dubbo, is entered
in that oldest-liv’
ing- Australian com-
petition. She was
born at Liverpool
(N.S.W.) in Janu-
ary, 1810, and in-
habited Emu Plains
till she moved to
1 hibbo 40 . years
ago. Seven children
are living, and
when the old lady

she had 26 grand-

“Feng - Shui” :
East is East, and
not all you can do
will shift it north
or south. Yuan
Shi-Ivai, greatest
man in China, saw
his end co m i ng.
European doctors
had been in attend-
ance : all of them
he dismissed and

and frogs’ elbows
diluted with im-

SENATOR
BLAKEY
(Vic.).
“D.F.”: If there is any family that has
done more enlisting than that of Private G.
Mason, of Hamilton (Vic.). I’d like to hear
of it. He is the 14th of them to get into
uniform ; 10 of his brothers are already at
the 1 ront, or thereabouts, and three sisters
are nurses. He knows something about the
Huns, for his mother and one sister were
killed in the first Zepp. raid on London.
E. B. Loughran, retiring chief of Vic-
torian Hansard staff, has had 37 years in
the gallery. Before that he was on the
Argus. He has subsided from his'whirling
job into a seaside nook at Brighton, where
he will follow up his triumphs as a State
chess-champion or attempt to better his poetic
prints, “ ’Neath Austral Skies” (1894) and
“The Ivory Gate” (1904). Captain Gerald
Loughran, of the A.A.M.C., concerning
whom many admiring tales have been told
by Anzacs, is his son. As for the vacant
Hansard chieftaincy, Amos Burr has
been nominated by the Parliamentary library
committee, whose job it is to attend to the
matter.
THE PRICE.
LITTLE GIRL: " And was THEM all the money
they gave you for fighting ?”
F. W. Lang, Speaker of the M.L. House
of Reps., who has been figuratively smacked
with the flat of the royal sword and made Sir
Freddy, is an easy-going old farmer when
he isn’t swathed in the aweful robes of office.
Between sessions he used to boss ploughmen
and dairymaids on a fat little farm at
Tuhikaramea, in the Waikato, which he had
owned since the early ’7o’s; and there he
cultivated the gift of holding his tongue., He
is. short and spry and grey, and has some-
thing of the air of a military veteran. A
naval brother captained H.M.S. Tauranga
some years ago, and would have been an
admiral long before this had he not been
drowned on the China station.
Died last week old “Jimmy” Mirams, who
was a well-beloved subject for Melbourne
cartoonists 20, 30, 40 years ago. During the
14 years or so that the little man was an
M.L.A. he figured in the pictures as a rather
untidy wowser, armed with a fat umbrella;
but in his land-boom time of prosperity he
was a carefully tailored little chap whose
brolly defied criticism. That was the time
when the whole tribe of Mirams drove to
church on Sunday in clattering equipages,
and the horses were stabled at the back of
the tabernacle. In his political capacity he
talked so freely of finance that Melbourne
partly accepted him as a born man of busi-
ness with a genius for figures, whereas he
was really a professional Recliabite who had
graduated in the bookselling trade. His re-
putation was his ruin in middle age. On
the strength of it he ran the Premier Per-
manent Building Association, and as the
money came 1 pouring in Mirams poured it
out in rash land speculations and jerry-
built villas. He formed the original Federal
Coffee Palace Co., which was another bad
spec. Finally his supposititious genius for
finance landed him in the Criminal Court.
He was the exploded boomster who was
made a shocking example of. He got a year’s
imprisonment for unlawful hanky-panky that
was venial in comparison with the frauds
ot bigger rogues who escaped punishment.
Of late years he cottoned on to the Labor
party, and wrote a reminiscence now and
then for The Bulletin; but, taking one
consideration with another, the Labor party
showed good sense in not resurrecting him
for a Parliamentary job.

merly editor of the Argus, 71 on 2nd; Fed-
eral Auditor-General J. W. Israel, 66 on
4th; pianist Percy Grainger, 34, and Dr.

dens, 76, on Sth; Dr. Geo. Syme (Melb.),
57, and John Perry (ex-Minister of N.S.W.),
71, on 13th ; S. Aus. Premier Vaughan, 42 on
14th; Thos. Tait (formerly Chief Commis-
sioner of Vic. Railways), 52 on 24th ; Carty
Salmon, M.H.R., 55 on 29th ; Colonel “Bill”
Williams, of Sydney, recently knighted, 60
on 30th; E. F. Pittman, late N.S.W. Under-
Sec. for Mines, 67 on 31st.
Captain Joseph S.
Crane, of the William
Mitchell, now in Hobart
port, believes he is the
last left alive of the old
school of skippers who
used to race in the China
tea trade. Fifty years
ago, on the clipper ship
Belle of South Esk, they
were becalmed east of
Sumatra, when three
pirate junks came at
them. The Belle carried a
cannon or two, and got in
a broadside which nearly
knocked the leading junk
endwise, on which the
whole three decided that
they had business else-
where. Another cher-
ished recollection of the
old man’s is the cuteness
of a Yankee captain who
drifted into a Chinese
port with a fairly slow
old tub in the days when
the fastest sailing ships
in the world used to race
home with the first tea
of the season. The Down-
Easter hunted out the
captain of the fastest
English ship in port and
challenged him to race
home for a stake of
£5OO. The Yank lost the
bet by a month or so, but
he came out ahead on the
deal, for the merchants
who heard of his bet con-
cluded that his hooker
must be something extra-
swift and rushed him
with about £2OOO more
than he would otherwise
in freight money
have got.
In its Anglicised pronunciation the name
of the new Lord High Admiral of the Ger-
man fleet sounds exceedingly suitable. Von
Koester!
Through continued ill-health, Jack Lem-
mon, Labor M.L.A. for Williamstown (Vic.),
has had to forego most of his activities. The
~ one that causes him most regret is his tech-
nical-school hobby. After years of agitating,
m and out of Parliament, for junior “techs.,”
Lemmon has seen his ambitions realised in
a fairly substantial manner. For years
he had been a member of the council of the
central technical institute for juniors, but
has at last been obliged to give even that up.
Maoriland Judge Chapman’s Dunedin-
bred capacity for avoiding a smile stood a
good test at the trial of Itua in Auckland.
juror who had been called three times
and fined the usual fiver turned up late in a
bedraggled condition and explained that he
had stepped into the harbor on his way down
to the court, and was now too wet to give
his mind to a juror’s job. Chapman
solemnly considered the explanation, passed
it as satisfactory and scratched the fiver.
There are some hearts of oak in the pine
forests of the Tasmanian West Coast. A
pine-cutter named William Finn slashed his
toot open with an axe away up the Gordon
Kiver the other clay. Ilis brother Jim and a
mate left camp with him in a rowing-boat
at 11 o’clock at night, and pulled all the
way to Strahan, a matter of 45 miles, doing
the trip m exactly 12 hours.
Two Westralian priests were in the Sus-
sex when she was recently torpedoed in the
English Channel. Father B. Briody and
Father D. O’Donnell, the former of
Albany and the latter of Northam,
intending to return to Australia by the
Osterley, were on the way to catch the boat
at Naples They left Folkestone in the
Sussex, with 400 other people, of whom 50
were lost. The two Australian priests man-
aged to get to Boulogne and thence back
to England. They say that when the Sus-
sex was torpedoed she was lifted 6ft. clean
out of the. water.

Josiah Bothby, who recently passed out
at Adelaide, aged 79, was a son of that Judge
Boothby who was “amoved” from the Bench
40 years ago. Another member of the familv
was the late W.R., who was author of the
Australian system of voting by ballot with a
cross within a square. The Boothbys were
from Nottingham (Eng.), where they knew
the distinguished Ilowitt family. A sister of
Josiah became the wife of Dr. A. W. Ilowitt
the explorer who went to Cooper’s Creek in’
1862 to tell Burke and Wills that thev had
lost their way.
“Billy Blue”: “Jolly Jim Conroy,” the
last of Cobb and Co.’s coachmen, threw un
the reins at the age of 80 the other day it
Tattersall’s Hotel, Sydney, where he his
lived for many years and was generally
known as “The Astronomer.” Conrov was
born in' England, but came to Australia when
a child. He first drove a coach from Nellisen
to Braid wood (N.S.W.) in 1858. In l8b()
he swung the mail on the Southern-road for
Crane and Roberts, having the stage from
Campbelltown to Bargo, and was so engaged
when Cobb and Co. bought the string of
coaches. Conroy kept ahead of the railway
till he ran the stage to Goulburn. When the
rail reached that town he swapped the box
for the mail van, and for 30 years was in
charge of it between Sydney and the west-
ern town. The old man’s handsomest boast
was that the length of his coach iournevs
was over 100,000 miles. y
John Shirlow, Australia’s leading etcher
has reaped the reward of years of industry
in the purchase by the Mitchell Library of
14 of his prints of Melbourne old and new
The library trustees have asked in addition
for a full set of his work. In private life
John is an officer of the- electric supply
department of Melbourne City Council. Born
in Sunbury in 1869, Shirlow as a youth
sketched anything and everything—even the
dreadful plaster statues in the Fitzroy Gar-
dens. As he had to be at work at' 8 he
was out and about by 4.30 each morning,
and once got in the line of fire when an
angry Chink pulled his gun on a flying
countryman. The arrival in Australia of
the Whistler and Seymour-Haden prints in
1891 made an etcher of the draughtsman.
He built his own press of Australian red-
gum, producing also etching grounds and a
mezzotint rocker, and in 1904 published the
first portfolio of etchings issued in Aus-
tralia.
Leighton S. Bracegirdle, who can add
D.S.O. if he likes now, is a son of
Captain F. Bracegirdle, one of Sydney’s
ancient mariners. Young Bracegirdle was
a clerk in an insurance office 20 years ago
when he joined N.S.W. Naval Brigade as a
cadet. He went to
China during the
Boxer rebellion in
1900 with a contin-
gent from the Bri-
gade, which saw no
fighting, but brought
back a few cases of
curios from Pekin.
Later, he took a
hand in the S’Afri-
can trouble. In 1902.
when the Federal
Parliament started
to economise in
naval matters,
Bracegirdle, being
only a midshipman,
escaped slaughter
and later on was
made naval officer at
Newcastle. He left
Australia with a
naval bridging train
last year, and had
charge of the making
of a pontoon for the
British landing at
Suvla Bay, where he
was wounded.

HIGGS.
One of the three oldest Cents One on any
roll in Australia, Alfred Charles Quaife, of
Stanmore, died in his 80th year recently. He
was admitted in 1862, and continued in
practice till about 10 years ago.
A DREAM OF BEAR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Russian advance continues.—Press item.
The tyrant slept. Far off the cannon spoke
Their thunderous challenge to the tireless foe.
The chiefs of war with stroke and counterstroke
Tossed maddened leagues of manhood to and fro.
Wide landscapes leaped in flame, but still he slept,
Regardless still of tortures, deaths and prayers,
Till through the silence of his dreams there crept
Those awful bears.
He dreamt that in a happy summer green
He wandered through a meadow lush with grass.
The warm sun on the hills appeared to lean, ,
The little winds with music seemed to pass.
’Twas joy a while to leave the world behind,
To put aside his crown and all its cares;
Blithely he whistled till he turned to find
Bears and more bears.
Kaiser he seemed of mighty realms and proud,
Of battle hosts drawn up in long review.
The legions of his dream appeared to crowd
The whole wide earth, and far his ensigns flew,
Flaunting his glory in the eyes of men.
His feet were on ambition’s topmost stairs —
King of the living world he stood....and then
He saw those bears!
Loping along the dreamscape of his sleep,
Brown, shaggy, purposeful, he saw them come.
Hard-breathing, waiting for the rush and leap,
He watched them with dry lips that fear made
dumb.
Bears and more bears. The white fangs shone and
gleamed.
Bears in the rear to catch him unaware;
Bears on the front, the flank—the whole world
seemed
One endless bear!

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Commonwealth Treasurer Higgs and his
officers have devised a scheme o£ extended
instalments in connection with the third war
loan, and the assistance of employers is
sought in carrying it into effect. It is sug-
gested that almost every employee could
afford to lend his country at least £lO with
which to prosecute the war. more particu-
larly as the greater part of this money comes
back to the subscriber in one way or another.
This £lO needn’t be all paid at once. Five shil-
lings a week for a period of 40 weeks, com-
mencing on August 1, will entitle the sub-
scriber to a full 12 months’ interest at 41
p.c. p.a. payable on June 80, 1917, and in-
terest at ihe same rate each succeeding half-
year to the date of the redemption of the
loan in 1925. In other words, the Govern-
ment actually offers the working man sub-
scriber usurer’s interest for his money for
the first year, and thereafter 41 p.c., the full
amount of principal being repayable in less
than nine years.
This bit of 41 p.c. interest is likely to be
very handy before many years are past. It
is almost inevitable that, after perhaps a
first outburst of joy, tliei'e will be a long
spell of gloom when the war is over, and we
have to pay the money-bill. HiggA.puts the
matter very well indeed :— ■
Millionaires become millionaires mostly because
the vast majority of people never think of “the
rainy day.” “The rainy day” may come when the
war is over and two or three hundred thousand
Australian soldiers have to find employment. The
stoppage of war-time industries may cause a period
of trade add commercial depression. I do not say
there will be a depression, but if history is to
repeat itself, as there have been depressions after
every war, it is reasonable to expect a depression
after the present war.
AVfege-earners will be wise if they save some of
the money which is now so freely circulating awing
to heavy local expenditure for war purposes.
But, apart altogether from these latter considera-
tions, it is the paramount duty of all who cannot
yo to the Front to furnish the money to maintain
the gallant men who do go to the war, as ivell as
to support their ivives, families or other depend-
an ts.
As for the general subscriber, it can’t be
too seriously urged upon him that the money
simply has to bfi raised. If it is not
got jit Australia' it must be secured somehow
and on some, terms from John Bull, who is
not only struggling with his own vast respon-
sibilities, but is also helping to finance Rus-
sia, France, Belgium and Servia. Apart
from the fact that it is incredibly mean to
“tap the old man” when the “old man” has
such vast calls upon him, it will be far better
for ourselves in the long run to provide our
own war funds. If we now borrow from
abroad, we must later on pay interest abroad ;
that is to say, we must ship our products
away, "and get nothing for them. The worst
danger ahead of us is that the market value
of our products is likely to fall very seri-
ously. Only during the "past week we have
seen how metals toppled when it began to
look as if the Russians had got their steam-
roller going again. Already we have incur-
red a tremendous .foreign debt; and if our
products slump we shall have to ship very
much increased quantities to pay interest on
those old liabilities. If we borrow abroad
for war purposes at the present very high
rate of interest, we shall have to ship away
so much more products that our own posi-
tion at home may be deplorable. Especially
so long as this very high rate of interest
prevails, the only prudent thing to do is to
borrow from outside not one penny that we
can possibly raise at home in Australia and
Maori land.
And it ought to be as easy to float this
third loan as it was to float either of its
predecessors. For one thing, about three-
fourths of the previous loans have been spent
in Australia, so that three-fourths of the
money previously subscribed is available for
the new loan. As for the rest, we have
been getting tremendous prices for the wool
and the meat we have sold outside. That
Ave have been paying tremendous prices for
the aa’ool and the meat we lia\ 7 e been buying
from ourselves for ourselves doesn’t affect
the position in the smallest degree, because
it has simply meant a transfer from one Aus-
tralian pocket to another.
* * *
There are a few hard nuts vet to be
cracked in the War-time Profits Bill. New
mining cos. have shaken down one of them.
To ascertain what is a war-time profit in
such cases the Bill says the tax-collector is
to first allow 6 p.c. ou the capital and then
ask for half of what is left. That is a
vicious system, because the more heavily
a promoter has over-capitalised his con-
cern the better oft' he will be. In other
words, it offers an advantage to those people
who did the very thing that the Treasurer
is stopping people from doing now. Of
course that will be altered. What is going
to take its place this paper doesn’t know ;
but the taxable amount ought plainly to be
governed, in the case of mines, not by any
consideration of capital at all, but by the
market value of the metal sold. Take the case
of gold. Though the bill is called a “war-
time” profits bill, the thing really aimed at
is to get hold of war profits: the “war-time”
is used simply to avoid having to prove that
a profit is a war profit. Now there can’t
possibly be such a thing as a war profit
made out of a gold-mine. On the contrary,
gold is worth less. to the producer than it
was before the war started. This is proved
by the fact that various cos. in Westralia
are seriously thinking of closing down for
a while because the increase in the price of
commodities (including labor) has made
their operations unprofitable.
A somewhat similar case is that of the
life insurance office. What is called the
profit of a life office may easily be larger
now than it was three or four or five years
ago. But if the surplus is larger it will
simply be because of the larger numlt/fj of
policy-holders. So far as war profits are
concerned it is almost inconceivable that
there could be any. The A,.M.P. the other
day reported that for the past year
the claims (Ordinary Department) were
£1,390,751, of which no less than £343,053
arose directly from the war. The claims
were' almost all in respect of young or com-
paratively young men from whom in normal
circumstances the society might have expected
something more than the average number of
premiums. The direct loss is therefore
exceptionally heavy. On the other side, cer-
tainly, there is the somewhat higher average
interest earnings; but that is a small matter
compared with the other.

Though the Tasmanian National has been
met easing its advances its holding of coin
and liquid iissets is very fair —about 41 p.c.
of all its liabilities. Still, that proportion
isn t so vood as it looks, because this bank
has an exceptional amount of free deposits—
money, presumably, held at call. While,
however, it is not in the strongest position
in the world to meet a sudden strain, its
creditors have quite unusual security. It
owes altogether £767,611. and has £313,678
liquid and semi-liquid. That leaves £453,933
to be represented by non-liquid assets; and
their book value is no less than £740,350.
That ought to leave enough margin to satisfy
anybody; and that, of course, is in
addition to the margin which the bank itself
required when it was making the advance.
The early future of this bank as of others
will be governed by the market value of our
principal products after the war. For the
present we are feasting—and a good many
of us don’t know it—on the strength of what
we are going to earn to-morrow, when the
bill will have to be paid. How keenly we
shall feel the paying of the bill depends on
what we shall get for the products that we
must pay it with.

Last year was full of bumps for a co. in the
wool and pastoral,agency business; but there
was some compensation for drought and
strikes and embargoes—the price for all sorts
of pastoral products was excellent, and com-
missions were very sweet in consequence.
But Goldsbrough Mort’s are something more
than commiss.on brokers. Twenty years ago
they were—to their own regret, no doubt —
probably the largest station-owners in Aus-
tralia. In those days and subsequently there
was a good deal of juggling with capital and
creditors. Some of it would be pretty hard
to justify. The co. had made large advances
in the old days of borrow and boom, and the
securities which they had been obliged to
take over were written down very hard
during the ’9o’s, share and other capital
being reduced to correspond. Ten years
ago the co. still had £965,667 in these
severely written-down station properties and
stock. Then a remarkable inquiry set in for
these assets. The co.’s stations were largely
leasehold ; and the value of these, especially
in N. S. Wales, jumped astonishingly, not
only because of the movement in the live-meat
market, but also because the graduated land
tax was pushing sheep-owners out of the
freehold areas into the western lease coun-
try. It was not only the leases out of which
fine profits were made; the stock on
these properties had been very pro-
perly valued at much under the • price
which they brought. The fizz in the figures
shown in some of the past 10 years is the
result of the transactions in these old assets.
But you can’t go on repeating them. There
is now only £260,771 worth of properties and
stock left to sell at a profit in the future.
Nevertheless there are excellent prospects;
for as properties, etc., have been sold and
profit accumulated, the co. has reverted to its
old and more legitimate business of financ-
ing the pastoralist.
The next table compares the position now
with that of a year and 10 years ago:—
„ ... , 1906. 1915. 1916.
Capital .. .. £558,995 £702,737 £702,737
Reserves .. .. 133,385 590,586 630,774
Debentures . .. 1,940,107 1,623,223 1,591,973
Other liabilities . . 167,575 233,541 159,309
Liquid assets .. 508,128 447,848 587,209
Advances .. .. 986,364 2,154,765 1,949,137
Properties and stock 965,667 280,859 260 771
Premises, etc. .. 339,903 266,615 287^676
The basis of the business is much sounder
now than it was in the early days of the co.
I/own to 1888 R. Goldsbrough and Co.
carried on in Melbourne, and Mort and Co.
in Sydney. Late in that year an amalgama-
tion was arranged, with the just-dead J. S.
Horsfall as chairman. It is interesting now
to compare the first balance-sheet issued
after the amalgamation with current docu-
ments. The total capital then was £350 000
and the reserves £243,246. Total sharehold-
ers money in the business, £593.246 On
debentures the co. owed £1,149,000, and in
deposits, etc., another £1,733.882. Total owed
to the public, £2,-882,882, or nearly five times
more than the shareholders’ funds. To-day
the shareholders’ funds are almost equal to
the public’s money in the business, and
instead of more than half of the liabilities to
tfie public being deposits and money owing on
open accounts, the all “other ‘liabilities”
amount to only one-tenth of the debentures
As this paper has often said, it isn’t so much
what a pastoral finance co. owes as how
it owes that matters. Most of the trouble
aiose in the past, not so much because the
mortgage cos. and banks had borrowed
Heavily, as because they had undertaken to
pay back on demand or thereabouts. This
co. learned its lesson. To-day it can afford
wait. instead of rushing a temporarily
droughf-depreciated security on to the
market.

Assurance and Thrift Association

This co. was formed chiefly to handle house-
purchase insurance—a perfectly legitimate
offshoot of straight life insurance. The idea
is that you insure your life for a sum pay-
able in 12, 15 or 20 years or at previous
death. You can at once borrow three-fourths
of the amount of your policy to buy a house.
Or if the policy has been 'in force four or
five years you can borrow the full amount.
The house must be worth at least the amount
borrowed, and you mortgage it to secure re-
payment, with 54 p.c. interest. If you die
your beneficiary gets the house and the
amount of the policy less whatever is owing
on account of the loan. The policy carries
bonuses in the ordinary way. A good case
can be made out for this class of policy.
There are not very many young men just
making a start and anxious to do the utmost
for their young wives who can extract better
terms than this co. can offer them. At any
rate, it is well worth the attention of the
young man who wants to make sure that his
family will have an unencumbered roof
over its head at his death. He can’t do
that, of course, by simply paying a deposit
and instalments of house-purchase. On his
death, if the family’s income stops, the in-
stalments have to cease; and then if times
are bad the family is likely to have to go out
and lose a good deal even of what has been
paid.
In addition to the assurance funds, which
consist of the undistributed premiums, the
co. has a paid share capital of £17,684. and
an uncalled capital of £35,369. Twelve
months ago, when the co. had finished its
first five years, the directors pointed out that
‘‘the assurance fund considerably exceeds the
gross renewal premiums received from the
commencement, while the capital has been
preserved intact.” Considering how expensive
busiuess usually is in the first few years of
an insurance c-o.’s life, this was a most cred-
itable performance. Mainly, no doubt, it is
the result of not attempting to unduly force
business. The society is really the baby of
W. It. Dovey; and that highly respectable
parent valued the liabilities at the end of the
first five years. After making some allow-
ances for the youth of the co. he found a
surplus of £1783. “The business,” he said,
“could really he reinsured for less than the
amount in hand. The financial position is
unquestionably sound.” The principal assets
are naturally loans on mortgage and pro-
perty loans, together £36 588. The co.’s own
resources have been nearly sufficient to
finance the purchases so far; including a
trifle accepted on fixed deposit the liabilities
(exclusive of policy contracts) amount to
only £1958.
Besides house-purchase the co. does general
business, including, of late, fire insurance.
The year’s net premiums, however, amounted
altogether to only £3BO. Naturally there is
no profit worth mentioning here yet, and the
assurance funds at the end of March were
only £2lB.

ALUMINOFERRIC is used by the Sydney Water
Board, New South Wales Public Water Supply Depart-
ment, and many country Water Boards and graziers
for precipitating impurities and clarifying water in
reservoirs, cattle dams, etc. It is simply thrown
into the reservoir in the form of a solid cake, or
dissolved and added as a liquid. In three hours’
time the most discolored water will be as clear as
crystal. One pound will clear over 2000 gallons of
water. It is exceedingly cheap and quite harmless.
Sole agents: William Adams & Company Limited,
Svdney, Melbourne, Brisbane and Perth. — Advt.

ROYDS, LTD., Investors’ Advisers, Challis House, Sydney
fanning The present price of rubber is 4s. Od. to
4 ■ 10d' p er ib. Allowing that the price will fall to
i ow ' figure of 3s. per lb. for our output, the gross
return from the 210-acre block will be £3402, and
•liter allowing Is. to cover costs the net profit will be
£9268 from the first year’s tapping. In the second
veaVs tapping the further 116 acres now cleared
and in process of planting should return £1267 net.
That practically was the preliminary esti-
mate. Now that 1915 has passed (the
accounts, however, are only to September
j>o), we can reckon up how the directors
stand in the prophet class. Tapping com-
menced actually in May, 1915. But only
100 acres were ready, though another 150
acres were just on the verge. The crop was
1481 b. per tree. Thus far, then, the direc-
tors guessed well. The price, however, beat
them. The average realised was only 2s. 71d.
for all grades—that’s at Singapore. Profits,
consequently, haven’t come up to expecta-
tion ; but the figures in the accounts, on
which of course those in the table are built,
are little to go on. The tapping hadn’t gone far
when September 30 arrived, and little had
been sold. In a year or so we may be able
to get a fair idea of costs. But this much
can be said already: the growth of the trees
and the yield prove that that part of the
estate at any rate is very good rubber
country. The cost of development has been
a bit greater than was expected : still on a
capital of £18,531 at September last such
progress had been made that “it is antici-
pated when this report reaches shareholders
[in May] the whole of the available land
in the co.’s 808 acres will be under Para
rubber trees.” The position, though not
set out in the report, appears to be: —
Planted prior to Oct., 1913. . .. 310 J acres
Planted Oct., T3 —Oct., ’14.. .. 2604 acres
Planted Oct., ’14 —Oct., ’15.. .. 23 acres
Total .. .. 594 acres
That left 214 acres, which was all cleared.
Taking it all round, the Victoria looks as
likely to succeed as any rubber co. in which
Australia is interested; and its moderate
capitalisation and the remarkable absence
of promotion loading is greatly in its favor.
The Australian anti-German legislation
was among the subjects touched upon by
chairman Govett at the Ivanhoe meeting in
London. It was going to be a pretty serious
thing, he said, if it wasn’t handled very care-
fully. What had been done by the Austra-
lian Government involved the destruction of
the general principle of the integrity of in-
vestments in foreign countries; and as
Britain was the largest holder of foreign
investments, it seemed suicidal to attack
the principle.
From the chairman’s speech at the Hamp-
den meeting : —•
In association with other important producers and
the British Insulated and Helsby Cables Ltd., who
are the largest manufacturers of copper wires in
the United Kingdom, we have inaugurated a com-
pany for the manufacture of copper wire, sheet,
copper tubes, etc. The full realisation of our pio-
gramme may call for the expenditure of some
£200,000. The first section will co.-jt some
£lOO,OOO, and orders for the necessary equipment
have been placed. In starting this industry we are
complying with the urgent requirements ot the
Commonwealth authorities, and with the assurances
of such support as will satisfactorily establish the
industry. The demand for copper wire, sheet and
tubes is a largely increasing one, and the greater
industrial expansion which we all look for and which
is possible with the protective policy proposed by
the Government should ensure the prosperity of this
new and important project.
Incidentally the co. has put £lO,OOO into
the Ivembla works, where tlie whole ot the
copper output is now being refined.

Having some rich stuff to draw on, Catb-
cart Central, Ararat (Vic.), paid os. last
half-year and carried, forward- £3221. 11m
efivs. raised the total return to 305.. per
share, so the old 50s. plungers have still a
long way to go to get their own back.
Manager George Bryant had to handle lyoz.
stuff against 2oz. 2dwt. dirt for the pre-
vious term, but costs were got down to i os.
Bd. per fathom. Still tlie figure is simply
appalling, judged by old standards. On
the other hand, Bryant states that by the
contract system he is getting 28 p.c. more
work, and the men are making good wages.
A surprise of the future may be a patch ot
good wash in a tributary off No. 7 rise. So
far none of these tributaries have ever
helped the Cathcart gutter; still Bryant
has pegged out 100 acres to give him room
to chase the little stranger. This also will
provide an escape valve for the nervy inside
crowd who have the blues over the forlorn
aspect of things in the extreme workings
south-east. The boundary peg is so near that
Bryant’s promised widening of the gutter
mustn’t be long deferred, otherwise lie will
have to go poking into tlie Laugi section ot
the lead to report the change.
* * * *
There is very little that is satisfactory in
the last report of the New Laugi Logan,
Ararat (Vic.). The co. was unburdened ot
its bank debit because with the mine hung
up and calls coming in it couldn’t help that.
But it hasn’t started on the track to divs.
yet. Finance has been made rocky not so
much by the extra wages bill as by the
failure of the Cathcart gutter west of No. 2
shoot to provide payable wash. Because
the cutting-up drives fail to prospect well it
does not follow that the gold isn’t there, as
the feature of the lead is its nuggets; but
the stuff Toole has gone through is so much
worse than usual that he doesn’t give it a
hope. Exasperating, too, is the distance he
is having to crosscut on the boundary to
pick up wash. The lead must have curled
north, shortening the length in the Cathcart
Central mine. Toole’s programme on the
big Langi gutter is a big mouthful. He goes
for cutting off the water in the south of the
junction with the Cathcart lead by a reef
drive from No. 2 shaft workings. Mean-
while he will push down the lead south
from No. 1 shaft, testing the wash by cross
drives 500 ft. apart. The inducement is
that the shoots lowest dowm the gutter have
developed higher-grade wash. Water is eas-
ing off, and if labor doesn’t turn down con-
tract work he should soon see what grist
panelling brings to the mill. If his ounce
estimate is realised and the plan of cutting
off the water far south pans out right, he
ought to be able to do cheap work and have
a mine.
* * * *
Greenfinch (W.A.) is urged by a local
critic to do some genuine development or
give up portion of the ground it is holding.
The co. has three leases, comprising 70 acres,
adjoining the Edna May and Deeps, but only
one has been prospected to any extent, and
in this values were cut off at a shallow depth
bv a granite bar. One of the other blocks is
believed to be worth deeper testing; but
although calling power and cash assets look
well on paper most of the money subscribed
by shareholders has either been “loaned on
short call” or “invested” in an opal bearing
area. As gold is in more demand than opal
the aforesaid critic rightly considers that
Westonia should be the sole theatre of the
co.’s operations for the present.
North Yilgarn (W.A.) rejoices over the
striking of an ample supply of water for ore
treatment in the Butcher Bird at Marda.
The want of water blocked the development
of a number of mines and practically para-
lysed the district. Much activity is now
being shown and crushings are being taken
out everywhere; 163 tons from the Butcher
Bird have just yielded over- 2500 z. of gold.
The Great Unknown and Allen’s Find are in
the locality.
Marvel Loch (W.A.) has disposed of its
remaining assets, and hopes to pay a final
div. of lid. before passing aw 7 ay. Portions
of its holding are still being scratched by
working parties. Several privately-owned
show's in the district are doing well.

At the Ivanlioe meeting chairman Govett
said that the tonnage and grade of ore re-
serves w r ere undiminished despite the extrac-
tion of 240,000 tons since last year. Hopes
were entertained that the main ore shoot
would get clear of the porphyry bar at
3470 ft., but if it didn’t the decrease of the
reserve tonnage wrnuld not be so fast as
might be expected with a mine with no
bottom such as.the Ivanhoe. Concerning the
firewood trouble Govett commended the
general manager for the great service he ren-
dered shareholders by securing supplies of
coal and preventing the show from shutting
dowm. This had not <mly enabled the co. to
continue making profits, but also to retain
their labor force, the demobilising of wffiich
had been disastrous to some of the cos. that
had hurriedly ceased operations.
* » * *
A month ago it looked as if Great Fingall
(W.A.) would have to shut down for want
of labor, but somehow it has managed to keep
going. But the only chance the co. has of
earning profits is to run the treatment plant
at full capacity, and that is dependent on
securing a bigger force underground.^
Having overcome to a great extent the
dust evil in the Kalgoorlie mines, the in-
spectors are now grappling with the trouble
in the treatment plants. It is a
more difficult problem than was faced
underground, but already considerable
improvements have been effected. In
the interior of one or tw r o plants which a
Bulletin man w 7 ent through a while ago it
w 7 as only by considerable eye-straining that
lie could see a foot or two ahead.

Northampton lead and copper field (W.A.)
lias awakened from a long slumber and
decided to hustle for capital. There are oO-
odd shows in the district which have been
worked from time to time in the last 40
vears; about two-thirds of them are lead and
the balance copper. Local syndicates have
tried to w T ork most of them, but in near .'
every case an insufficiency of funds to cope
with water has blown them out. The Fre-
mantle Trading Co. «> operating on two of
them, and according to official icpoits is
doing fairly well.* * »
“Oofbird” : Is the Battler Co. (M .A.)
within the law? A second-hand mill is being
hurriedly installed to treat a few hundred
tons of high-grade ore, and when this has
been done its occupation for some time at
least will be gone. By dumping some good
stuff on the surface, blowing about the good
return that is to come, and announcing the
amount of amalgam taken off the plates each
week, somebody will be able to get out while
the public is getting in. This is the sort of
thing that kills mining.
* * * *
Manager Stuckey has got the Edna May
Deeps shaft down below 440 ft., and swears
that he will reach the required depth of
480 ft. without much hindrance. He may, but
the chances are still against his opening up
the reef with his present unwatering appli-
ances. The emphatic opinion of most mining
men who have gone into the matter on the
spot is that the cutting of the reef means
flooding.
Manager Craze had a tale of promise to
tell at the Round Hill (Tas.) Silver and
Lead Co.’s meeting the other day. Two
parcels of hand-picked ore were sent to the
Cockle Creek works as a bulk test. From
No. 1 tunnel, 1.4 tons gave 38.4 p.c. lead,
239.50 z. silver, 12dwt. 13gr. gold per ton,
and 4 p.c. copper. From No. 2 tunnel, 22.2
tons gave 157.4 p.c. lead, 61oz. silver, 3dwt.
17gr. gold, no copper. As No. lis the lower
tunnel this is reckoned evidence that there is
an improvement in values with depth.
The Heemskirk (Tas.) tin district boomed
in the ’Bo’s, though most of the shows ate
more than they gave. But the Heemskirk
Tin Syndicate reckons there’s still something
there. It is sluicing away the overburden,
and has a barge on the property ready for a
trial.
The Jupiter, a zinc-lead show lying on
the main line of the Rosebery belt and
between the Hercules and the Tasmanian
Coppei*, has been sold to a syndicate, which
proposes to float itself into a co. and then
get busy developing the property.
The signs are that the Mt. Lyell Co. has
definitely made up its mind to own the Mt.
Read and Rosebery districts. The snuffing-
out of the Hercules, Primrose and Tas-
manian Copper cos. means good-bye to a
laggard lot. With Sticlit in command, there
should be a wake-up as far as Zeehan. If
Lyell intends to go in for electrolysis, how
can it finance a 50,000h.p. scheme with the
limited capital provided in the options taken
from the selling group? The B. H. Prop, iron
and steel business has simply swallowed cash.
Yet Delprat hadn’t to do any experimenting.
There will have to be a big expansion of
the capital so far talked about; for Rose-
bery, unless Sticht has a surprise up his
sleeve. If not the variations in terms
shouldn’t bar the deal, as the country wants
every dormant asset vitalised.
