“Buckley,” from North Queensland: —
“The Great Lake of Tasmania is reckoned to be
good for anything from 75,000h.p. to 80,000h.p.” I
do not think so, but perhaps I am spiteful, as it
knocked out my own little concern over four years
ago. I can forgive a genuine thing, but hate to be
beaten by a pretender. Take the data given by the
company’s experts who visited it in October, 1910.
Area, 205 sq. miles; net fall, 1000 ft.; rainfall 90
inches. Knock off six inches for evaporation and
we get 129,000h.p. I looked up the rain book for
Great Lake. Couldn’t find it, but got instead Lake
District. The average rainfall was about 40 inches.
Give it 45 inches. Multiply this by the 1000 ft. fall,
and deduct according to the recognised formulae. The
result is 18,000h.p. delivered to the generator every
second. Now 18,000h.p. is equal to 16,400e.h.p.,
which will become 11.000e.h.p. at the step-down sta-
tion. Bosh! Well I admit formulae sometimes go
wrong. This thing must disclose its power value
before long, and if it is much below what it is
cracked up to be, then the person who wants to sell
a hydro, will be met with the reply: “You cannot
tell the power. The State engineers couldn’t. No
one can!’’ And the person won’t bring off his deal.
* * * .*
Having put bucket dredging in the Malay
Peninsula on its feet and made a start in
Bolivia, it looks as though Australian min-
ing men might now do something in Korea
and Siberia. The Malaya Tin Corporation
(Sydney) has George Poole putting the
tape over the Korean option; and T. 11.
Martyn, the Sydney broker, theosophist and
dredging enthusiast, who arranged the co.’s
earlier business in Siam, is leaving at once
to look at the Siberian property. It is in
the Tomsk district, and considering that it
is alleged to be dredging ground almost
fabulous values are talked about.
The S. Aus. Oil Wells Co. hasn’t found
a gusher at Robe yet, but Charlton Dod,
the manager, is confident still. “Nothing
unfavorable to the chance of our striking
oil has been encountered,” he says. On the
contrary, “even if nothing more definite be
struck in this hole we have piled up enough
favorable evidence to warrant our putting
down bores elsewhere to search for the pro-
ductive sands.” Meantime, after drilling to
1575 ft., the co. is casing the hole to shut
off the’water —a tedious but most necessary
precaution.
* * * *
Waihi (M.L.) treated 192,333 tons last
year for 1333,433. Including bullion under
treatment and interest the revenue was
£303,078. Exnenses took £208,108, leaving a
balance of £155,409. The co. paid 20 p.c. on
capital or 4s. per share for the year, £24,0b4
is put aside to pay taxes and £21,840 is
written off for depreciation.
* * * *
Canada has encouraged the metal trades,
and as a consequence manufacturers of metal
goods and owners of steel furnace plants were
quickly able to adapt their works to the pro-
duction of war munitions. Thereby, through
selling some millions of pounds (the cable
says £60,000,000) worth of munitions and
clothing to the Allies, Canada is recovering
a fairly-large proportion of her expenditure
in equipping 340,000 men. Yet with the ex-
ceptiou, perhaps, of nickel, Australia is
richer in all metals than Canada. _ The dif-
ference lies in Canada’s past determination to
do her own job, wherefore no Customs duty
was regarded as too high so long as it was
necessary to shut the foreigner out.

How I Darkened My
Greyjjair.
Lady gives Simple Home Recipe That
She Used to Darken Her Grey Hair.
For years I tried to restore my grey hair
o its natural color with the prepared dyes
and stains, but none of them gave satisfac-
tion and they were all expensive. I finally
came across a simple recipe which I mixed
at home that gives wonderful results I
gave the recipe, which is as follows, ‘to a
number of my friends, and they are all
delighted with it To 7oz. of water add a
small box of Orlex Compound, loz. of bay
rum and -}oz. of glycerine. These ingredients
can be bought at any chemist’s at very little
cost. Use every other day until the hair
becomes the required shade, then every two
weeks. It will not only darken the grev
hair, but removes dandruff and scalp humors
and acts as a tonic to the hair. It is not
sticky or greasy, does not rub off, and does
not color the scalp.— Advt. [5

Sydney, June 26, 1916.
My Dear Arini, —
The Munro Fairgusons, after a bout of
WeStralian welcomes, will get to Adelaide
about July 17, homeward bound for the
Yarra. Early in that gusty month his Ex.
adds A.D.C. Firth to his staff. He’s the
tall young soldier with the dignity of an
archbishop and a pair of blue eyes like a
girl’s. For a while he’s been convalescing
behind our State Governor’s chair.
Government House spread to the Jap visi-
tors had to be limited to 40 knives and
forks. The old dining-room isn’t a spacious
apartment for these days of big corroborees.
But its dignity is tremendous, and the por-
traits on the wall are solemn enough to
quell the most frivolous. The feature of
the decorations, gorgeous poinsettias, cast a
becoming blush on the official countenance.
The brilliant red blooms were much admired,
in fluent English, by Admiral Matsumura.
The mix-up over the invitations for some
of the official shows to the Japs—notably
the one at Parliament House —led to breath-
less sorties on the part of the uninvited. One
or two ex-Gaud Mayors, various aldermen
and innumerable indignant citizens wanted
to know who was responsible for the fact
that the pleasure of their presence had not
even been mentioned? They got in, of course.
But it was a last-minute’s anguished cry
for help that did it. The same chaos over
the Jap Admiral’s reception on his own deck
was not the fault of the visitors. They
left the matter in the hands of our sailors
at Garden Island. And they seem to have
left everything to Providence and the bo’s’n’s
mate. A nice social squall in consequence!
Dazed citizens who refused to believe they
were not bidden clamored fur help. In vain
this time. Garden Island ’phones went deaf
and dumb-*-there was an end of it!
The Government reception to the Jap.
sailors at Parliament House was shorn of
the star guests early in the afternoon. Ad-
miral Matsumura and his men—or some of
them —were itching to be off to Government
House. After profuse apologies and low
bows they did succeed in slipping away to
play tennis. On the courts the Japanese
wear soft-felt slippers with a pocket in each
for the big toe. Shod in silence they slip
over the ground very fast. But they hit the
balls sky-high—so high that indignant
sparrows on the Stricklands’ chimney-pots
scuttled away.
One feature, of the Iwate reception, with
the Jap Admiral as host, was a wonderful
collection of scenery in miniature. Every-
thing was on view—from imitation goldfish
fused with electric life, darting after micro-
scopic prey, to a Zoo furnished with toy
inmates. A Buddha or two gave a prayerful
effect to a Jap village. The Jap sailors
had sat up all the previous night to make
this baby hamlet, and our Botanic Gardens
had gladlv supplied some of the herbage. Our
Best People are busy elsewhere; but our
Second Best were in great evidence during
the gaieties, and had at last an opportunity
to. show their smart drapery. As for the
millinery, it either shot up like a church
steeple or sat squat like a penny tart.
. According to a male critic the most con-
sistently well-turned-out little woman of
the week was Mrs. Shimizu. The Consul’s
creamy-complexioned, petite wife has a
passion for European clothes, and gets her
gloves and. shoes on the roomy side to dis-
guise the tininess of her extremities. At the
Iwate party she wore a cream cloth coat and
skirt that was It from a tailor’s point of
view, and a saucy felt hat in the same milky
shade. Mrs. Archgaiters Wright and her
two daughters made an impressive progress
along the deck. Mother’s dark eyes were
matched by the serious brown velvet of her
fur-edged gown. Governor Strickland left
the show early, but daughters Mary and
‘r i, staye *? .behind amongst the scenic
waterfal s and jiu-jitsu items that followed.
All the local Consuls made an inspection of

stuff looks like the heavy lingerie in which
Julius Ivnight so often thrills the gallery.
s P° rt y voung man who
used to attend Governor Strickland, has
officially, rejoined his old regiment in Eng-
hind. He fought in Gallipoli, you remember,
with our boys, and was wounded. Unoffi-
cially poor Wallis is still wrestling with
tlm effects of recent pneumonia, neurasthenia
and a few other casualties.
• A ll ew youn g gentleman will presently
join the Stricklands’ mdnage. He is a
slightly-damaged hero named Boulton, who
belongs to a- Hie lan’ regiment.
Colonel Bartlett, one of this State’s older
militia men, of late years on Headquarters
Staff, died lately after a few days’ illness.
“Charlie” Bartlett was a genial, kindly
soul who had the good word of the khaki
crowd in Barracks. Retired from the billet
of A.A.G. in 1912, he often strolled into
the mess to have a yarn with old pals in the
service. He leaves a handsome widow and
two sons.
A Brisbane man, Norman Christensen,
drove away from St. Philip’s with a Mos-
man girl, Doris Nankervis, the other even-
ing. At least, she had been Doris Nan-
kervis till a few minutes before. The
grey old church was abloom with flowers.
The bride and her father —7ft. or so of him
—were a striking pair as they stepped up to
the waiting ’groom. Doris floated through
the church in billows of white tulle over a
shimmer of silk. A new-old device of yards
and yards of white satin trimmed the skirt.
Of course there was a gleam of pale pink
on the fluffy corsage. Madge Nankervis, in
buttercup with raven millinery, attended to
the long bridal train. Second-best man was
P. Fleming, of Brisbane. The cake was
cut at Petty’s, next door.

The conquering Russians gave another
brilliant sample of their wares on Saturday
night. The Conservator]'nm was a packed
sardine tin. Pianist Miroviteh, the older
and more matured artist, stunned the local
talent into complete silence (it generally
chatters its criticism while the piano
ripples on) by a magnificent performance
of the Bach-Stradal organ Concerto! In
this, which took the chill off the pro-
gramme, M’vitch actually persuaded a
Chappell piano to gammon it was a church
organ. Piastro, holding his expensive fiddle
high up on one shoulder for a start, had to
warm the cold strings (I suppose a price-
less violin catches the sniffles as easily as
a baby) before he got his heavenly notes in
order. Later, with his colleague at the
piano, he gave Sydney a treat—Nicolaeff’s
Sonata in G Minor. And for once a Syd-
ney audience heard a programme straight
through to the finish. Then the pair re-
sponded to the tumult with an encore or
two.
Mith the Russian Mirovitch-Piastro duo
comes a big Belgian as friend and advance
agent. He’s Emile Bucker, the man who
controls so many theatres in Java. The
minstrels cleaned up the Dutchmen’s cash in
the spicy isles and brought the majestic
Bucker along to pilot their Australian tour.
Talking of his battered country, he wonders
—like others—at the folly of the Huns.
“They got all they wanted out of Belgium
before the war. Even on the streets of
Antwerp German was spoken. Now —and
I k Q ow the temper of my countrymen—for
100 years to come we will hate every
German who sets his foot on Belgian soil.
Others may forgive them. For us? Never!”
Professor “Andy” Stuart was 60 the other
day, and 250 faces met him at the Medical
School. The roomful rose and cheered
wildly, and the “father” beamed. It was a
great reception. After a three-minutes’ riot
the noise subsided, and the cause of it all
got his word in. “Gentlemen,” he said. “I
presume this is in honor of my 60th birth-
day (More tumult.) “I hope you may all
celebrate the same number. Of course, you
won’t, but by knowing your physiology you
will go a long way towards it. We will
now proceed to prolong our lives”—and the
lecture started.

An ecclesiastical note from an assisted
immigrant:—
If the S. M. Herald and Telegraph adopted the
plan of The Bulletin and retained the services of a
Religious Editor, assisted by a fully-qualified staff
of churchwardens and acolytes, their expansion of
the morning war cables would cause less merriment
in liturgical circles. Both papers, apropos the
Kitchener memorial service in St. Paul’s, gravely
announced that the Liturgy of St. Chrysostom was
sung to Kiefi’s chant. I guess the congregation
would get very tired if “The Divine Liturgy of our
Holy Father John Chrysostom,’’ according to the use
of the Great Church of Constantinople and the Holy
Mountain of Athos, had been sung in full, as the
said services comprise a pretty bulky volume, while
surely even the Herald knows that Kieff or Kiev
is the name of a monastery. What was sung of
course was the famous “Contakion of the Departed,’’
from the Liturgy of St. Chrysostom to the traditional
Kieff melody. One of the most dignified pieces of
church music in the world, its increasing use at State
memorial services is one of the fruits of the closer
understanding that now exists between the Anglican
Church and the Greek especially The Holy
Orthodox Church of Russia. It is frequently sung
in St. Paul’s Cathedral. It is not so well known in
Australia, but is heard from time to time at Christ
Church St. Lawrence and St. James’s (Sydney), at
St. Saviour’s Cathedral (Goulburn), and in some of
the Queensland cathedrals.
Did you notice that one of this State’s
squatting Ryrie family, Oswald, was married
in London a few weeks ago? He’s in the
Light Horse, and has chosen a Scotch lass
named Jean liowatson. She comes from
Ayrshire.
The slim, spectacled Sloman, the young
headmaster of Sydney Grammar School,
finds his glasses a nuisance. The local re-
cruiters won’t pass his eyesight. So he trips
to London, via Canada, to try his luck on the
other side.
The Merryvale Pops on Saturday nights
have caught on. Cyclones of encores show
that the average citizen wants a quart of
music for a thimbleful of cash. But the
treasury manager doesn’t mind. The list of
items includes everything from a Storm Fan-
tasia to “The Last Rose of Summer.”

The Homes for Heroes Concert, backed un

Phil Newbury, pianist Eafalewski. Un
Sharp and the Ivennedys helped to keen

Social life in Victoria Barracks shows
all kinds of mild eruptions, due to the
changes and chances of war time. In the
old days Permanent officers’ ladies frankly
discussed each other’s domestic affairs—
from the color of the washing that hung in
Mrs. Colonel Starcher’s back yard to the
newest shade in Mrs. Major Highflyer’s
front hair. But the gossip, even when it
got on to the suspected connection with
“trade” of Mrs. Starcher’s grandfather, was
for the most part not unkind. There was
an esprit de femme amongst the women who
lived in the. long bleak terrace. In any
sudden crisis their housemaids borrowed
tea and butter from each other, and knew
exactly how much loaf-sugar Jane, next
door, could lend at a minute’s notice. To-
day this is all changed. Strangers, who
scarcely know each other, come and go.
Mrs. Icy.de Monde in XYZ quarters does
not “call” on her next neighbor for nine
months. By that time the good lady is her-
self ready to pack up. A Barracks tea
party the other day was up to the latest
good form in this muffled community.
Ladies who scarcely nod to each other on
the lawn assembled to solemnly drink pekoe
together. The arrival of the Icy de
Mondes, who never opened their lips except
to> say “Good-baye,” broke up the exhilarat-
ing affair.
The latest word from Mrs. Cyril Bell
(who used to be Mrs. Bob Brough) is that
hubby is now a sub-lieut. in the R.N.R., and
wears his uniform on H.M.S. Hermione.
Earlier in the war he saw seven months’
service in France, and came intact through
the Loos argument.
Died last week at North Sydney, Eliza-
beth Metcalfe, a daughter of that baronet
William Macartney, rector of the parish of
Deserteguy, whose brother was the late Dean
Macartney, of Melbourne. The baronet father
of these Macartneys was a member of the
Irish Parliament before the Union of Great
Britain and Ireland, and voted against Union.
The present baronet is Sir John, born in
1832. He is now, or wms verv recently,
living in Queensland.
The J.C.W. firm has lent one of its pic-
ture places for a Y.M.C.A. National Fund
show on July 19. John Brownlow, as hon.
manager, is rounding up a likely-looking lot
of events for the King’s Cross Theatre on
that date.
The 9th Brigade has sent its ultimatum
per hon. sec. ,Mrs. Alex. Jobson. Its sock
competition will not close till July 18.
Yours affectionately,
VANDOTRIAN.

A AVAR-TIME BEAUTY SECRET.
HOW TO LOOK BEAUTIFUL ON LESS
MONEY.
In these days of economy we welcome
anything which enables us to cut down
expenses, and many lady readers will gladly
accept my advice and try this excellent and
inexpensive recipe for the complexion. Any
chemist will mix it up for you, and it is
made up from 2oz. rose water, 1 dram tinc-
ture of benzoin and 2oz. flowers of oxzoin.
When well shaken it should be applied with
a sponge and allowed to dry, then dust the
face with a soft cloth. Even if you have
been using expensive creams, powders _ or
rouge, you will be surprised how much nicer
this lotion will make your complexion and
how delightfully smooth and soft your skin
will become under its influence. It will
save you money, too, because it takes the
place, in your daily toilet, of the best cream,
powder or rouge you can obtain, and the cost
is comparatively small. — Advt.

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melbourne, June 26, 1916.
My Dear Yarrie, —
The vice-regal Fergusons lately decided
that they were quite as tired as Governor
Stanley, so started to look round for a
fresh visiting-place. Westralia hadn’t been
touched for a bed and breakfast, so, in
defiance of the seasonal upheaval in the
Great Australian Bight, the couple have
gone off to visit the Barrons in Perth, where
the winter is beautiful and Government
House is nerched high above a magnificent
selection of river and ocean scenery. By
the way, the Barrons’ “time” is up in
August.
Melba in opera drew the social battle-fleet
to the Playhouse on Saturday night. The
Playhouse has found its feet, for the Best
have “taken it up,” and the panting Intel-
lectuals will run alongside and see that the
baby isn’t dropped. (Excuse this mixture
of metaphors—l always do it when excited.)
Lady Helen and the Stanley dame blessed
the occasion, and though all the vice-regal
men were out of Melbourne the show didn’t
fall down and die of a broken heart.
The Willow Song was beautifully ren-
dered. The night-robe was a dream of white
chiffon and the dressing-gown of purple vel-
vet and ermine built on to a sheath of white
satin. Melba dropped this gown in a crum-
pled heap of fine color mid-stage at the
climax of a wonderful note and slipped into
the bed of dread with the orchestra playing
beautiful murder music.
Melba took her triumphant “curtain” and
was pelted with flowers. One popular doctor
made a fine cricketer’s shot from the far-
away centre balcony, and the posy landed
like a bird at her feet. Someone handed up
two chirpy yellow canaries tied up in a cage
with pink ribbons. A barricade of flowers
quickly gi-ew up on the stage, and Melba
ordered a taxi to take them to the Soldiers’
Rest Rooms just round the corner. Then
the stage was cleared for the Chippendale
furniture of “Quinney’s.”
Melba’s daughter-in-law sat in a box, with
tier black hair tied up in a veil of royal-
blue tulle. A girl with her added a color
note of bright seagreen satin and lit her
brow with diamonds. The Baillieux occupied
some latticed boxes, and so did Mrs. Isaacs
and her daughter—the clever one who is her
father’s Associate. The Fenners were in the
front row, and the Norman Bayles were in
evidence. Pianist St. Leger sat in the bal-
cony gloating. Melba herself had a box, and
when she came in to occupy it, muffled up to
the eyes in furs, the house nearly blew up
with applause.
Melba had a small duel with the musical
unionists the other day, and they allowed
her to win like musicians and gentlemen.
A scratch orchestra was being got together
for “Otello” at the Reps., and a non-unionist
was found in the band. The union leader
was just getting into his war-paint when
.Melba tackled him personally. The singer’s
smile beat him, and for Saturday’s perform-
ance (only) the lion lay down with the lamb
and forebore to eat it.
Lesley Madden gained another lap or two
on the next “Queen” by selling a £5O bunch
of supper tickets on Wednesday night. An
Evening at Home was arranged at Cliveden,
and you can imagine how the peerage rolled
up to it. Lesley herself wore brilliant
purple, with a band of purple round her
forehead. The Italians, who are the lions
and lionesses of the moment, were present
in force. They speak half a dozen words of
English between them, but got on famously
by smiling everlastingly and making glad
noises that don’t appear in the dictionaries.
Tenor Kirby, who is something of an Italian
scholar, was very voluble for a time; but
the visitors didn’t seem to have learned thmr
Italian in the same part. Mrs. McGilli-
cuddy had a supper party to help this St.
Vincent’s effort. So had Mrs. Tom Hogan
and Mrs. Tom Hogan’s ermine cloak. Mrs.
Davidson, whose husband keeps sheep, was
in with the Madden crowd for her last
party before departing for the Backblocks.
Mrs. Jack Sternberg had rose-pink touches
about her, and Mrs. Hennessy was good to
look at in black and white.
As it was a sit-down supper party, most
of the women brought their husbands. It
was quite a night-out for these poor fellows,
and their delight was pathetic to witness.
As a rule these patriotic sprees are attended
only by the women and a few serious draw-
ing-room males who audit the accounts.
There was a dais where the singers took
refuge, and the listeners who could not grab
lounge seats had to stand. Some suggestion
had been made of hiring light cane chairs,
but as the lowest tenderer wanted threepence
each for them, the idea was stamped on.
The show was kept as “select” as possible;
but, despite all the passports and entangle-
ments, some of the ticket-buyers left with
their imperial noses in the air. I think some
of them had been annoyed by the entry of
unconsecrated outsiders and others by the
lynx-eyed inspection before admission to the
card rooms. of the resident flatters
offered their dens as card rooms, but insisted
on personally choosing the paying guests.
A whole gang of private detectives, more or
less disguised as society loafers, were en-
gaged to watch the stairs and hold down
the doormat. If there is anything in this
world that gives me joy it is the feeling that
there are detectives everywhere, watching
and waiting and drawing their salaries in
coal-scuttles, umbrella-stands and pie dishes.
Mrs. Ross Soden, who has lately been
dusting out the ballroom at Grong Grong
and applying it to charitable uses, is having
a quiet time in the absence of most of the
family on war-service. Gordon, after mark-
ing time in motor-ambulance work for some
months at Reading (Eng.), lately managed
to catch the eye of the right people, and
went up aloft with the R.F.C. Captain
Jack is at Broadmeadows and expects to
be off during July. It will be his second
trip abroad with the Medical Corps. Soli-
citor Harry, who made a name for himself
at Henley before the war started, has been
twice turned down for the A.I.F. because of
an alleged defect in his vision, though he
can read his Bible by candlelight without
an effort. He is still struggling to break
in.
Mrs. Gerald Loughran, back in Melbourne
after a month in Egypt, was one of the last
Australian trippers to visit that country
before the edict was issued prohibiting offi-
cers’ wives from dropping in to see their
husbands.
Writing last week of Captain Fitts I
told you he had gone away from his own
Australia to get a commission. He was in
Bradford learning wool-classing, it seems,
when the war broke out, and he enlisted a
fortnight later. Had he been here the boy
would have enlisted here; for the Hamilton
Fittses are as good Australian as you can
get anywhere.
Natty little Dorothy Pearce’s friends are
organising a novelty variety programme for
a cabaret at the Austral Salon rooms on
Friday night. The proceeds, of course, are
to go to St. Vincent’s fund by way of votes
for Dorothy in her “Queen of Defence”
capacity.
.Miss Freeman, the Victorian girl who has
been talking about her experiences in Ger-
many, hails from Gordons, which is next door 1
to Bungaree, where the savages used to come
from. Miss Freeman says that in the early
part of the war she and other Australians
were treated very carefully. The Germans
were uncertain who were American and who
Australian, and were terrified, at that
stage, to offend the Wilsonites. The people
were told that Japan had declared war on
England, gnd the Japanese Ambassador was
carried through the streets on a golden
chair. They were also told that Japan had
sent troops to quell riots in India, which was
absurdly inconsistent with the other yarn.
But German diplomacy lies have always
been absurdly inconsistent.
Mrs. Melville, a survivor of the Lusitania,
has arrived in Melbourne. The poor lady
saw her husband drown and her two sons
spring off the deck to their death, while she
was in the water clinging to her six-year-old
daughter. The mother came back to life
on a destroyer; but the little girl had
died of shock. Mrs. Melville is now taking
unto herself a new protector. Mr. Walter
Best becomes her second husband some day
this week.
Rippon Lea, the Elsternwick home of the
late Defence-Minister Sargood, is still an
establishment of some splendor, though
parts of it have been pared off. The public,
which used to swarm thither to garden fetes
in the Sargoods’ time, have lately had a
to make comparisons, now that the
Ben Nathans have been offering views of
their orchid-houses at a shilling a time —pro-
ceeds to patriotic funds. The Nathan orchids,
attended to. by James Deering, who nursed
the collection through its youth, had in
Nathan’s Armadale days begun to gain re-
spect for their owner; since their transfei*-
ence to Rippon Lea they have gained wide
advt. as the most complete assemblage of
the quaint vegetables in Australia.
The Embling miss, of St. Ivilda, is begin-
ning to find a difficulty in storing the admir-
ing letters that arrive from kid-vested fight-
ing-men. The pattern vest was made of
kid-glove tops, very light and verv warm,
and only costing sd. to post to any part
of the Front. The industry began with one
for a young brother. It was greatly admired
in camp. “Sister made it,” he told them.
“Make you one? Of course she will!” So
a swarm of “orders” came along, and then
sister called for help. Now the vest is being
wern in most parts of the fighting area
where there are Australian soldiers, and
glove-tops come from the public in vast
numbers.
The State Parliament raises steam in the
old political boiler on Wednesday, July 5,
and invitations to witness the cocked-hat
solemnities from the Ladies’ Gallery have
gone out. Mrs. J. M. Davies is preparing
her usual tea-party.
Doris Gumming, of the Western District
squatter clan, is the latest pretty thing in
nursing probationers at St. Vincent’s Hos-
pital. Aunt McKellar married specialist Dr.
Dunhill not so long ago; so Miss Doris may
get a helpful hint or two in the family circle.
Astonished friends say this nursing business
is just to pass the time while the particular
Baillieu soldier in whom pretty Doris is in-
terested is away strafing the Hun. Any-
how, it is a good way of passing the time.
The Albert Miller widow sheltered with
her younger boy at Ballan after her dual be-
reavement of husband and son. She has now
come back to town, and is located in a Mel-
bourne Mansions flat waiting for a chance
to dodge the U-boats and join her two
daughters in London. Son-in-law Alan
Currie has been slightly wounded and men-
tioned in despatches, and nephew Arthur
Wrixon fell in action this month somewhere
in France.
Nearly all the richest Toorak families are
in mourning, the latest being the Grice clan,
who are mourning the Jack Grices’ youngest
boy Tom, just reported killed in France.
When the war started he rejoined the British
Army, from which he had resigned in peace
in order to return to Australia. He and his
young wife went off to England, and he got
a captaincy in the Royal Fusiliers. An
elder brother was killed in the Boer War.
Pasitoralist Campbell and family have been
in a turmoil of jubilation of late. They
had hardly ceased patting little Johnnie
for helping to pull off the ‘‘Head of the
River” victory when a cable message an-
nounced that son Walter had won the Mili-
tary Cross. Walter got a commission in
England soon after brother Donald had
waded ashore at Gallipoli.
George Fairbairn and his wife have
steamed north to Bananaland to look over
the family sheeprun and bake awhile in the
sun before tuiming back to Red Crossing and
politics in July. Mrs. George is involved
in too many of these things to take a whole
winter off.
The residents of Hay and thereabouts suit-
ably farewelled Dr. Field and his wife on
their departure for Melbourne, where the
medico from the country will get into uniform
and pack his kit for foreign service. The
Hay farewells then changed to welcomes to
Dr. John O’Brien, who has done military
work both here and in Maoriland.
Melba boxed in great glory at the first
night of “Cavalleria” at the Princess’s. But
she sat well back and out of the spotlight.
Her dress was a dead-black except for white
arms veiled w T ith fine black tulle. Tenor
Lois made an impressive first appearance.
He is a handsome boy who wears the right
sort of clothes. and legs. The principals
dress well —just as well as the chorus do
not. The chorus is so thin that you can
see daylight through it, and its clothes are
weird.
Adelaide rings up as usual: —
The weather is to be fined 40s. for being’ drunk
and disorderly on the day of the street-collection for
the Maruba Red Cross Hospital; also, £ 3 for re-
peating the offence on the day of the Maruba fair
at Austral Gardens on Friday. But the good, loyal
crowd rolled along and did its financial duty—£2oo
worth.
On the same evening there was a Government
House reception—a smallish and mostly official affair.
Next day the Victorian Stanleys departed, and the
Fergusons looked in for an hour or so on their way
to Westralia.
The engagement of our syndicate singer, Clara
Serena, to Roy Hellish is signalled.
Maude Plunkett, an Adelaide girl who did good
work here for the Repertory Theatre, and has since
been on the professional stage and in the films in
America, is on a visit to her people here prior to her
marriage to C. Langworthy Poole, of Chicago.
Mrs. A. A. Scarfe and Mrs. Gavin Gardner were
joint hostesses at a Queen Adelaide Club lunch t’other
day. But I don’t suppose it really will shorten the
war.
Old Dr. Bevan was billed to lecture on English
painters at the Art Gallery on Wednesday evening,
and the howling wind and rain didn’t prevent the
veteran from doing so in the coldest spot in Adel-
aide.
The League of Loyal Women, which is almost a
3 ear old, shook its rattle at a public meeting on
Thursday night, and the Exhibition was almost filled.
The Governor presided and said an encouraging word
as to the work the League has done and the more it
must do. Then came Lady Galway, who invariably
bears off the oratorical laurels when she speaks. His
Ex. of Vic. added a word ; so did Chief Justice Mur-
ray. The League has an eye to after-war problems
and depression, and makes sane and reasonable
economics and patriotic shopping two of its watch-
words.
The lute Syd Day’s blue-eyed daughter
Marjorie and young Arthur Riley have just
become mutually engaged, and are absurdly
happy.
Isabel Alcock, daughter of the late H. A.
(Billiard) Alcock and Mrs. Alcock, of Haw-
thorn, is getting her trousseau together for
a wedding with Herbert Smith, of the Mel
bourne City Council and Kew. iel '
. L ef w y Madden has hitherto appeal,,]
111 the congregation of the people at
placidly-prim, gently-aloof damsel to who,!!
humor is an utter stranger, moving care
tully among the multitude in dignified , '
serve But, in the latest photogranh
circulated for general admiration! the mr
pink miss is shown as smiling a record smile
into the camera, with an immense muff held
up beneath a coquettish chin—the familiar
large, frank, all-ivory, ain’t-I-mce smile of
the rising variety-actress. The outbreak
has something to do with Dimple’s Candida
ture as “Queen of Charity” in the St Vh '
cent’s cash-raising competition.
The Victorian Artists have begun their
Pageantry by proposing to cast Carrie Haase
for the central figure of Australia.
Evelyn Scotney and husband Howard
White tuned up on Saturdav night at the
Auditorium. Melba’s pointed remarks
about the musical starvation of Melbourne
seem to have started a famine-relief exnerli.
tion. The White-Scotney pair are fresh
from America, and Evelyn has the proofs
ot a visit to New York in the shape of <mr
geous frocks. Put your head in a bag and
imagine ivory white silk under pale blue
transparencies and finished with fur edges
The wearer’s hair was swathed neatly round
her head, and a wreath of baby forget-me-
nots rested on her brow. Of course she w,«
lit up with diamonds. was
Dorothea Low, Mrs. Ted Dyson and Mrs
Jackson are preparing a cabaret for Friday
at the Austral Salon. A patriotic showlet of
course, and mysterious novelties are promised
besides the usual music.
Fred McCubbin and son Louis are to have
their combined show opened by Melba in
the Athenaeum on the 27th. The shutters
won’t be put up till July 11.
The arrival of the latest opera director
reminds me of a yarn that is going the
rounds. It is to the effect that Quinlan is
now keeping a hairdressing shop in one of
Arnold Bennett’s Five Towns in England—
Manchester, I think it is. If the story is
right, perhaps Quinlan is doing it for a rest,
with special rates for tenors.
Beatrice Day, who has been living in
M.L. these many moons, has started for
more exciting zones. Her son is already in
khaki and a trench.
Kitchener’s salute was a characteristic
variation of the one described in the drill-
book, and all the “permanents” here tried
to copy it after the great man’s visit, It
was a thoughtful, rather slow touch of bis
cupped-fingers to his cap-peak, and would
have been a very slight courtesy indeed but
for the accompanying bow. The exercise was
the despair of the officers here. Major (now
Colonel) Carroll gave the best imitation—
but he had to be on horseback. Unmounted
he lacked the Kitchener inches necessary for
the performance.
Petticoated patriots who frantically sig-
nalled the dear old Wimmen’s League in the
hope of waking the heads up to the dangers
of the “enemy in our midst,” have been told
to subside. “No politics in war time,” say
the social semaphores. (The anti-German
question is classed as “political.”)
Sleeping out is healthy, but it has its
disadvantages. A newly-wed St. Kilda
couple woke up in the dark hours the other
morning, finding the nuptial couch rather
chilly, and discovered that some person
unknown had invaded the verandah and
“pinched” from their bed a squirrel-skin
rug valued at £25.
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNA.


DEWAR'S
THE THREE GREAT TESTS
OF GOOD WHISKY ARE
QUALITY—TASTE—AGE
Quality is the Essence of Goodness and Fine
Blending. It conveys that indefinable distinc-
tion, which makes a man a prince among his
fellows, and Dewar’s a king among Whiskies.
Excellence in Distillation is a Science; Pre-
eminence in Blending an Art. Dewar’s is a
Mingling of Choice Spirits. Quality stands for
Character, and Dewar’s for the ideal com-
bination of both.
Taste. The Taste of Dewar’s is the Golden
Key which unlocks all the pleasant avenues
of the memory. In Dewar’s Whisky there is,
something creamy and yet not cloying;
something velvety and yet not heating;
something rich and yet not obtrusive; some-
thing crisp and yet not crude Strong and yet
gentle, Dewar’s is the Paladin of Whiskies.
Age is the Crowning Stage in a series of fer-
mentations. Fermentation means Life. Age
dissipates its roughness, rounds off its edges,
and refines its character, leaving nothing
behind but the Rarest Elixir of Life. Dewar’s
is the Veteran of Stimulants.. It is ripe for
Promotion to your Palate.

ETHEL ( very much annoyed with the
school-mistress): “Well, I hope the old
thing's young man goes to the war and
marries a hemigrant."
“Warre”: “A.G.B.’s” experience with
smut in barley was that of many wheatgrrowers last
season on Riverina. Carelessness in “pickling’’ is
the cause of the trouble. Steeping in half-bags does
not always mean that the brine gets to the centre of
the bag, and those who pickle otherwise invariably
put the grain back into a bag which has not been
“treated.” If the burst-spores are on the sack the
seed becomes re-infected. Unpickled seed sown on a
dry seedbed before the autumn rain very seldom shows
any signs of smut. Why? I don't know, but has
anyone seen a smutty self-sown crop ?
“Yarrum” : Here’s my instance of drunk’s
luck. One very dark night in June, a couple of
years ago, eight of us were out on a house-boat on
Lake Macquarie (N.S.W.). One of the party, a
publican, was the tightest man ever I saw trying to
fish, and I’ve seen some. He had a beautiful head
of hair and flowing whiskers. Presently he fell
overboard. 1 grabbed where I thought there was a
ripple, and landed a wig. Next grab I collared
genuine whiskers and we landed our man at the
other end of them. When he came round he told
us he had gone into a barber’s shop the day before
to have a clean shave, but was called out on urgent
business before the razarist had time to cut off the
life-lines.
* * * *
“Termite”: The assertion that white ants
kill trees in the Northern Territory is no more
correct than the argument that the Labor party’s
energy kills the foreign exploitation of Australia.
White ants never tackle a healthy tree; but once it
starts to decay the termite assails it. He who leaps
may discern the moral.
X- * * #
“Haldane” : I’m not going to vouch for
things as facts just because I hear them, but a few
weeks after Fox’s death in India I travelled from
Calcutta to Madras in the train with a Calcutta
chemist who had a share in financing Fox’s trials.
He seemed a fairly-truthful cove, and he swore that
Fox’s antidote was safe. Fox, he said, did not die at
once when bitten by the cobra. He was nipped at
10 o’clock in the morning, didn’t feel sick till three
in the afternoon, and died at 5 p.in. He was
bitten three times, but by carelessness missed one
wound, and treated only two. When he felt sick in
the afternoon he realised that it was good night. If
Fox’s care had been as thorough as his cure the Indian
chemist who financed him reckoned they would have
lifted the bonus for certain.
“Malice Root”: That old delinquent,
False Pretences, turned up again recently in the Port
Moresby district of Papua. A party of blacks in-
vited a neighboring tribe to a prime gorge. The
guests ran all the way thinking of nothing but what
the menu would be. They were the menu, and when
the last of them had disappeared and the strategists
were picking their teeth the unexpected happened in,
the shape of some John Hops. Lord only knows
where they got the clue from—found it in their beer
perhaps. In the clean-up patrol-officer Kirby and
two native police were killed. Now I hear that the
Government is forming an expedition to avenge
Kirby’s death. But there’s no mention of the dead
Johns. Are they protected now, or is the close
season over?
“Pick”: Among bullockies and shearers
west-o’-the-mountains (N.S.W.) queer matches are at
times the outcome of argument, but the chaps all
allowed that the dead finish was at hand when Fergu-
son backed his working bullock Baldy to take a quar-
ter start and race shearer Wright’s racehorse Greased
Lightning along a mile stretch of lane that led from
the Kilfera woolshed. The contest was arranged for
cut-out day, and every shearer, teamster, boundarv-
rider and dog turned out of the shade when saddling-
up time arrived. Odds of £lO to a tin of sardines
on Greased Lightning were treated with profane dis-
respect, but when a gun was fired to start the flutter
a revelation came to the horse supporters. Ferguson
had decorated his champion with a fanciful fringe of
tar-drums, kerosene-tins and hobble-chains, and when
Greased Lightning heard the fiendish pandemonium
of jangle occasioned by the laborious jig-jog of his
rival ahead, he turned and bolted towards Sunset, and
Baldv was whip-handled in an easy winner.

“F.H.R.”: To the man who follows pre-
carious ruining, bush contracting and such-like jobs
a permanent residence is out of the question, so let
him leani how I make a temporary house that fulfils
its purpose, can be rolled up and put on a cart when
the shifting is not too distant, or left behind at little
financial loss. Materials are two forks, one long,
strong ridge-pole, two corresponding side-poles, and a
bag fly large enough to cover. The side-poles are
placed in forks about sft. from the ground, and the
fly when stretched over the ridge and side-poles must
be tacked to other poles laid on the ground to ensure
tightness. One end of the mansion is filled up by
sewing in bags, the other contains the fireplace and
door, and a tent may be rigged for a bedroom. A
bag roof is perfectly waterproof as long as the fly
touches nothing but the ridge and sides. The cost of
a snug camp like this would not be more than, say,
ten shillings, and my evidence as to its efficiency is
that my wife and I and three small children lived a
winter in one at a mine near Orange and felt no
inconvenience even when the snows were frequent.
* * * *

THE SPREE ON SODA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN .]
The pubs were closed, and naught was left
But gingerbeer and tea,
IVhen Jones spoke out those magic words:
“Come, have a drink with me!”
It onlj' made us angry then;
A jest lie seemed to fling
About a circumstance that grief
Had made a sacred thing.
Brown leaned against a cabhorse near,
And wept salt, bitter tears;
While Blithers said a man who joked
Like that deserved six years!
But Jones declared he did not jest;
He gave a knowing wink.
“Come, boys,” he said, “I really know
Where we can get a drink !”
Still, when he led us to a shop
Where gingerbeer was rife,
Our righteous wrath was so immense
I feared for Jones’s life.
He calmly called for sodas—while
We raved about his sin—
And then he drew three bottles forth
And poured some whisky in.
i
In vain the soda-seller raved;
His anger we defied.
We drank his sodas by the score—
With whisky fortified !
The hours flew by on speedy wings;
And, finding all his fuss
Was no avail to get us out,
The Dago drank with us.
Then, when the whisky all was spent,
Like happy, sporting elves,
Home on our whiskied way we went,
And all mislaid ourselves.
With dawn I woke, to find my head
Upon a cabbage laid ;
While apples and tomatoes ripe
My bulging clothes arrayed.
In town I passed the Dago shop,
And in the window saw
Some prawn-lieads and a tray that held
An apple in its claw.
And three fat bottles stood upright
To tell the glad night’s tale;
'They looked as if they advertised
That whisky was for sale!
Within, a green-faced Dago spoke
To a policeman grim,
B ho, by the doubt upon his face,
Was not believing him.
1 don't know what ’twas all about,
But loud that Dago swore,
As with his waving hand and head
The atmosphere lie tore.
Of course, I did not interfere,
Bufj left him to his fate.
What right has any man to sell
Dull soda after eight?

“L.E.”: What race produced the boom-
erang? Professor Stirling, of Adelaide Museum,
maintains that long-past, ages knew the implement,
but he can say nothing definite as to its origin.
There,are Indian boomerangs of iron and wood whose
“returning” qualities are, however, not ascertainable.
These date back several centuries. Quite the most
interesting exhibit in the Adelaide Museum is a boom-
erang which Prof. Stirling brought with him from
England about 10 years ago. It is of wood, and
was taken out of an Egyptian tomb. It is exactly
similar in shape and size, in fact in every particular,
to the Australian weapon. The tomb had been traced
to the Gebelen XI. dynasty, a period about 2700 to
2500 before the birth of Christ. Professor Stirling
wonders whether the existence of the boomerang in
Egypt, India and Australia may be taken as an indica-
tion of some far-distant communication between the
peoples of those three countries. That clue may yet
lead to the discovery of the true origin of the Aus-
tralian aborigines.
A SAFE BET.
NURSE: “I think little Rebecca grows more
like you, every day, madam. I mean no
disrespect to the master, ma’am, but his
face is scarcely salgble for a lady, is
it now?”
“Rail”: Re “Sliprail’s” flock of Border
Leicester ewes that averaged 200 per cent, of lambs
(B. 4/5/’l6). I can beat him out of sight, being
the owner of a flock that averaged 250 per cent. One
of them has twins and the other triplets. The flock
is not a large one, as you can see, but I would not
mind betting that it is nearly as big as “Sliprail's.”
“Sassafras”: Let me enter my two best
nags for the long-distance jaunt. I rode a full-
blooded brumby (caught on the A. A. Co.’s estate,
near Cooplacurrapa) from the Manning to the Macleay
rivers (N.S.W.), distance 94 miles, taking from
early morn till sunset, and the conveyance finished up
game. He refused corn or hand-feed of any descrip-
tion, and never had a shoe on his feet. He was 16
years old when this happened, and I chased stock
with him long after he had turned 20. The other
competitor was a Blarney mare, well known on the
Northern Rivers of N.S.W. My brother rode her, on
very urgent business, from the Clarence to the Rich-
mond, 87 miles, in the day, and next morning she
had a son and heir that afterwards- turned out a
topnotcher on the track. How is it that the horse
of to-day is so inferior to the horse of 20 years ago
as regards stamina, speed and faithfulness?
* * * *
“Catamain” : I ouce saw a girl of a very
hysterical temperament standing absolutely silent
and motionless, with every muscle rigid, staring into
a tree. She was a perfect imitation of a statue of
Terror. Speaking to her and even a mild shaking
had no effect. Following the direction of her
gaze, I saw a green tree-snake with his eyes fixed on
hers, as motionless and apparently as terrified as the
girl- An obstacle placed in front of her eyes broke
the tension and she immediately went limp and
started to scream. The snake hurried off as fast as
his ancestor did the day that Adam came home un-
expectedly and reached for his gun. “Axehandle”
tells of a mesmerised fowl (B. 4/6/’IG) standing
with wings drooped and beak held to the ground. If
a fowl is held so that its beak is touching some sur-
face and a chalk-line is drawn ahead of it from the
tip of the beak, it will remain for some time rigid
in the same attitude.
“Hay” : A little to the west of Broken
Hill, just inside the South Australian boundary, is a
small, conical hill with a little watercourse running*
down one side of it. To judge from
appearances it was the water which
first exposed a vein of red ochre and
a parallel vein of white material like
kaolin. Even in the early ’7o’s
Binghi had been mining there for
supplies of pigment for very many
years. This ochre mine used to be
visited annually by the blacks from
the Lake Frome district,
and the local or Kill,
parra tribe were very
much afraid of the Pur-
nappa, as they called
their warlike saltwater
visitors, who came very
quietly and dug out as
much red and white clay
as they wanted, and, after
kneading it into damper-
like cakes, took their de-
parture and anything else
they fancied without
meeting any opposition
from the Killparras. Once
when a boy of 17 I was
sent to bring in some
horses, and had to camp
in an Outback hut for
the night not far from
the local blacks’ camp.
Tt was just at the time
iv he n the saltwater
blacks had arrived to
grub out their year’s
supply of warpaint. .Tust
as I was about to put
out the slush-lamp the
door of the hut was
pushed open and in
walked a local gin. She
was trying to explain
that she was afraid of
the Purnappa, when in
strode a warrior about
Oft. high, straight as a
rush, armed with a nulla
and shield, and beauti-
fully dressed in stripes of
red and white clay. He
took no notice of me,
but spoke to the gin in
his own lingo. I picked
up an old shovel and told
them fiercely to get out,
while I quaked inwardly.
They left and shut the
door after them. I kept
it shut by bracing it up witli- everything movable I
could find. At daylight, to my relief, both local
and visiting blacks had departed.
“M.M.”: In answer to inquiries in The
Bulletin' re the word lubra. It belongs to the
dialect spoken by the abos. who had their quarters
on the Lower Murray and at Milang on Lake Alexan-
drina. It was supposed to mean wife only, but I
have heard the gins calling their men lubras. The
tribes who used the word called their place of abode
wurley, and not mia-mia, as in X. S. Wales.
“Sniktaw”: Smut is propagated by spores
from affected black ears becoming attached to the
seed barley. “Pickling” the seed grain is the best
remedy. As germicides “A.G.8.” should try copper
sulphate, ljlb. in 10 gallons of water (immersion for
three minutes), or formalin (40 p.e.) at the rate of
lib. of liquid to 45-50 gallons of water. In the latter
method treat one day and allow the grain to remain
piled up overnight so as to give the fumes time to
get to work. The main point is to see that every
grain is thoroughly wetted, and this is best done by
soaking, not spraying, the solution over a heap of the
grain.
COMPLIMENTS.
THE BORE: “D'yer know, Miss Jean, I passed your place yesterday?"
MISS JEAN : “Oh, thank you.”

“Baijoe”: If the Abo. court is still hand-
ing out licenses for pubs with quaint names let me
nominate the Green Gate Inn, which stands at the
north-west corner of Orange (N.S.W.). Years ago
it displayed the sign of a green-painted gate with the
legend:—■
This gate hangs well and hinges none,
Refresh and pay and travel on.
“Alex. Outback”: At Burrinjuck dam
(N.S.W.) some good hauls of fish are made at times
in an original way. The fish get fooling about, in
what the teacher of the little school calls “the
immediate vicinity” of the pipes, and are sucked in,
literally as well as figuratively. I have seen incau-
tious cod up to 301 b. in weight thus caught. (N.B. —
Burrinjuck is the place where “Jimmie Pannikin”
Sherlockholmesed the foxes burying hen eggs in the
ploughed-up potato patches for future reference.)
* * * *
“F.H.”: “Hambidge” (B. 1/G/’l6) tells
of his cat’s passion for pickled beetroot. I, too, have
a cat of curious appetites—a black-and-white animal
very much like Souter’s, but of better habits, since
he never comes into the house and listens around.
Cockchafer grubs were plentiful in this district last
year. Whenever I went into the garden to get a
few ounces off by digging a row of potatoes the cat
came with me. Almost' every turnover of the fork
showed one or two grubs—fat, whitey-grey things
about the size of a thumbnail —and not one got
past The Vicar. When I had done my dash and The
Vicar had eaten 6doz. or so, he would stay there by
himself and go on mining for them in the loose
soil. This year, there being but few grubs about, lie
has taken to moths, chasing them in the twilight
and eating ’em, feathers and all.
* * * *
“Miner”: Passing the Pinnacles I met
Potts of Pingin. lie told me I could get water ahead
at an old gold-mine and that there was a haunted
house I could camp in if I liked. Potts likes his
litile joke. At dusk I reached the place, found water
and fixed my horse up comfortably for the night.
The house had a lonely look, so, after all, I decided
to camp near the water. At midnight it started to
rain, so I made for the cottage with my nap, and
entered. As I dropped my blankets I heard a slight
noise from the opposite room and turning quickly
saw something white dart through the doorway bv
which I entered the house. “No good to me,” I said,
and grabbing my kit I transferred to the bough-shed
where my horse was tethered. I was up at daylight,
just in time to see something coming out of the
scrub. It was a man, clad only in a flannel shirt,
and shivering in the morning air. “When did you
come here?” he said. “Last evening,” I answered.
“Did you come into the house about midnight?” “I
did.” “By cripes ! I took you for a ghost,” he said.
I told him he was very silly—there were no such
things as ghosts.

“Nick Bottom”: “Corio’s” 143 fencing-
posts out of one tree ought to constitute a record if
he means posts for a bull-paddock (9ft. long, 18in.
wide, and 6in. thick) ; otherwise it doesn’t. On the

DAN’S MEDICINE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN .]
The mildest bloke on Gopher Run
Was little Danny Lauder,
And when the fellows craved for fun
Of an ungodly order
’Twas usual for the smartest man
Their merriment to kindle
By-playing up on simple Dan
A most outrageous swindle.
One night when he was feeling ill
(Some trick the cook had played him),
For medicine a fearful swill
The graceless devils made him.
Painkiller was the base, a tin
Of oil that’s still more risky,
Then aloes, sheepwash, and some gin,
And half a pint of whisky.
They added someone’s hairwash, too,
And tar in weak solution.
Dan took this most infernal brew,
And drank the contribution.
He coughed and turned a trifle red,
But neither kicked nor cursed he.
He passed the cup. “Thanks, boys,” he said,
“Just one dose more— l’m thirsty!”

* * * *
The Vie. Railways Department has had
another interesting sale of unclaimed goods.
There was the usual large supply if trunks,
"ports." rugs and handbags, with gamps
galore and small goods too numerous to
mention. A few marquises had also left their
matildas behind iu their hurry to hit the
track again. But the most remarkable items
were a tip-dray and a 400-gallon tank. How
they happened to be left behind in the lug-
gage-rack the speculators who attend these
sales didn’t trouble to find ouu being too
busy at the moment trying t. • jnck up 50s.
worth of something else for 15s.
"Quidain”: If anyone-could find a profit-
able use for bracken fern he would soon be
able to afford nieat for breakfast. It is said
by some people to improve the laud for culti-
vation. and possibly it may. but it is such a
curse to get rid of that the improvement is
often more' than offset by the expense
of achieving it. Nothing, not even the
rabbit, appears to eat bracken: consequently
it increases continually, and countless acres
that formerly carried good grass are now
covered with it. In Gippsland and else-
where it is cut down with scrub hooks at a
cost of about ss. an acre, but as it takes
three cuttings before the roots die the clear-
ing runs to about 15s. an acre, and much of
the land it frequents in the Blue Mountains
is not worth that, though the aggregate value
is a large sum. It forms the best of harbors
for rabbits, snakes, rats and other pests, and
affords splendid material for bush fires,
which, while consuming its upper portion,
together with fences, crops, sheep and other
brie-a-br&e, only urge the roots to more
vigorous efforts.
* * * *
"Nym”: A new industry has been started
in the Wangaratta (Vic.) district, where
natural frog farms abound in the swamps
and billabongs adjacent to the Ovens and
King rivers. As the result of a demand by
Melbourne University for 500 frogs, fresh
and frisky, at 2ld. each, boys have been
doing brisk business for the local Red Cross
funds. Dunno what the frogs are wanted
for. but I’ve got a suspicion it won’t meet
with their approval.
* * ■» *
"Briner": Barraeouta is decidedly most
inferior sea-fish for eating. Yet the Port-
land (Vic-.) Freezing Works have just begun
tinning it. and the finished article is excel-
lent. Chief Inspector of Fisheries Lewis
joins in praise of the toothsome transforma-
tion. for which I predict a big demand when
the lean days arrive. But why can’t we
use our canneries on the rock cod,
which is better when smoked than any im-
i*ort? Our pilchards, too. are identical with
the valued Fx-ench sardine and much
superior to the Norwegian sprat which the
Australia grocer shoves over the counter.
We are nearer a meat famine than ever we
were, and now’s the time to push these sea-
ii-dustries of our-.
"0.W.": The Labor party was going to
run the savings banks differently when it
got into power; but the same old cumber-
some. inconvenient, out-of-date ideas still
prevail. To hank or withdraw one must
produce the passbook, and depositors are
expressly warned that as no other receipt
is given it should be kept under lock and
key. If anyone wishes to deposit money in
a private bank to a depositor’s credit, all he
has to do is to walk iu and do it, and he
can show the depositor the deposit siip
later as proof. Whether he does so or not
the fact will be duly recorded in the depo-
sitor’s passbook at any time he cares to
take it to the bank. But to make a deposit
in a savings hank to Smith’s credit, one
must first obtain Smith's passbook, thereby
receiving definite information as to his
financial standing with the institution. If
the passbook is not returned by the bor-
rower. the owner will probably go broke or
mad while wrestling with the red tape that
ties his money up. The same intallible
methods for creating trouble and profanity
prevail in regard to withdrawals also, and
if one is any distance from a savings bank
the trouble is multiplied.
* * * *
“Sparrowhawk”: Keat's gentleman who
for the inconvenience of other people "set
sharp racks at work to pinch and peel,”
would cordially approve of au ingenious
device which has sprung full-armed from the
brain of -Webster, machine-made P.M.G. For
a moderate charge, never to be less than a
tanner, a telephone subscriber may engage
Central to transmit, “not less frequently than
once very hour,” a message to any other
subscriber who may be out of call at the
first time of asking. This means that Cen-
tral will ring every hour for 24 hours—
if it does not get its victim sooner or “unless
special circumstances warrant an extension
of this time." Therefore I have only to wait
until I know that the head of a family whose
every member I detest is away week-ending
to pay my sixpence on pretext of communi-
cating with him alone. He being of course
out of call Central will automatically give
that family Hell at intervals for 24 hours,
"unless special circumstances,” etc-. The In-
quisition. the Vehmgeric-ht. and the Council
of Ten are Mothers’ Guilds compared with
the I’.M.G.'s department.
* * * *
“Maori”: There has been a paper mill
on the Mataura Falls (M.L.) for over 30
years. In the beginning the works had a
chequered career, when various fibres were
being tested, and only rough brown paper
was turned out. largely from old cornsac-ks.
Now. they produce wrapping and printers’
and fine writing paper from local material.
Old sacks and bag stuffs of all grades are
purchased from merchants and farmers, and
a very important material used is a native
tussock called "snow grass." a reedy vege-
tation 3ft. to 4ft. in length, and of the
same botanical class as the bunchy tuss. >ck
that grows so abundantly on the Millic-ent,
Tantanoola and Rendelsham flats in S. Aus.
With 'the material at hand c-an’t we ask the
Maorilanders how to do the rest:
* * * *
There is a c-o. in Adelaide —the Austral —
making the best plaster of paris. Before
the German went mad Australia imported
its plaster from U.S.A. and Ilunville —
mostly Ilunville. The Adelaide people saw
Australia’s chance and jumped in: and,
having overcome the early technical diffi-
culties of manufacture, they are now busy
breaking down prejudice against locally-
made goids. Fortunately the plaster itself
has been helping them, so the works at
Port Adelaide are getting plenty to do. The
raw material is mostly gypsum, and there is
plenty of tbat of first-rate quality.
* i i F
"Hampden": Potato digging is ranked
among the unskilled industries. but there
is much more in it than meets the eye. An
experienced artist dips the fork in. and
apparently without any trouble at all
throws a large section of earth in front of
him in such a way that Australia falls
underneath, and the potatoes on top. Hav-
ing given a couple of scratches in the
cavity to secure any lost, stolen or stray
spuds, he does it again, and in a minute or
two has several drills neatly spread on top
of the ground. They are then picked
into kerosene tins, and placed in baes
large and small being kept separate. Them
retically it all seems as simple as sin Rut
let the newehum try, and he will find that
the wretched spuds always have a habit of
growing outside the large portion of
tralian earth his fork casts forth—alwavs
except two or three large ones, which im
pale themselves on the fork. Unfortunately"
bayonetting potatoes is not regarded bv the
profession as good practice, and the' boss
blames you for it. Personally I am in
dined to think that suicidal tendencies are
terribly prevalent among potatoes. I have
also observed that a large amount of hard
labor is necessary to properly expel all the
potatoes from each dug-out. The remark
able persistency with which they can
remain in a burrow is a matter that calls
for scientific explanation, and partly ex-
plains why, after I have smoothed out the
kinks in my spine and recuperated suffi-
ciently to walk home, I find that I have
filled three bags while other experts have
done 13.

gravel pump for trench warfare IB
20/1/T6). It would be a kindness to warn
the inventor not to waste too much time or
money on it, as I submitted the same pro-
l>osal to the War Office some months ago
Perhaps if the inventor came over here and
actually saw the trenches he might evolve
some other scheme more quickly than I
I spent six months gazing at the Turks
before I thought of it.
An unreasonable legal tradition has been
knocked on the head by the Supreme Court
of South Aus. Iu a judgment carrying
interest counsel asked to have it reckoned
at S per cent. —"the rate which it has been
the practice of the Court to allow.” There
seemed to be no excuse for it except tradi-
tion. and after a little argument the claim
was reduced to six. The other side said
that four is allowed in England, and five
might be a fair thing here. Judge Gordon,
having poor respect for bad tradition
allowed 51.

A SHORT DISCOIRSE CONCERNING THOSE
THINGS WHICH ARE TRULY EVERLASTING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN .]
Nations may fall and empires wane
And faith and freedom pass away.
Flesh may return to dust again
Ami cities crumble to decay,
The weariest prophets cease to wail.
The truest lovers break their vows,
Crops, students, health and merchants fail;
But somebody must milk the cows.
Hearts, cups and banks alike may break;
Live-, trains and trinkets may be lost:
Corns and bad debts combined may ntake
Existence something of a frost;
Age may come creeping on apace,
While care no peace of mind allows,
And eaeh year leave ji- less of grace,
But still we can't escape the cows.
Youth is but as a dream that Pees.
Joy is at best a transient boon.
And, still less permanent ihan thrse.
Our hair and teeth go all too soon.
Each little dog has but his day
Ami then to fate’s fell mandate bows,
But what untimely fate can stay
The immortality of cows.
Though r.-es droop ami violets die
Ami even dandelions fade.
Though nothing mortal may defy
The edge of Time's il.i'e.
Though all our human pride is is in
At length in lowly clay to house,
Serene and hearty still remain
Tfcote blanky everlasting cows.

COLD TEA FOR MA!
[FOR THE BULLETIN .]
The Iced is done! Ours is the shock!
Confound Cold Tea’s brown tricks!
r T«- Freedom shrieked for Nine O’clock,
While Slav’ry yelled ior Six!
Three thousand of Beer's Prussian Guard
Marked down Eleven! They
Were cm to pieces. It was hard
To see them swept away.
The Ten Brigade, the Nine Brigade,
Went down like Seven and Eight
Before the desert tribes arrayed
In tannin and in Hate!
Faith’s barrels burst before their spears;
The greedy sands drank deep
Of alcohol, and barmaids’ tears,
And stuff Drink’s stalwarts weep.
The hordes of Tea swept on like mad,
And no more could be done;
Though Freedom shrieked in bar and ad.,
Twas Sis and Slav'ry won!

ADELAIDE & BRISBANE.
Dumping Position
POLITICAL
POINTS
Are the two main political parties going
to change places altogether? Not three
years ago the Laborites had no time for
trusts, combines and pools. They were the
great thieves of the commercial world. Up
to the very moment when war broke out
“Billy” Hughes was yelling at the top of his
voice for power to deal with monopolies.
Since then William has spent a lot of time
fixing up a metal ring; and now Tudor, Min-
ister for Customs, asked by the Tasmanian
hop-growers for a duty, has recommended
them to form a pool instead.
WINTRY.
HE: “No, I don’t drive my car during war
time; I always walk.”
SHE: “I see —trying to get your feet warm.”
This month McGowen, Cann, Edden and
Nicholson will complete their 25 years’ ser-
vice in the N.S.W. Assembly. They were
all elected as Labor members, and how
successful they have been in releasing the
worker from the yoke of the capitalist may
be judged from the fact that to-day the
State is paying interest to the moneylender
on £130,000,000, whereas 25 years ago the
debt to Cohen was only £67,000,000.
“Uncle James”: Those who talk about
“no conscription of life without conscrip-
tion of wealth” miss one all-important point.
In the case of the man, whether rich or poor,
who returns from the Front, his life has not
been conscripted. The authors of the above
cry, to be logical, should during the war call
for the conscription of men and of all in-
terest derived from property, and should,
when the war is over and the conscripted
men are set free, call upon the Government
to levy no taxes, but only to accept what is
given them voluntarily by the more patriotic
of its citizens.
There is nothing for it but to hang Trivett,
the N. S. Wales Government Statistician. At
the very moment when he ought to be show-
ing that the enormous loans of the Labor
Ministry have been so well spent that the
surplus profit has reduced taxation, the
absurd man issues a table to show that
N. S. Wales State taxation has increased
from 15s. 7d. per head in 1911 to £1 14s. 10? d.
per head in 1915. The progress in the last
three years has been as follows: —1913,
19s. 2d.; 1914, £1 Bs. 9Jd.; 1915, £1 14s. 10M.
The Government is getting such wretched
value for the millions it is spending that the
works simply can’t earn their own interest;
and ; that means increased taxation to make
good the deficit.
Maoriland's Premier stumbled against a
large-sized truth the other day. There was
a local yell about shortage of paper, and
someone remarked that to make matters
worse the supply for Hansard was running
out. “No matter,” said Massey, “we would
be better without it.” The thing represents
a wicked waste of public money—this labori-
ous reporting and publication of miles of
blither. In Maoriland every member of the
Legislature gets 75 copies of the wretched
issue free, and the Post Office carries the
stuff gratis all over the country. The cost
runs into thousands' a year, and the. only
people who read Hansard are politicians
looking for something old to dig up against
an enemy and hayseeds anxious to discover
if their member is battling hard enough for
that culvert he promised. ,
Last week the Commonwealth Government
turned in its sleep and remembered that it
already had a Compulsory Service Act on
the premises. In 1909, the first Fisher Gov-
ernment being in power, universal service
was such a ..democratic thing that the Labor
party took all the credit which it earned for
passing the principle into Law. Since then,
however, the occasion for service has actu-
ally arisen, and a lot of the people who were
prepared to make political capital out of the
party’s action have suddenly discovered that
universal service is a shamefully undemo-
cratic thing. This leaves a nasty taste in
the mouth, suggesting that, at rock-bottom,
what some people object to isn’t conscrip-
tion, but the personal inconvenience of being
shot at.
From an interview with Admiral Mat-
sumura, in command of the Jap warships
which have just visited Sydney: —
“Japan is not looking for open spaces, except in
regard to trade. Her people are happy, and there
is plenty of room for them to expand in Korea, in
Manchuria and in Formosa. Saghalien is too cold,
and the Pacific islands are too hot.. So long as
there is peace, and so long as civilisation, with its
peaceful avenhes of trade, is not threatened, Japan
will be satisfied. The Japanese do not want to
settle in America, in Australia, or in any other
country in large numbers —we want to keep our
own people in Japan. Australia,” the Admiral
remarked, with a shrug and a smile, “is too expen-
sive.”
That is just it. It is not because of any dis-
like or any discourtesy that Australia says:
“We must protect ourselves with a heavy
Customs duty—against Japan as well as
others.” It is because Australia is “too ex-
pensive” : the cost of living is so high that
without that protection there can be no hope
of local manufacture. So Japan may under-
stand many things which hitherto may have
seemed to her mere offensiveness.
Our soldiers have been great, but our poli-
ticians are splendid. How they battle for
the Empire in its hour of trial and call on
every citizen to put his right leg foremost
that victory may be achieved! Look, for
instance, at Holman pleading with J". Bull
not to be downcast, but to lend him a few
millions that triumph may be secured to our
arms by continuing the beneficent works of
peace at Condobolin and Cootamundra. And
Hughes—backwoodsman and boundary-
rider —is helping the weary Atlas to carry
the too vast orb by negotiating another
loan. And Ryan, of Bananaland, whose
patriotism shines through the clouds of
battle and lights the path of progress
towards Liberty. There are no pettifogging
ideas about Ryan. He sees things in a big
way. “Let me have a few millions,” he says,
in the simple language of Australia. “Let
me build more railways, so that no one who
votes for me need be without a job while the
money lasts.”
There is talk of a peerage for one English
gentleman who has made half a million out
of explosives. Who says the profits have no
honor in their own country?
According to Knibbs 1,301,000 days’ work
was lost through strikes in N. S. Wales
alone during 1934 and 10i5. The result, so
far as the State is concerned, is exactly the
same as if the Government took 3565 men
out of employment and kept them and their
families in complete idleness for a year,
maintaining them out of the nublic funds.
In that fact lies the State’s complete justifi-
cation for declaring strikes illegal and pun-
ishing the striker.
TOO MUCH HOSPITALITY.
“And did you men meet with much hospi-
tality in Egypt?”
"Faith, too much aw it! I teas in one ward
or another nearly all me toime.”
The strength of the Westralian Parliamen-
tary Labor party lies in the nine pocket
boroughs representing the faded and fading
goldfields of Coolgardie, Cue, Kanowna,
Menzies, Leonora, Mt. Magnet, Mt. Mar-
garet, Murchison and Yilgarn. The rolls for
this group show a total voting strength of
14,276, or an average of just under 1600
per member. Even this is flattering, as there
has been a steady exodus since the last com-
pilation of rolls. The metropolitan area has
12 representatives for 70,200 electors, while
the agricultural districts and Golden Mile
average between 3000 and 4200 votes per
member. The situation demands a Redistri-
bution Bill. While conditions like this pre-
vail one man one vote is a mockery. The
anomaly is that a Redistribution Bill re-
quires an absolute majority of the House;
which is like putting the accusedrin the jury-
box. The redistribution should of course be
automatic, and the House should have
nothing to do with it.
The Westralian Labor Congress, recently
concluded at Kalgoorlie, kept the “hostile
press” outside the door, but the official sum-
mary revealed that the position of the State
trading enterprises gave some of the dele-
gates sinking feeling. Works Minister
Angwin pleaded patheticallv for more work
for the wages,the State paid. In the imple-
ment works alone the Government paid £3OOO
a year for holidays, he said ; and the brick-
works had closed down the previous week
because the men had struck for higher wages
when the Government was already up against
a mountainous deficit. Of course, the Con-
ference couldn’t pledge the men to put more
ginger' into it; all it could say was that,
ginger or no ginger, deficit or surplus, the
State should go on extending the system of
nationalisation, even to the carving of emu
eggs and the manufacture of babies’ com-
forters.
There was really no need for the “econ-
omy” circular issued to Commonwealth de-
partments by the Acting- Prime Minister.
Federal and State Ministries have for a
long time been convinced that the affairs of
Australia are administered with the greatest
care, the outlay of every Id. being carefully
examined by multitudinous officials. Any-
how, thev have frequently said so. The
Defence Department, for instance, has got
full value for the millions it has spent this
last couple of years. Also there has been no
waste at Canberra or in the Post Office. In
addition to all this conservation of funds
there are the further examples of prudence,
not to say parsimony, afforded by seven
Cocked Hats, fourteen Houses, 800" M’s.P.
and multitudinous flunkeys. As long as Aus-
tralia has these, the population can be satis-
fied that the politicians are keeping an eye
on the pence and officialdom is watching the
pounds.

THE GREAT UPLIFT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN .]
Von Moltkc and Kitchener held the same opinion—
that without wars the human race would speedily
degenerate. It is by war that the virtues of courage
and endurance are maintained and man remains a
strong-willed firm-fibred animal. —Melbourne paper.
In enervating times of peace
The miners go below ;
To give the eager world increase
The farmer wields his hoe;
And unknown hills the seekers tread
In everlasting quest of bread.
These face the warring elements,
Browbeat the" blazing sun,
Or pitch their thousand tiny tents
- Where ice-gift waters run ; t.
By forge and bench they strive, and yet
The world is quickened with their sweat.
But ’tis not so that men are made.
If left to peaceful shifts
The race must pass, a thing afraid,
On custom’s turgid drifts.
Not toil’s incessant pangs can give
The vital force, the will to live.
I see on far-stretched, tortured fields
A million mangled sons;
And still a mad-eyed monarch wields
The devastating guns.
I must applaud, though hearts be breaking,
For here is mankind in the making!
VICTORIA.
EDWARD DYSON.
