SPORTING
NOTIONS
Weights for the A.J.C. Epsom and Metro-
politan and for Melbourne and Caulfield Cups came
out on Tuesday. The rest is easy; all punters have
to do is to pick the winners and book them straight
out and in doubles and trebles with the long-suffering
Mac Moses. Then they can lay back in peace and
comfort and wait for settling-day—or the scratching
pen, or the crook leg, or anything else that matters.
Meanwhile there is an easy chance to make a
hatful by having a dream, and for anyone who hap-
pens not to be acquainted with all the nags engaged
there is always the illumination of the “training
notes” in the daily papers to show how things are
going. In the Epsom the fiying Woorak heads the
list with lOst. 41b., and has earned every pound of
the burden, for he is a miler of rare brilliancy, and
if the race were run on his day and the wav he likes
it the tonnage would not stop him. The little fellow
also stands top in the Metropolitan, but whether he
deserves that is another matter. The position in
these handicaps of Wallace Isinglass, the Spring
failure, is a bit strange. What did he ever do de-
manding that he should give weight over two miles
to Green Cap and be handicapped within 21b. of
Marculfus and lib. of Prince Bardolph? In the
Metropolitan Wallace Isinglass is weighted to be
lib. worse over a mile and a half than Kingsburgh ;
in the Caulfield Cup the estimate is that over this
distance the 4-year-old will be 81b. the better. The
weight-adjusters have taken no risks, anyhow, with
Wallalo, who has been apDortioned Bst. 61b. tor
each of the Cups and 9st. 31b. for the Metropolitan.
The return of this holiday-maker to the track will
be one of the interesting events of the season if he
is saddled up well. Also the handicapper has
reckoned it was up to him to keep an eye on the
imported Redfern, and for the Melbourne Cup has
handed him 9st. 61b., which is a fairly substantial
helping-.
Punters who rushed in early to back their
Grand National fancies, singly or in doubles, have
had more shocks than usual this year. Some of
them picked Grand Fleet for the Hurdle before the
weights appeared, but as soon as his list. 131 b. was
published he was scratched. The Maoriland horse,
Morning, had been talked of, but both horse and
trainer were dead and gone before the handicap came
to light. El Gallo, also of Maoriland, had a crowd
of fervent admirers, who said he was good enough
to win either Hurdle or Steeole, and particularly
tipned him for the Hurdle. He was scratched for
both events more than a week ago. Tim Doolan,
another Maorilander, who ran second for the Steeple
last vear, looked a very promising candidate —until
he lamed himself at Caulfield. The popular Booligal,
always a rheumatic subject, is showing new signs of
his old complaint; and three early fancies for the
G. N. Hurdle Race, Eishon. Von Homreigh and Sir
Prjm. went back in the market as the result of their
form subsequent to the weights coming out. . Of the
original favorites Jullunder (for the Hurdle) is about
the only one that has gained new friends within the
past three weeks.
* * * *
The next two Saturdays and the Wednes-
dav that comes between are Grand National w T eek at
Flemington, when the punter’s heart is in his mouth
every time his dead-bird rises at a fence, and in his
boots when it comes a cropper. The Hurdle Race
on this Saturday is not much less open to heated dis-
cussion than it was a week ago when Beccassine, Sir
Prim and others were mentioned in The Bulletin.
Si rice then Beccassine has won a Maiden Hurdle Race
at Williamstown, and if he can go three miles he
should win the big event'. Two years ago, when
Clpntaft -got home, and Lord Grey finished second,
thd running for 21 miles was made by Bill Brandon,
Beccassine and Sir Prim. Two of these pace-
makers aforesaid are likely starters for this year’s
National on almost the same terms as before. Bill
B. was an old stager in 1914, but Beccassine was a
mere youngster at the game. Both of them will
meet Lord Grey on better terms. The writer reckons
that Bee. is a better chance at the price than, say,
Jullundur, Merrimax, Hypo, Shot Out, Strifeness, Wis-
consin, Jack Rice and a dozen other possibilities. At
any rate, the good-looking son of Bobadil has a win-
some weight—9st. 61b.
* * * *
/The ancient steeplechase glories of Bulla-
w&rra were revived at Williamstown on Saturday. It
was his first reappearance in Australia after a lapse
of nearly three years, and the old champion, with
12st. 131 b. on his back, beat a moderate field hand-
somely. He may be even equal to the job of beating
Booligal and co. in the G. N. Steeple.
* * * *
The imported animal continues to leap
past the post first. Medley is the latest offender,
and it happened at Rosehill (Sydney) on Saturday
in the Rydalmere Mile after the books had been
frantically yelling 12 to 1. Medley was sent to. this
part of the world last year by ex-Australian trainer-
owner Dick Wootton, and has earned his oats. Vic-
toria Park trainer Tomlinson owns him, and since
the acquisition has won three races. Two of them
were at fixtures outside the 25-miles radius, and
hard-working pilot Lilyman was on top each time.
Tomlinson has had nothing good to feed since
Nuwara Eliza was given up as hopeless. That parti-
cular horse was always threatening to do things on a
big scale, but usually declined to carry out the
promise.
* * * *
Bookmaker Lou Abrahams had a turn of
luck at Rosehill on Saturday when his four-vear-old
gelding Lord Linnet annexed the Flying Welter. The
titled nag had been filling places with such con-
sistency that he became the desoair of the people
behind him. Not long ago Lord Linnet was regarded
as being essentially a sprinter, but somebody with a
say in the matter had a brain-wave which led him to
the conclusion that a bit of"distance was the prad’s
game. The inspiration was just short of being
correct, as Lord Linnet always found the last furlong
beyond him. That fact must have been responsible
for the horsfe starting at 14 to 1 on Saturday in the
six furlongs. He just scrambled home bv half a
neck. Abrahams is also the proprietor of Willow
Green and one or two others which are not nearly in
the same class.
* * * «
The day has gone when Billy McLachlan
had only to throw a leg over a racehorse for the nag
to be rushed by a confident populace imploring the
Bags to take their cash for a few minutes. He
doesn’t ride much these days, probably because —
well, out Randwick way there is a long string of
cottages each bearing the name of a horse which at
one time or other won a Cup in various parts of Aus-
tralia. But he had a ride at Rosehill (Sydney) on
Saturday and kicked Duckie along so fast in the
straight in the Granville Stakes that the filly had
two lengths to spare at the finish. The onlookers
cheer must have sounded like a voice from the long
ago to McLachlan. Wonder how much cheer
there would have been had Duckie been an out-
sider instead of favorite. Anyhow, Christian Moses
was not observed waving his hat.
* * * *
The Rosehill Jockey Club (Sydney) has
abandoned the theory that its course is wide enough
to accommodate a field of any size, . and in. future
when over 28 animals insist on having a go they
will be cut into two divisions. The number looks
excessive. Anyhow, why not leave the cash in one
lump and ballot the superfluous prads out?
* * * *
One of the new V.R.C. betting rules
simplifies a tangle which has bothered the betting
public for a long time: —“All place bets are (a) 1,
2,3, if there are eight or more starters; and (b)
1, 2, if there are seven starters or under. The odds
under (a) to be one-fourtli, and under (b) one-third
of the odds for a straight-out win.” Although some
bookmakers have paid these rates, the punter has in
the majority of cases been obliged to accept what-
ever the other side would disgorge. The fact that
starting-price odds are to be those of Melbourne
A rous helps to further clear the mist.
* * * *
Australian-born nags will have to hurry
up after the cash this spring-time, because there
will be a lot of strangers on the job and there may
soon be less to hurry up for. The English-bred
animal is getting ready in droves, and it looks as if
the Maori landers’ invasion may be considerable.
Trainers Mason and Quinlivan are coming oyer
with a bunch, and as a rule they have something
that can move with reasonable rapidity.
* * * *
It was mentioned on this page recently
that the old-time jockey Paddy Pigott, who rode
Chester in the Melbourne Cun and V.R.C. Derby, had
gone into Brisbane Hospital with an injured foot.
Since then Paddy has lost one of his stirrups for good.
The foot has been removed.
* * * *
At the recent meeting of the Bowen River
(Q.) Turf Club there were 14 events in the two days,
and A. W. H. Cunningham, of Strathmore station,
annexed nine of them right out, participated in a
dead heat, and had two seconds and a third. He
was after his last year’s- record when he won the
whole programme.
HARRY STONE,
Yankee pvg.
Heaven knows what was happening on
the premises, but a Brisbane paper the other day
burst out with the weights for the Casino races as
results. It looked all right, and top-weights never
had such a time before.
* * * *
Ivalgoorlie and Boulder Racing Clubs
(W.A.) have been having a rough time since the
war, mainly because two proprietary clubs there-
abouts run alternate weekly meetings all the year
round. When the time arrives for the registered
meetings most of the local cash has gone into hid-
ing. So, with a view to making something break,
the big clubs are going to start monthly programmes
and hand the profits over to soldiers’ relief funds.
Even if this arrangement doesn't break one or other
of the clubs, the public may be expected to go
bung, anyhow.
* * * *
The Peacock Ministry’s intention to further
reduce the number of days of racing in the Cabbage
Garden, particularly the threat to cut down trotting
from 20 to 10 days a year, brought a note of protest
from Melbourne’s one-time, but now almost forgotten,
music-hall favorite Billy Whitburn. William dwells
now at Clayton, beyond Oakleigh, on a small
“estate,” of which part is a training ground. His growl
was that the trotting horse is more useful to the
country than the galloper, and to cut any off the 20
days now allowed him to run is to practically wipe
him out. Which is probably true.
* * * *
Apropos a recent Bulletin par. about a
four-furlongs dash at Rosehill (Sydney) in the long
ago, in which a pony named Sambo played a prom-
inent part, the following, dug from a paper of June,
’92, is evidence that things were rather more raw
once than they are to-day: “About one of the coolest
pieces of business that I ever saw or heard of took
place over a pony race at Botany last Mondav. There
were six starters for the Bunnerong Handicap, a
pony called Sambo going out at evens. Getting
well away, Sambo had everything hopelessly beaten
at the end of the first furlong, and from that to the
finish it was 100 to one on him. Despite his long
lead, however, several of the bookmakers could be
heard offering odds against him, and this was the
case when he was 50 yards in front, and only a
furlong to go. Directly Sambo passed the post
these same men could be heard offering to take two
to one he was short weight. The rider, on weigh-
ing in, was found to be 81b. short weight, though
he weighed out correct. It is needless to say that
owner and pony were disqualified, the rider escap-
ing, as he, according to evidence, was in no way
guilty. That the weight had been purposely ex-
tracted was clearly shown by the bookmakers openly
offering to bet. Compared with such men, Ben
Hall and Gilbert were gentlemen.”
* * * *
‘Sol”: Wlmt is the use of blaming
trainers because a lot of the yearlings handed over
to them turn out failures? Owners send the animals
in to be trained, and trained they have to be. If a
man refuses to take them he loses hls face
Owners know the risks they are running. They face
them. They lose their cash or their horses, but that
is their business. Take any of the leading Uaineis
and look at the good horses they have turned out as
proof that they know their job.
To which this paper replies that the fact that a
man has turned out a good horse occasionally does
not show that he knows his job. The essential thing
to know is how many horses he has murdered, and
it was to get at this that The Bulletin suggested
an expansion of rating statistics. That, taking the
thing by and large, horses are murdered in the
training stables is absolutely certain, and in conse-
quence many of the supposed “facts relating to
breeding in Australia are wholly lllusionarv. the
racing of two-vear-olds is so excessive as to be a
scandal, if the matter is viewed from the horseflesh
point of view as distinct from that of breeders,
whose business is to make money by selling youngsters.
The question of trainers having to take the annual
crop of immature nags and prepare them has nothing
to do with the point at issue—whether the existing
system of training babies does not result in all-round
disaster to a huge percentage of animals. How many
of the two-year-olds raced all through the late season
will be worth anything? How many of the 20-
months-old foals in training with a view to the
spring will last? It is all sheer recklessness from
end to end. Owners are pitching money and horses
away, and trainers are wasting their talents —-those
who have any. And with the plentiful numbei
of track failures, combined with the excessive quan-
tity of subsequent stud failures, the truth has become
obvious to a lot of people.
* * * *
“Flarrison O.”: Of all the gee-gees in this
world —brown, chestnut, piebald, black —how is it
that the best bv far, the surest and the swiftest
are the ones I do not back? The matter long has
worried me, and in the stilly night I lie awake and
think it o’er, and in the afternoon once more I
strive to set it right. But though I get the best of
tips and information straight from stable sources all
my schemes for making coin go wrong ; it seems I’m
doomed to such a fate. Nags that as “dead birds
are described more oft are merely “dead” ; for e’en
the quick are “dead” at times in racing (curse
these frequent rhymes!)—thus punters are misled.
A horse kept “dark” may be a nark, and “sports”
have pawned their pants to back “dark horses”
which, alas! have proved white elephants. Some
nags that start at 2 to 1 don’t finish till two past
for verily to you I say: The first are often last.
Since Moses makes the laws which we poor sinners
must obey it matters not how our vote goes ; though
we may favor “ayes” or “noes,” the Nose will win
the day.
* * * *
There is one horse-owner in England,
always near the top of the list of winners, who
seemingly is going to romp in this season. This is
Edward Hulton, for whom Australian R. Wootton
used to train. He has already appropriated the two
big classics, the Derby and Oaks, with Fifinella, and
recently collared the handicap which has been sub-
stituted for the very popular Ascot Royal Hunt Cup
—usually one of England’s biggest betting races.
Its war-time title is the Cambridgeshire Hunt Plate,
and Hulton’s four-year-old Silver Tag, a half-sister
to Fifinella, was the winner. Hulton is a Lancashire
man who made his money in the printing and news-
paper business. His firm owns the Manchester
Chronicle, originally a Sunday paper, now published
daily—also a winner from the jump. Hulton started
business in London a few years ago and runs a
daily illustrated paper in opposition to the North-
cliffe firm. As it never rains but it pours, that
also makes enough money for its owner to enable
him to have a little bit on his “good things” occa-
sionally.
* * # *
Dave Smith interviewed Les Darcy at
Sydney Stadium on Saturday, and in the twelfth
collision caught a long hook on the chin, groped
wildly for the glove that stung him, and was dropped
by a hard right to the jaw. At no stage of the pro-
ceedings did Smith look like a winner, and it is
doubtful if he ever felt like one. Still he boxed
very well —at times brilliantly—but it was a rear-
guard action nearlv all the way. At the seventh
episode Smith began to weaken and hold on, spark-
ling up at times for brief countering efforts. He was
very tired when the end came. Darcy made few
attempts to box his opponent. He spent most of the
time looking for a chance to make a fight of it.
Smith has returned to the ring as good as ever he
was, and judiciously matched has it in him to do a
lot of damage yet. His next controversy is with Mr.
Buck Crouse (U.S.A.), and in that argument there
ought to be something really strenuous.
* * * *
Australia’s two top-notch feathers —Jimmy
Hill (N.S.W.) and Bert Spargo (Vic.) —will be
blown together by the force of circumstance at Syd-
ney on Saturday t& decide a trifling unpleasantness
which had not been settled by the referee at their
last meeting, when a verdict was given to Hill on a
foul. On that occasion Spargo seemed to have the
upper-hand until he made a mistake through excessive
eagerness. They are two very fine boxers, and can be
relied on to do what the imported article frequently
doesn’t. This paper has an idea that Jimmy will
have a busy evening.
* * * *
Though he had an advantage of 111 b.
thrown in it was a bewildered Mick King that faced
Fred Kay at Melbourne on Saturday night. King
would have been more at home with a streak of
lightning. Kay frequently stood sideways and belted
his left seriously into Michael’s forage department.
At other times he varied the unpleasantness with
rights to the chivvy. Whenever Michael would get
set to deliver a wallop Kay promptly evaporated, and
then before King could get the hang of what had
happened something would hit him. On rare occa-
sions King manoeuvred Kay in a corner, whereupon
the elusive young man wriggled away, had a spit,
bogged in and shoved off some more. The referee
naturally gave Kay the verdict, and the Kingites
naturally gave the referee a severe talking to. .
* * * ■*
Proficiency in the manly art of bash
doesn’t seem to be worth tuppence as a protection to
the pocket, for though Life is short and Time is
fleeting there is no class of men who make such a
bad job of hanging on to their cash as pugs. Of
course, the explanation is simple; but that is another
matter. About the central fact there is no doubt at
all, it being quite an exceptional pug who finishes
ahead of the game. This paper has a theory that
every pug during the period of his money-making
prime should be compelled by law to produce his
wad three times a day for inspection by a John Hop
armed with a warrant to arrest him for lunacy if
there is Id. short. There are a lot of pugs in
Australia who after making fair-sized heaps in the
ring are not taking up large blocks of War Loan
certificates. One or two of them may get back some
of the missing links with fortune, but the lives of
most of them merely make additional chapters in
“The Old Story Over Again,” a standard work which
20 years ago had reached page 9762, ohap. ccelvii
vol. 674. Since then an additional vol. has been
issued every five minutes.
* * * *
J. A. Prout, who had the largest appetite
on the first day of Queensland track and field cham
pionships last Saturday, is a versatile performer
He won the broad jump (20ft. was second
in the 440yds., and third in 120yds. hurdles' and
this accumulation of points lifted him into leading
position for the all-round championship. Inciden
tally, his legs helped Thompson Estate Harrier-
to sit in top place for the club premiership.
The memory of Jim Macmanamey’s work
for N.S.W. Rugby Union will be kept green by the
shields introduced into public-school football Mae
was a stalwart player in the days when the Union
flourished : then as president and referee he kept the
concern together. The Union never had a worker
whose toil better deserved recognition than that of
the big-hearted Major Jim.
Syd Stearnes, of Launceston (Tas.), isn't
one of the sports who could go to the Front but
don’t. He is too young—only 17. Nevertheless
the other day he whirled over the distance from
Hobart to Launceston ,(121 miles) on a 7h p
Indian motor-cycle in 2h. 32min. 20see., knocking
lmin. 10 sec. off the record lately put up by AV. H
King. Stearnes now claims the five-mile world’s
record of 4min. 40 2-ssec., the 50-mile Tas. record
of 51imin., and the 4-mile Tasmanian record of
27see. —not bad for a boy.
* * * *
Australian cyclist Bob Spears put another
feather on his wheel lately by defeating the great
Yankee flier Kramer in a match at Newark (U.S.A.).
Spears hails from Dubbo (N.S.W.), and before he
climbed aboard an outgoing boat was one of the finest
sprinters in this section of the world. The recent
win puts Spears in a place nice and handy to the
cashbox.
* * * *
Australian cyclists are not finding fault
with the financial side of pedalling in the U.S.A.
Besides the prize-money they annexed last season,
Spears, Goullet, McNamara, Grenda, Walker and
Clark cut up £3900 in promoters’ fees between them.
That ought to keep them in chewing-gum for a while.
* * * *
Apparently there is no way of making a
close game of billiards between Fred Lindrum and
Williams. They played another 18,000 up at Alcock’s
Parlor in Melbourne lately, with the proviso that
neither man should make more than 25 consecutive
“losers” off the red. The restriction was all in
favor of the Englishman, of course, but it didn’t
save him from a severe doing. Without making
any sensational breaks, such as ..brother Walter and
Maconachy scored off the red in their “all in’’
match, Lindrum beat Williams by a few thousand
points. Now he is tackling young Mac. in a
restricted game, giving a start of 3000.
* » * *
Though he wasn’t permitted by the mili-
tary authorities to get back into khaki after his
attack of appendicitis, swimmer hon.-lieut. Frank de
Beaurepaire is now in France among the boys of the
A.1.F., after all, listening to the guns by night and
dav and doing the usual duck when the time seems
ripe for ducking. He is there on behalf of the
Y.M.C.A.
* * * *
N.S.W. Cricket Association is determined
to do something new next season with its scoring
system. The scale of points will run this way:
For an outright win 4, win on first innings 3, draw
2, loss on first innings 1, outright loss 0. It is
explained that this is highly scientific. Another
scheme is to bestow a club championship based on
the records in the three grades. It all looks very
good, but .the interest in cricket is not going to
distract attention from Europe for a day or two.
* * * *
The oldest cricket identity in N. S. Wales
is George Moore, of West Maitland, who is 96 and
not out! He played in the N. S. Wales 22 against
Stephenson’s All England XI, the first English team
to visit Australia (1862). The locals won by 12
wickets. In the first innings George Moore’s analysis
showed 83 balls, nine maidens, 22 runs, four wickets.

Lieut. Wrixon, killed in France, was the
eldest son of H. J. Wrixon, a former Presi-

grandson of “Money’*'. Miller. The original
Victorian Wrixon was an old-time County

When Kitchener was in Maoriland he
had a close call in the Manawatu Gorge.
When motoring through he came to
a deep ravine and a small bridge which work-
men were repairing. The bridge had been
stripped, and the car was held up. While
“K.” was uncorking a few hints as to the
speediest method of replacing the (Jecking
he boarded a piece of loose stuff which tilted
with his weight, and there was a scramble
to avoid a whirlwind trip to the rocky
bottom.

How to Treat Your Hair and Complexion.
A Few Simple Beauty Hints.
By Mdlle. GABY DESLYS, the welhknown Parisian Actress.
YOU ask me for a few hints on the
treatment of the hair and complexion.
Well, the less “treatment” you give
the skin the better. I do not
believe much in massage, but a
little cream to the face is neces-
sary to counteract the effects of
wind or sun. What cream
would I recommend? Well, I
advise you to use a little mer-
colised wax every night and
again in the morning, after
washing the face. Rub it
gently into the skin, then wipe
off any superfluous wax and
dust a little barri-agar over
the face. - You will find that
this will be the only “treat-
ment” necessary and will keep
your face fresh and youthful-
looking for all your life. The mercolised
wax removes all the dead outer skin, so
that you have always a fair, fresh com-
plexion like a girl’s.
For the hair, the first and most important
Photo: Wrather <£■ Buys
thing is a good shampoo. Never use any:
thing inferior to wash the hair with. Get
some good stallax from your chemist and use
a teaspoonful in a cup of hot
water. Then rinse the hair
well and it will look bright and
gloss-y.
A tonic is necessary when the
hair is inclined to fall out more
than it should, and is always
good to use during the spring
and autumn. Then the hair
needs a little —what do you call
it V —stimulant, and for this 1
would advise you to get a packet
of boranium and mix it with
some bay rum ; dab this into the
roots and it will not only stop
the fall, but make your hair
grow long and thick. Give your
hair a good brushing every night, and that
will be all that you need do.
Blackheads Fly Away.
Instantaneous Remedy for Blackheads, Greasy Skin and Enlatged Pores.
A practically instantaneous remedy for
blackheads, greasy skins and enlarged pores’,
recently discovered, is now coming into
general use in the boudoir. It is very
simple, harmless and pleasant. Drop a
stymol tablet, obtained at the chemist’s, in
a tumblerful of hot water. After the. effer-
vescence has subsided bathe the face in the
liquid, using a small sponge or soft cloth.
In a few minutes dry the face and the offen-
sive blackheads will come right off on the
towel. Also the large oily pores immedi-
ately close up and efface themselves natu-
rally. The greasiness disappears and the
skin is left smooth, soft and cool. This
simple treatment is- then repented a few
times at intervals of four or five days, to
ensure the permanence of the result.
Grey Hair—Home Remedy.
An old-fashioned Recipe restores Youthful Appearance.
There are plenty of reasons why grey
hair is not desirable, and plenty of reasons
why hair dyes should not be used. But, on
the other hand, there is no reason why you
should have grey hair if you do not want
it. To turn the hair back to a natural color
is really a very simple matter. One has
only to get from the chemist two ounces of
concentrate of taminalite and mix it with
three ounces' of bay rum. Apply to the
hair with a small sponge for a few nights
and the greyness will gradually disappear.
This liquid is not sticky or greasy and does
not injure the hair in any way. It has been
used for generations with most satisfactory
results by those who have known the
formula.
To Kill Roots of Superfluous Hair.
The most Effective Formula ever Discovered.
Women annoyed with disfiguring growths
of superfluous hair wish to know not merely
how to temporarily remove the hair, but now
to kill the hair roots permanently. i* or
this purpose pure powdered pheminol may lie
applied directly to the objectionable hair
growth. The recommended treatment is
designed not only to instantly remove the
hair but also to actually h\ll the roots so that
the growth will not return. About an ounce
of pheminol, obtainable from the chemist,
should be sufficient.

Good News for Fat People.
Something New in Obesity Cures.
A Loudon chemist says: “The latest
method of reducing obesity certainly is far
more pleasant and convenient than all pre-
vious methods. It consists merely in eating
clynol berries. The fat person who wants
to reduce without the usual rigid diet, exer-
cise, sweating baths, etc., now puts a few
of these little brown berries in his or her
pocket and eats three or four each day.
Clynol berries not only eliminate fat from
the body, but also correct the tendency,
which is usually constitutional, to create
fatty matter. No discomfort whatever is
caused by their action ; in fact, except for
the loss of superfluous fat and the feeling
of “fitness” so created, you would not be
aware that these little berries were doing
their work.
Local inquiry shows that clynol berries are
not very well known in this country, but
the demand- is increasing daily, and any
chemist can quickly procure them if specially
requested to do so.

“Stargazer”: A well-known Australian
songstress who thinks she is (on the whole)
rather better than the greatest, was leading
a company in a Commonwealth concert
tour. At one Queensland hotel the bird
was exercising her throat in early-morning
trills. She vocalised with surpassing
energy in her room, which was one floor
up, and facing the street. Presently over
the verandah roof peered a wild-eyed, rough-
haired man. “Madam,” he said, “can I be
of any assistance to you?”
“Jwosp”: Only 43 plays have run more
than 500 performances on the London stage,
and of these 25 are musical shows and six
are farcical. None of the others worry
about the infernal triangle. Seven have
gone over. 700. “Charley’s Aunt” (1466)
leads, but for length of run “Our Boys”
has three months to the good. Then come “The
Chinese Honeymoon,” 1075; “Dorothy,’
931; “The Arcadians,” 809; “The Private
Secretary,” 785; “The Merry Widow,” 778;
“San Toy.” 775; “The Geisha,” 760; “A
Country Girl,” 729; “Les Cloches de Corne-
ville,” 705; “H.M.S. Pinafore,” 700. In
Australia “A Shop Girl” did nothing, but
London went to it 546 times, and saw “A
Message from Mars,” 544 times. “Peg o’
My Heart” is the latest to get on this
honors board with 520. The first produc-
tion in Britain to go over the 50 mark was
“The Beggar’s Opera,” in 1728, and it
raised Lavinia Fenton from rags to be
Duchess of Bolton. The first to beat the
century was “Tom and'Joey” at the Adelphi
in 1821. It was an adaptation of a book
called “Life in London.” Bayle Barnards
“Casco Bay,” in 1827, reached 140, and,
strangely enough, has never been revived.
It was passed two years later by Douglas
Jerrold’s “Black-Eyed Susan,” with 150.
T. P. Cooke played his part twice nightly—
at Covent Garden to Ellen Tree’s Susan, ana
at the Surrey to Miss Scott’s. The first
play of Master Will’s to pass the century
was Charles Kean’s “Henry VIII.” in 1800,
and the run was stopped by the end of the
season. It opened the following season with
50 more nights. In 1860 “The Fobcen
Bawn” blew everything late high with Z6X.
successive nights, and modern long runs date
from that point. In New York, by the
wav. “A Trip to Chinatown leads with
690; then “Adonis,” 651; ‘Teg o My
Heart,” 604.
“D.F.”: Interesting just now to note that
the first play ever staged in Australia was
called “The Recruiting Sergeant. It is
described as a comedy, however, while the
present version is a tragi-comedv. On the
Evening of .Tune 4. 1789, the birthday of
Kino- George 111., the not-yet-18-months-old
Sydney saw its first play. The contemporary
critic Collins says: '
Some of the convicts were permitted to perform
Farquhar’s comedy, “The Recruiting Sergeant, in
a hut fitted up for the occasion. They professed no
higher aim than “humbly to excite a smile, and
their efforts to please were not unattended with
applause.
“Harrison O.” : I have just sampled Wish
Wvnne at Melbourne Bijou, and found her
the best of Wishes and a meritorious
Wynne. As an artist in character, as dis-
tinct from caricature, she is entitled to any-
thing her press-agent may say of her. Bhe
ia the first stage performer I have seen who
presents the common or crockery-breaking
Martha with absolute fidelity, and her truth
is funnier than others’ fiction. Restraint is
the keynote of her work, yet she “gets there ’
every time, chiefly, it seems to me, because
she lias a large human sympathy that gives
her a real affection for the types she is
guving, and which has helped so many
artists to success, from Chaucer to C. J.
Dennis.
THE ORCHESTRAL CONCERT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN .]
To Melbourne William Fenn had come
Upon his yearly burst;
Me looked upon the beer when brown,
And sought with foaming pints to drown
A really gorgeous thirst.
At night the Town Hall Fenn descried
Lit up, and paid to go inside.
The orchestra was in its place;
A man, long-haired and thin,
A baton waved and straight away
The wood and string began to play,
And soon, with noisy din,
The trombones and the cornets tried
Like mad to beat the other side.
They made a most impressive row.
Euphoniums blared scorn,
And then, with cold dislike imbued,
The big bassoon said something rude
That roused the silent horn;
Whereat the whole dashed crowd joined in
And drowned the argument with din.
Things went more smoothly for a while,
But soon broke loose again.
Till half-past eight Bill thought it fine;
But when it. got to after nine,
And still they kept on, Fenn
Arose in wrath, demanding, “Shay,
When do yo’ slitart th ’ btanky play?”
VICTORIA.

J.F.A.: That joke is earning a lot of overtime now.
When Australians wanted flour, vegetable
and even some farm seeds they generally, in
the past, sent around the world for them;
hut it’s such a job to get the imported stuff
now that a few men in the business are
thinking of trying to actually grow the seeds
in the Commonwealth. They have got hold
of. 100 acres near Hobart, and if Treasurer
Higgs will let them raise the capital—as, of
course, he will —they mean to get busy on it
and see if it will grow turnip seed and peas
and beans and phlox and one thing and
another. If the venture goes all right they
propose then to get the local inhabitants to
come into the scheme. The co. will supply
the seed to start with, supervise the cultiva-
tion, and buy the crop. As the few seeds
which are grown in the Speck have a big
reputation on the mainland—Tasmanian
asters, for instance —the scheme looks good,
and worth this paper’s blessing.

IN BANANA LAND
Undeterred by the piffling detail that Aus-
tralia is growing less sugar than is required
for local needs and acquires the remainder
from Formosa, Ryan, talking in London,
asks J. Bull to explain why he doesn’t supply
himself from Bananaland. Why does Lon-
don buy cheap German sugar instead of
cultivating cane in Bananaland? It is a
question that ought to be answered. And
the responsibility of Answering it lies more
with T. Ryan than with J. Bull.
The Full Court’s decision, that the Act
passed last session did not give the Stale
insurance office a monopoly of Queensland s
workmen’s compensation (accident) busi-
ness, hasn’t settled the trouble; and Costs
still has hopes. Companies before being
permitted to compete with the State office
must get the permission of the Governor-m-
Council; and so far, though many have
applied for it, none have got it. The cos.
are now taking opinion as to whether per-
mission can be refused without reasonable
cause. What the people, for their _ part,
might consider is whether the salaries of
Ministers and M’s.P. shouldn’t be charged
with all this law expense as a punishment
for passing such a muddled law.
“Cato”: A storm is raging about the A ar-
sity Senate elections. Graduates elect 10
members and the Government nominates 10.
Certain meds., lawyers and parsons writhe
in agony lest Labor nominate “hewers ot
wood and drawers of water.” A caucus of
the cultured has issued a circular asking for
votes for its selection ; but old members of
the Senate like R. H. Roe and J. L. Wool-
cock are not in the circular. The idea is
that, if Culture wins, Labor will practically
be compelled to nominate the old members.
The result would be a Senate of the truest
blue. The scheme was working beautifully
till a newspaper got hold of the circular.
Now the smile is on the face of the tiger.
BROTHER BEST GIVES OUT THE HYMN.
THE EIDER: “Brethren and sistern, we will now all yewnite in singin that beautiful
yinn, ‘Man Wants but Little Ear Bclow, Nor Wants that Little Long!”
“McNorth”: The right to set a trap-gun
with the object of blowing an in-
truder to the hereafter was contested at the
recent sittings of the Cooktown District
Court. A troublesome Binghi had developed
the habit of breaking into the humpy of a
miner in an outlying portion of the Cohen
Goldfield, Cape York, whilst the miner was
at his claim a mile or two away. The
miner wearied of the repeated thefts, and
at last fixed up a gun to pepper the intruder.
He also stuck up a big notice of warning,
but as Binghi couldn’t read, his next attempt
to burgle the humpy was his last. The
miner was charged with manslaughter. For
the defence it was claimed that anyone may
set a trap-gun in the night-time for the pro-
tection of his property. Binghi, in his dying
depositions, had said that he was shot some
time “before breakfast,” and as “before
breakfast” might mean any time since sup-
per the previous night, there was no proof
that the gun had been discharged in the day-
time. The Court agreed and the miner was
acquitted; but he was feeling very thankful
that Binghi was an early riser.
To June the Twenty-first.
[FOR THE BULLETIN .]
Shortest day of all the year,
When the misty, humid tear
Slowly falls from Norfolk pines,
Surest of the wintry Signs;
Weeping like a schoolboy's nose,
As the weary, short day goes;
Clear, Cold nights and watery moon—
Good-bye, twenty-first of June!
Morningsunlight’s cold and wan ;
Summer ‘seems for ever gone;
Cold the nose of him who dares
Daybreaks, full of polar bears;
Nights of winter that remind
Us there’s chopped ice in the wind,
Of our good blood-heat the thief,
Spoiling our best handkerchief.
Mallee roots in grates a boon —•
Good-bye, twenty-first of June I
Broken Hill sees flakes ..of snow-
Where shall we for summer go?
Melbourne’s eyes are watering still,
It is cold in Wowserville;
Bleak the air at Ballarat,
Freezing dog and freezing cat.
Hear the snow-gnome tell his rune —■
Good-bye, twenty-first of June!
Zeehan holds it holiday
If the rain but stays away;
Over frozen button grass
Hear the wind from Cape Horn pass;
ltocks are goosefleshed in the sea,
Chattering reefs of Reniine;
And the sperm whale plans to blow
In the streets of Bendigo ;
And the walrus takes his seat
In the train for Burrumbeet;
Icebergs crash a splintered tune—•
Good-bye, twenty-first of June 1
Colder, shorter hours to sin
Make it easier Heaven to win;
Who can be a bad, mad lad,
If he be not warm and glad?
When the summer blisters paint,
Hard it is to be a saint;
But, when winds are cold and keen,
Many a martyr I have been;
I could in a cloister croon—
Good-bye, twenty-first of June 1
Warmed am I with memories
Of the splendid northern seas,
Where no cold, imported snow
Comes, nor alien winds can blow;
Where Australian weather stays
Every year for all the days;
Where pineapples send their scent
To the deep blue firmament.
Come no polar bears to shock ;
Glad snakes frizzle on the rock ;
Haste we north to life and noon —•
Good-bye, twenty-first of June!
There the naked Binghi sprawls,
Listening to the schooner’s pawls,
When the anchor comes apeak,
Near the Islands of the Week.
Icebergs may not dare the fates
Of the wondrous Torres Straits;
Let Cape Horn its curses spill
In the streets of Wowserville;
We shall watch the palm trees sway
Though it be the shortest day.
VICTORIA.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, June 24, 1910.
My Dear Thargomindati,—
His Ex. and camp-followers are week-
ending in Rockhampton and inspecting the
annual collection of poddies and pumpkins.
He has promised to come home on A eclnes-
day.
Arthur Morgan, who has been running
his Ex.’s job as a side-lme, bestowed
official patronage upon a kindergarten turn-
out at the Stadium on Thursday. ihe
battle-ring was judiciously disguised as a
model play-school for the occasion. Dur-
ing the show several gents, with well-
developed jaws wandered in. and after gaz-
ing at the invasion in a dazed manner made
a dash for the exits. The Arthur Morgan
lady beamed upon the infants, and every-
body seemed satisfied.
Acting-Premier Theodore opened a fete
this afternoon in aid of the Catholic Club
and Catholic Military Tents. Fortune-
tellers, art unions and a mixture of sweets,
cushions, hair tidies and vegetables were
thrust upon the hapless visitor.
Colonel J. F. G. Foxton, kindliest and
most courteous of gentlemen, died on Fri-
day afternoon. For 21 years he represented
Carnarvon in the State Parliament, w r as
Minister for Lands in three Ministries and
Home Sec. in tw 7 o others. Then he went to
the House of Reps., stayed there for four
years, and was Minister without portfolio
in the third Deakin Ministry. Ilis biggest
job w r as as Australian representative in the
Imperial Defence Conference. A solicitor
by profession, he was a member of the firm
of Foxton, Hobbs and Macnisli. Out
of office hours he was a keen sol-
dier, and in pre-Federal days did much
to make this State’s volunteers efficient.
Tw'O awards from the Royal Life Saving
Society-—one for rescuing a girl at
St Kilda beach, another for attempting
something similar in the Brisbane River —
w 7 ere among the things he never referred to.
Mrs. La Ferme, a Russian lady wintering
here, is acting as auctioneer at various
charitable functions. Last Wednesday £3O
fell to her hammer for the Wounded Sol-
diers’ Fund, and to-night she auctioned
several gifts at the Empire in aid of the
Children’s Hospital. She operates in a very
pretty Russian peasant’s costume.
Gladys Brown, a cool young person aged
10, who recently made a hurried excursion
into the river and returned bearing her 10
year-old cousin and then completed the i 0 b
by restoring the nearly-drowned, had a gold
pendant and a silver medal handed her one
afternoon last week. The pendant was the
gift of the Mayoress, and the medal was
from the Kangaroo Point State School
Swimming Club, of which Gladys is
member. The little girl’s brave perform-
ance was undertaken in full dress, including
hat and lace-up boots.
The committee working for the sth Light
Horse Comforts Fund has started a kitchen
in the main street of this village—anyway
you can step into it from the main street’
Excellent cakes, straight from the oven, are
sold, and the money so raised is converted
into dainties for Billjim.
A widower-twice recently acquired a
bride who also had had previous experience
in the housekeeping business. In sending
out the invites, he added for tlie informa-
tion of a few pals: “Be sure and don’t
miss this; it will be no amateur perform-
ance.”
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
Six o’clock closing is coming in Banana-
land, too. Mrs. Harrison Lee-Cowie, who
was responsible—so her supporters lie— for
the downfall of Bung in N. S. Wales, has
arrived to lead the charge; and Acting-
Premier Theodore promises a referendum
as soon as the House meets.
Donald Cameron, of Brisbane, who died
last week, was one of Queensland’s hest-
known educationists in his day. Starting
off in the Isle of Skye 77 years ago, he came
from Edinburgh University to the big
MoConnel family, of Cressbrook. After a
spell at Brisbane Grammar, he took over
the Ipswich school —putting through Judge
O’Sullivan, J. W. Blair, and sundry other
notables —finally going out as inspector of
grammar schools.
Justice-Minister Fihelly dropped an un-
expected bomb last week when he announced
that seven police magistrates had been
“relieved.” Moore, Eglinton and Morris
(all of Brisbane), Burrowes (Longreach),
Parkinson (Maryborough), Jackson
(Mackay) and Walker (Goondiwindi) have
all passed the 05 limit fixed by the Public-
Service Act; and apparently they have all
been shown the door without regard to their
present judicial ability.
“Itan”: The Ryan Government is
threatening to give the sugar monopoly a
rough twist. Though the details are not
known, it is stated that the C.S.R. Co. and
the Government have been unable to agree
on the refining terms for this season’s
output of raw sugar from the State mills.
Apparently the Government is sticking to
its guns, for it is now discussing the ques-
tion of turning the produce of the mills into
unrefined sugar. This was the article used
in the early days of the industry when the
snjall mills had no refinery at hand. It is
a first-class substitute, and, what is im-
portant in these days of economy, can be
produced a penny per lb. cheaper than
the refined. The Government is said to
contemplate a State refinery, despite the
Sugar Commission’s report. Whatever is
done, the C.S.R. is apparently to receive a
bump.

An official report from the Northern Terri-
tory secretary of the Australia Day I und
states that the mere handful of people up
there have handed out £2670 in 11 months.
It is a land of big ideals and bigger hearts.
Died at Richmond (Tas.) lately, Mrs.
Mary Crawley, probably the oldest woman
on the Speck. She had allegedly lived 162
years. Not long ago one Daniels, also of
Richmond, weighed anchor at a reputed 105.
An active competitor in the Ouse district
named Mrs. Kitchen recently had a bit of
bad luck. She had done a brisk 99 laps
when she fell in the fire and was burned to
death.
An Australian middy, Joseph Mack, was
killed aboard the Defence in the recent North
Sea fight. His father was Gardner Mack,
of Berry Bank, in Western Vic.—the family
has hung together for many years at that old
spot. Young Mack had been in the Aus-
tralian Navy until passed along under an
arrangement existing for such educative
transfers, and was promoted to sub-
lieutenant shortly before the big battle.
Owing to the war, Dookie agricultural
college has lost most of its students for the
time being; but at Burnley school of horti-
culture, which until lately used to receive
woman-students twice a week for afternoon
classes only, it is now found that the girls’
demand for tuition justifies an all-day and
every-day supply. It looks as though Mira-
bel and * Maude were beginning to take a
really serious view of the situation —parti-
cularly of the situation as typist in a stuffy
office at 15 shillings a week.
Kitchener’s exit per medium of German
explosives recalls that he was the soldier
who rescued Charles Neufeld from Omdur-
man. Taken prisoner by the Dervishes
while travelling by caravan from Wady
Haifa to Kordofan, starvation and scourg-
ing were this German’s lot for 12 awful
years. He was wearing 401 b. legirons when
Kitchener released him, and with his own
hands struck off the fetters. One German, at
any rate if he is still alive, would have cause
for a pang of regret at hearing of the Hamp-
shire tragedy.
Sergeant T. W. McCrystal, one of the
most prominent recruiters in N. S. Wales,
was one of the most argumentative of its
revolutionary Socialists a year or two ago.
When he was a member of the Wharf Labor-
ers’ Union he once opposed, on straight-out
Sosh lines, the Labor candidate at Pyr-
mont (Sydney), and at that time trounced
those behind the uabor movement as mere
muddlers. Nothing has since happened to
justifv him in making his old opinion stand
on its head.
A poor-looking thing was carried back the
other day to Sydney—charred and stained
and pierced by two holes, one of them
plugged with a piece of lead and a fragment
of blanket. It was a Bulletin of February
10, and with its good Australian mates it
had got into a warm corner —into a billet
behind the Australian lines in France. Cor-
poral F. C. Poole, of a battery of Field
Artillery, in forwarding the relic to a friend
in Sydney, gives the history of its
wounds: “It was with my kit in a
house we were billeted in—beneath a
roll of blankets. The squareheads evi-
dently took a tumble to us and shelled
the place heavily. So we quit. When
I raked over the ashes to see if any of my
kit had escaped, all I could find was the
last family journal to reach me. It may
interest you to see how it had suffered in
the bombardment.”
“10-2-4”: It seems to me that many of
the people who yell for the internment of
“all enemy aliens” do protest too much.
They bay for the unattainable, and then use
their failure as a reason why they, or their
relatives, should not enlist. The most
dangerous spies in Australia are probably
Australians, just as in France they are
Frenchmen, and in England English. It
would be better business for Germany to
buy an Australian of unimpeachable stock
to do -its dirty work than to employ a
German, who would necessarily have to
operate beneath a continuous flood of lime-
light. Australia needs more reticence far
worse than it needs more internments.
Why should Germany trouble to employ
paid spies of any sort, when there are
numbers of prosperous citizens ready and
willing to do all that hired traitors could
accomplish for the mere love of yapping?
Will Lawson puts on his sea-boots:—
The Takaptina, the little flier that was built to
carry the ’Frisco mail over the Onehunga bar (the
engineers will tell you that one turn of her propeller
drives her along 17ft. from a standstill), has been
running to the Sounds and West Coast again. A
tarry sea-dog pointed his finger at her the other
day and said he: “That there boat’s a miracle on bar
harbors. Every roller, cornin’ up astern, takes your
craft on the quarter and cants her out of her
course. That gets some skippers rattled. But, wait
on, an’ the roller runs alongside, like a big, friendly
pup, and gives ’er nose a tap, which it straightens
her up again. You must take bar ’arbors easy. Go
slow is the ideer, with a big kick up yer sleeve.
Then, when the roller has trimmed ’er up, let ’er
’ave it! I rec’lect once a roller let us down with a
bang right on the bar. ‘Stan’ by, to give ’er a
’ell of a kick!’ the old man sings out to the chief.
There we sticks, tremblin’, while another roller
comes lollopin’ up astern. First it cants ’er in a
sickly way ; then, as it begins to straighten ’er up,
the old man rings for steam. She got it and the
lift of the sea together, and in we goes like a
bullet, with me ’anging on to my sou’wester in the
wind she made.”
“J.F.IL”: Kalgoorlie continues to be the
boldest of Australian mendicants here in
London. The annual meeting of the Kal-
goorlie Diocesan Association has just been
held. This is the British begging agency
established by Dr. Golding-Bird, the Church
of England bishop of the second-richest gold-
field in the wide Empire. In its first year
the Association cadged from Cockneys £760,
and this ought to secure three square meals
a day for the Gaiters of Central Westralia
for the next few months. But are the
Church of England folks in rich Kalgoorlie
so desperately hard up that they could not
raise this £760 among themselves and escape
the humiliation of taking it as a charitable
gift from commiserating Cockneys?

Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy.
Nature lias endowed vour body with sixteen million snots to
which an ache or pain can be attached. When each of these spots is

BIG BLN
Lei
«8
Has a Get Up all his Own
It’s the buoyant note
in his get up signal
that lifts you out of
sleep, glad to be
awake. It’s a get up
that makes you smile
an answer to Big Ben’s
morning greeting.
A wake up that tempts
a whistle as you dress —
that puts an edge on your
breakfast appetite—starts
you down stairs feeling
like a two year old.
At five, seven, or whatever
hour you set, Big Ben will wake
you and start you on your day.
He’ll rouse you gently with
ten half minute calls, or shake
off your sleep with a five minute
ring.
Big Ben is seven inches tall,
well built, handsome, punctual.
He has a big, easily read dial—a
cheerful, deep toned voice—large, com-
fortable winding keys—a light running
movement with watch-like escapement
—an alarm that calls steadily for five
minutes or intermittently for ten.
Big Ben undergoes a hundred inspec-
tions in the making and six rigid timing
tests before leaving the factory of the
Western Clock Co., La Salle, 111.,
U.S.A., Makers of Westclox. At alt
clock dealers, price 12/6; postage 1/-
extra.

The Inky Way.
“E.H.L.”: “Copy Boy” (B. l/6/’l6)
claims for the Herald and Weekly Times,
Melbourne, “the best array of newspaper
veterans in Australia.” Then he parades
them —16 employees with a record of over 30
years each. But that’s a mere kindergarten
class. At the Adelaide Register office
there are 26 hale-and-hearties with over 30
years to their credit and holding their own
quite easily with the recruits who are in
their 25th year of service. Quite a crowd
of youngsters have scored 21. Two have a
record of 60 years’ service, one 58, one 52,
one 47, two 42, five 41, one 39, one 38, one
37, two 36, two 35, two 34, two 33, three 32,
three 29, three 28, two 27, and three 26. The
employee who hasn’t been at the office 20
years or so is almost sure to be at the
Front: no less than 30 of them have enlisted.
Sydney Telegraph still manages to beat
the Censorship at times —as when it
announced the other morning that Persia
had joined in the great scrap and annexed
all the south-west of Russia. Apparently
the rumor that Xerxes is dead has been
exaggerated.
* * * *
From a S. M. Herald law report: —
Costs to be cots in the cause.
For the barristers and lawyers to sleep on
in their second childhood, no doubt.
Lieut. Syd. Addison, who hails from the
Huon, is the first man from the Speck to
pass the test for a pilot’s certificate at the
flying school in Victoria. He used to swing
the editorial “we” as editor of the Huon
Times ; and to one who has been in the
habit of perpetrating six fervid leaders a
week, lofty flights should come pretty easy.
“Pejay”: I disagree with “Black Rock”
(B. 1/6/T6) when he says the Argus is
the third-oldest paper in Victoria. The
Portland Guardian beats it easily for the
place. The first issue was printed by
Thomas Richardson at “Portland, District
of Port Phillip, New South Wales,” in 1842,
and the paper, therefore, commences its
75th year on August 20 next.. It is the
second-oldest rag in Victoria, . Geelong
Advertiser winning by a stick, with Mel-
bourne Herald third.
T. Dunbabin, an ex-lihodes scholar who
has during the last six years emptied many
inkpots on all manner of things, from leaders
up to advt. write-ups, in the service of
Hobart Mercury, has found life in the
Speck too strenuous, and is moving to a
job on the Melbourne Argus. In his leisure
moments he has imagined a good deal for the
Scientific American and other frivolous
productions.
A Bendigo paper, complimenting a local
official the other day, assured its readers:
“Mr. is the round pig in the round
hole.”
* * * *
Early days of the Melbourne Austral
Salon are recalled by mention of the death
in London of Mrs. Alexander, better known
in Australia as Molly Hirst Browne. She
was a rather well-known press-woman in
her day, and in conjunction with the late
Mrs. Charles Bright was in the genesis of
the best known of Melbourne women’s
clubs.
* * * *
Another great reform has been effected
by the war. Even the Age makes promise
of amendment. Referring in its issue of
13/6/T6 to the need for curtailing its
consumption of paper, it says: “This will not
involve the exclusion of news, but its pre-
sentation in a more compressed and more
readable form.” Let it be accounted to the
Kaiser for righteousness.
C. A. Pearson, talking of the recently-
deceased London Standard, tells of a curi-
ous practice he discovered when he took over
the paper. A porter (30s. a week) stood
at the door of the office and saw that no
deserving person went away without a “beer
ticket.” The drayman who brought a load
of paper, the boy who kept a visitor’s coat
from brushing against the wheel of a hansom
cab. the bearer of a letter —each received a
ticket entitling the holder to half a pint of
beer at any publichouse in the neighborhood.
The passports were redeemed on Saturday
mornings, and cost the hospitable old paper
about £BOO a yen’-. There is m unconfirmed
rumor that the Fairfaxes do the same thing
in Sydney.
SPEED AND SONG.
[FOR THE BULLETIN .]
\ irgil wrote five lilies of the iEneid daily, taking
the utmost pains to polish and perfect his verses.
Qive me a Roman toga, draped about me
In white and clinging folds to keep me proud ;
Let no dull modern voices come to flout me;
Build solid walls to shield me from the crowd.
Spread me white paper on a marble table;
Let some fair slave-girl hold my tuneful ink;
Smash down the telephone and cut the cable
And stand aloof from me and let me think !
W reathed with a chaplet of the choicest roses,
With music down the garden far away—
So far that silence round its cadence closes
And scarce I know the tune the harpers play—-
I should set all my happy fancies t moving-,
Beat out five lines of my triumphant ode
And slowly reap a golden harvest proving
The mettle of the Pegasus I rode.
But here, where motors honk and tramcars rattle
And half the world is mad with haste and fret,
The singer girds his soul to wildly battle
V hile all the discords of the sphere are met.
Tv eed for his robes, a telephone to tinkle
When finest frenzy moves his throbbing brain,
The clatter of the street to duly sprinkle
His chaste, white thought with its obscene refrain.
Speak not to me in high and lofty praises
Of those who wrote in fine aesthetic calm !
Hence! While the holy flame within me blazes
To light the haggard darkness with a psalm!
Bring your old singers here and let us try them
Amid the rush and hurry of the time—
Our modern cooks of song would simply fry them
And dish them brown in phantasy and rhyme.
N.S.W.
MARY McCOMMONWEALTH.

own children, but are denied the privilege
of doing so, because they have not suffi-
cient nourishment for them. The “Allenburys”
scientific system of Infant Feeding has provided
for all this. When a mother is partly able to
feed her'baby she may give The "Allenburys" Milk
Food No. I for alternate /feeds, so alike are the
natural and prepared food in this instance, and this
food should be continued until the weaning process
has been entirely completed. The mother herself
must be well nourished and to this end may partake
freely of the "Allenburys" DIET a partially pre-
digested milk and wheaten food, which like the '
Milk Food No. 1, is instantly made ready for use
by simply adding boiling ivater , only. After
childbirth the “Allenburys" DIET is surprisingly
helpful in maintaining the strength and producing
a good flow of rich milk.

[FOR THE BULLETIN .]
SO I who wooed her watched her timidly
Stoop low beside the waters, with
’ her cool,
White hand poised like a lily o’er the pool.
Blue eyes were in that clear tranquillity,
And two white-cloud breasts. How I grieved
to see
Her grief was paramount. She could not
school
Fair Reason. Avho is always Passion's fool;
So her tears fell, and she was lost to me.
Then I cried to her: “June!” The very
word
Found echo in the sorrow of the skies.
A wondrous wind came storming from the
South,
And every clamorous pulse of Winter stirred.
1 could not see, the rain was in mine eyes;
Nor cry again, a green bough lashed my
mouth.

BOYCE BOWDEN.
The Frightfulness of the C.L.S.C.
[FOR THE BULLETIN .]
SYDNEY was in the grip of one of its
recurrent burglary epidemics. Even
the oldest inhabitant/ admitted that
he had known nothing like it. As always,
the police were helpless. They nailed a
stray offender here and there, but he usu-
ally turned out to be some pettifogging
amateur who represented little real danger
to either life or property. It was known
that the city and suburbs were being
methodically worked by a sort of burglari-
ous Camorra, if not several of them, yet
in four months not one of the gang, or
gangs, was taken. The police w r ere not to
blame. The fact was the supply of burg-
lars vastly exceeded the force available to
cope with it. Some of the richest suburbs
ran to no more than one policeman to
5000 residents. Given bad seasons and tight
money, and burglary became one of the
obvious professions.
The most audacious things were done.
A Puisne Judge and his wife were
awakened one night by the switching on of
the electric light in their bedroom. Before
them stood two men heavily masked. One
held a levelled revolver. “It’s no use
kicking, Judge,” he said. “We’re after your
wife’s jewels and we mean to have them.
It may save trouble if you say at once
where they are. Make a noise and
I blow your brains out.” The Judg<* was
as courageous as the next man, but he
appreciated his limitations. Urged on by
his terrified wife he told where the jewel-
lery was, and a few minutes later the click
of gears being pushed home and the faint
suspiration of a high-powered motor-car in
action indicated that the thieves were off.
The Pentarvons, who own half Bond-
ahra, were famous for their gold plate;
and also for their habit of leaving it in an
ordinary sideboard between the ending of
one of their big parties and the next morn-
ing, when it was sent back to the bank.
Sydney was thrilled, in due course, to learn
that, while one masked scoundrel had held
up the Pentarvon household from the top
of the main stairway, another had stolidly
piled the huge gold dishes and bowls into a
sack, flung them brutally into a Rolls-
Royce touring-car and then returned to
cover his friend’s retreat upon the hall.
The insurance companies put up their rates
against burglary to an impossible figure. A
panic set in. The community agreed furi-
ously that the situation was intolerable.
The usual people wrote to the papers, pas-
sionately, ungrammatically, to say so. The
leader-writers ascribed it variously to
Socialism and the unrest caused by the
propaganda of the Wade party. In the
meantime no one put forward a practical
scheme to cope with the calamity.
* * * *
Jimmy Fulton, son of old Jim Fulton,
the bank-manager, was commonly regarded
as a fool. His health was bad, and people
have a way of confusing poor health and
poor mentality. .. Furthermore, having in-
herited £BOO a year from his mother, he
declined to engage in regular work. He
interested himself in travel, in crime, and
in the task of adding to his little fortune.
The few who had had business dealings
with him did not subscribe to the popular
opinion of his intelligence.
He was dining with his father on the
night of the Pentarvon robbery. When the
coffee had been brought in and the servants
were gone Jimmv leaned across the table
and said: “I’ve got a scheme to stop this
burgling nuisance and I want you to help
“Tell me about it?’ said the older man.
“Of course I’ll do what I can in reason.
“You’ll agree,” began Jimmy, that our
present system of burglary-prevention is
rotten. There aren’t nearly enough police-
men. The people with the most tempting
portable goods are often the most crimin-
ally careless. Hardly one big house in 2U
Ims a burglar-alarm installed. Not that A
blame'the owners for that. Who wants to
find a burglar, anyhow? Personally I d as
soon stumble over a tiger. To disturb a
burglar in his operations is, to my mind,
simply asking for serious injury, if not
death. What the average peaceful, non-
athletic housefather wants is to have his
burglar taken for him. Now look at that.
Fulton, the elder, bent his head over a
line drawing of what appeared to be a
packing-case. . ,
“What the devil is it." ’ he asked.
“A burglar-trap. My own invention, and,
like all great inventions, childishly 1 ?-
I got the germ of the iflea from the Oubli-
ette at Chillon. Let me explain. The body
(marked A) is of iron. The clamps (L C
D F) are to fix the body to the underneath
part of a staircase. G and II are carpet-
covered flaps which work on the principle
of the ordinary stage trap-door. If you
look up the newspaper files of the past four
months you will find that over 90 per cent,
of our burglaries were committed by men,
working in pairs, who went upstairs. These
flaps are so designed that they work, when
set, only in the case of persons coming
downstairs. My theory is that if one of
two malefactors saw his companion dis-
appear through the stairs ahead of him he
would stay in the hall and so be lost to
Justice. More than that, he would give the
show away to his friends. With both of
them on the return journey, and the house
aroused, the second man would be bound
to take a chance, jump the trap that had
engulfed his companion, and land on the
one below it.”
“I rather fancy your notion,” said the
older man. “Where would you get your
traps?”
“Fleet Bros, have made 5000 for me and
are prepared to deliver a second 5000 at a
month’s notice.”
“And what is it you want me to do?”
“Put my plan before a dozen leading
bankers—they would listen to you where
they wouldn’t have me in their offices—per-
suade them to call a meeting of their chief
clients to consider ways and means of deal-
ing with the burglary scandal, and arrange
for me to address the gathering.”
And so the Citizens’ League for the Sup-
pression of Crime came into being. Inci-
dentally, Jimmy cleared £lO,OOO on his first
lot of traps.
* * * *
The initial success of the device was sen-
sational. For the first few weeks burglars
fairly poured through the staircases of
the rich. The war lost its place as a topic
of chief interest. The first breathless ques-
tion flung at the bearer of the morning tea
invariably was: “Any more arrived?”
During breakfast nothing ' was seriously
discussed but the appearance, kit. weapons,
etc., of the unhappy beings, sitting blas-
phemously in their prisons beneath the
stairs. Sympathetic folks, mostly females,
began to take a personal interest in their
burglars. They provided mattresses for them
to fall on, and they left food, drink, cigar-
ettes, chocolates and other little luxuries
in the corners of their cages. An organisa-
tion on the lines of the Red Cross: was
started. Arrangements were made to visit
the burglar’s family, and see that it did
not want; to provide the members with
paper, envelopes and pens wherewith to
write to the burglar during his incarcera-
tion ; to employ counsel to defend him, and
so on. The social life of the community
threatened to become revolutionised. A
special corps of police had to be told off
to collect the matutinal catch. The busi-
ness of the Criminal Courts became con-
gested. A Judge collapsed from overwork.
And then, suddenly, the bottom fell through
the whole business. Gossip and the press
had done their work. The burgling profes-
sion, grown wise to the situation, took to
avoiding staircases ' back and front, as
though they were infernal machines.
“I saw it coming a fortnight ago,” said
Jimmy to his father on the day the papers
noted the first serious diminution in the
morning haul. “I shall call the C.L.S.C
together for next Wednesday.”
To the society, which was now in a mood
to hang on his every word, Jimmy spoke
as follows: —-
“Ladies and Gentlemen, I have gloomy,
and at the same time heartening news for
you. Our burglar-trap is played out. In
the whole of the city and suburbs last night
only two burglars were taken by it. Both
are from the hack country. One is sus-
pected of being half-witted. By to-morrow
I venture to say the tally will be nil, and
so on for many a long dav to come. Carpers
may, no doubt will, say that the device
which so many of you have installed is now
useless; they may even go so far as to
argue that it represents a foolish invest-
ment. As against that consider its record.
In little more than "a month it has been
instrumental in capturing 306 burglars,
among them Flash Harry Bligsome, Her-
vey Nokker, and other masters of their call-
ing. It has given us all, if I may say so,
a good deal of innocent fun. And the traps
still stand as so many insurances against
the violation of at least the upper parts
of our homes. I venture to say that at
£l9 13s. 4d. per trap you have had a fair
return for your money. (Loud and pro-
longed cheering.)
“Unfortunately the curse which we are
banded together to exterminate is still with
us. Nine robberies were reported, last
night. But, ladies and gentlemen, every
one of them was a downstairs affair. I
have here a sample of a drug, made from
a rare South American leaf, that was shown
to me by a member of a native firm when
I was in Lima last year. A consignment
of the leaves, which I cabled for some time
ago, has just arrived. The action of the
ordinary sleeping draught is, as you pro-
bably know, slow. The peculiarity of
Punta-Varella, my drug, is that it fells
whoever takes it like a pole-axe, renders
him helpless for 24 hours, and at the end
of that time leaves him, if not exactly
comfortable, at least in good health.
“What I would suggest is that each of
you take over a supply of Punta-Varella
from me —I must warn you that it is expen-
sive—and use it as you think best. A
decanter of port, or a few of those beer
bottles which have a clip instead of a cork,
if left carelessly about, would prove a
deadly lure to most criminals. Doctor the
liquor with the drug and you will get your
man every time.”
• * * *
For a while it was as though the halcyon
days of the past had come back to the
houses of the well-to-do. Every morning
there was a dishevelled rush to the dining-
room to see what manner of game the
alcohol had brought down. The young men
of the family took a virile pride in drag-
ging the defeated miscreant into the wash-
house. The ladies examined the grim im-
"plements of his trade with delightful little
shrieks of dismay. There was the excite-
ment of ringing up friends to let them

The River.
[FOR THE BULLETIN .]
ON, on! Faster, faster! Feverishly
he spurred bis tired hack. His
pack-horse Avas a lather of sweat
and blowing badly, but he must push on
—mile after mile through the rich
bloodAVOod, apple and ash flats, over the
stony, ironbark ridges, past lagoons cov-
ered from sight by Avaving masses of pink
and purple Avater-lilies. Wild duck, plain
turkeys, kangaroos, wallabies and an occa-
sional mob of Avild pigs—past them all un-
heeding. No time for shooting, this; and,
besides, he’d had enough of shooting.
Shooting! Well, Avhy shouldn’t he shoot?
And what if it was a steer? You couldn’t
expect a man to buy beef all the way from
Rockhampton to the Towers. It was these
Havs, capitalistic laws —they’d give a man
liA'e years for the sake ol a bit of beef. But
if they reckoned he Avas going to take it
lying dOAvn —well, they’d find out their mis-
take.
Anyway, what did that stockman want to
come nosing around for? Any darned fool
ought to know better than that. Of course,
he had to shoot; what else Avas he to do?
It Avas just his rotten luck that the shot
didn’t do the bloke in, and he had to finish it
Avitli his butclier’s-knife. It seemed ghastly—
near murder. Borne chaps might call it mur-
der. Yes. he Avislied iioav the shot had
finished it. Or lie might have risked
another, and kept liis hands clean. How
sticky it Avas! But, then, how was he to
know that a party of musterers mightn’t be
somewhere handy? And. God knoAA'S! one
Avail enough.
The river ought not to be far away now.
Once across that he could breathe easier.
V os, he would camp for an hour or two the
other side, and by to morrow night he could
be half-way up the range. Once on top of
the range lie could' fossick round for a bit
of tin and keep out of sight for a month or
two.
Didn’t knoAV the river was such a long
Avay. It would be dark in another ten
minutes. And his paekhorse was stumbling.
It Avas a bit of luck having the full moon.
A good job lie knew the river pretty well,
too. Quicksands were bad in places. But
up at the rocks there Avas a good stony
bottom right across. And, anyway, with
only those few bits of showers there would
be only a narrow stream running under the
far bank.
What Avas that? Bounded like running
Avater ! Yes, thank God ! Here Avas the
river at last!
But Avhat a row it was making! His
nerves must be going crook on him. He
Avould be able to see the sand, a Avide stretch
of sand, directly.
Yes, there it Avas! Baud! My God!
Water! The river a hanker! A mile and a
quarter of solid, broAvn, creamy, foaming
water!
What a fool he’d been! But how was he
to know? Why, they must have had a foot
of rain higher up.
How cold and cruel and dark they looked,
these flood waters in the moonlight! Bah—
i * :-ves again ! Could he, dare he attempt
the crossing? No question of could—he
must.
Of course, he could. Why, only the last
flood they took a mob of fats across....
Wasn’t that the time,' though, one of the
drovers.... But a ’gator got him, they said.
And they didn’t cross at night, either; and
’ gators —didn’t they reckon ’gators Avere
Avorse at night?
Well, if they were, it Avas no use thinking
about it. It had to be faced. He could
ride in front and lead his paekhorse till it
got to a swim, and then he could let the hack
swim ahead and hold on to his tail. The
paekhorse? Well, the paekhorse would
never do it. But so much the better. If
they found him dead they might think the
Avliole lot had been drowned and not bother
tracking any further.
Noav, then, neck or nothing. It wouldn’t
be very deep for a good Avay yet... .Cold !—-
Supposing he got the cramps!
Those nerves!
It was getting deeper—he’d have to let the
paekhorse go. How the poor devil grunted
—pity to see a good horse drown. But. ...
lucky enough if he made the other side him-
self. „
His saddle horse was a good swimmer . . . .
no, he must get off to lighten the weight.
Ugh ! It was cold. What a trip ! What a
SAvim ! And at night!—yes, they said they
Avere worse at night.
What was that! What a row a horse
made swimming! And ’gators could hear a
minnow splashing!
Supposing a ’gator was following them up.
Watching him. . . .with jaAvs like a great foul
trap
Supposing—huh, no, only a dead stick
poking up out of the water.
It teas coming nearer! My God!
**. * *
And a shriek Avas cut in two!
II.
The Closed Door.
[FOR THE BULLETIN .]
I.
BROWN was the cloak o’er his shoulder
thrown,
Brown were his buskins high,
And he held in his fingers a rose half blown,
And the knights as they rode by
Whistled their goshawks and laughed, and
swore
That he stayed for aye by a fast-closed door.
But some day he knew, as a child believes
In a Christian God who is always kind,
A latch would rustle the clinging leaves,
And a hinge would creak in the door
behind,
And the dust of ages would- break and rise
As incense under his eager eyes;
That a tress of hair would be outward blown,
He would hear the drag of a silken gown,
And there where he waited all alone
The cruel barrier would hurtle down,
And the oaken panel swing, to show
A jewelled throat or an arm of snow.
Perchance it would come on some moonlit
night,
When the hills were purple along the sea,
And the moat was guarded by mist-ghosts
white,
And the stars were a golden argosy,
He would hark to heralds of happier fate
In a handle’s turn, or a hinge’s grate.
Or belike an eve when the wild grey rain
Washed mosses out of the castle wall,
Drove like robber hordes on the beaten plain
And dripped despair from the poplars tall,
Through the moaning wind and the dead
leaves’ drift
A light would flicker, a wet latch lift.
Or a summer’s end that was dark and warm.
No moon galleon in breathless skies,
Hills low and sullen Avith threat of storm,
And by the shingle a seamew’s cries,
Where the pale moths circled in languorous
play
A hand might hover, more white than they!
ii.
’Twas a night of roses, sick-sweet with love,
A night of passion and tender dew,
When the thrushes dreaming in boughs abOA r e
Heard a mailed footfall breaking through,
As he trampled the bluebell in the glade
And shattered the laurels’ pavisade.
Did he see, I wonder, a brown-cloaked form
Couched in the grass by the fast-shut door?
Oh! his was a brow of force and storm.
And he stepped like a king on the marbled
floor —
And a greedy night moth, poised to sup,
Blew back, unslaked, from its honey cup !
And the ivy, slmpen like outspread hands,
Stirred too, and swung from the old grey
wall.
And the ficus loosed from the latch its bands,
Erect, as though to a bugle call.
While little hare feet on the inner floor
Came like falling petals towards the door!
111.
Butterflies, born with the limpid day,
Yellow things, as a shred of sun,
On a lily’s bosom they swooned away,
For the wanton lily lo\ r ed every one !
And stiff-rosetted, like ladies’ frocks,
Shone the gaudy pinks of the hollyhocks!
Trailing ivy athwart the ledge,
One spray severed as if by steel,
Sharp and clean on the dusty edge.
Deep imprint of a mailed heel—
Doors fast barred and windows black,
But the foot that ventured had not com-
hack.
iv.
Brown was the cloak o’er his shoulders
thrown,
Brown were his buskins high,
And he held in his fingers a rose full-blown,
And he watched the world go by,
A-tid the world was a death-march , strange
and grim,
For the door had opened—hut not for him.
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
